aDepartment of Chemical and Biological Engineering, University at Buffalo, The State University of New York, Buffalo, NY 14260, USA. E-mail: swihart@buffalo.edu
bRENEW Institute, University at Buffalo, The State University of New York, Buffalo, NY 14260, USA
Received 
      16th February 2021
    First published on 11th May 2021AbstractThe synthesis of nanomaterials, with characteristic dimensions of 1 to 100 nm, is a key component of nanotechnology. Vapor-phase synthesis of nanomaterials has numerous advantages such as high product purity, high-throughput continuous operation, and scalability that have made it the dominant approach for the commercial synthesis of nanomaterials. At the same time, this class of methods has great potential for expanded use in research and development. Here, we present a broad review of progress in vapor-phase nanomaterial synthesis. We describe physically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods including inert gas condensation, spark discharge generation, and pulsed laser ablation; plasma processing methods including thermal- and non-thermal plasma processing; and chemically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods including chemical vapor condensation, flame-based aerosol synthesis, spray pyrolysis, and laser pyrolysis. In addition, we summarize the nanomaterials produced by each method, along with representative applications, and describe the synthesis of the most important materials produced by each method in greater detail.
Mohammad Malekzadeh
Mohammad Malekzadeh is a PhD Candidate under the supervision of Professor Mark T. Swihart in the Chemical and Biological Engineering Department, University at Buffalo (SUNY) since 2016. He earned his Master's degree from Sharif University of Technology, Iran and his Bachelor's degree from Persian Gulf University, Iran. His research mainly focuses on the design and synthesis of nanomaterials using vapor-phase (aerosol) techniques.
Mark T. Swihart
Mark Swihart is UB Distinguished Professor and Chair of Chemical and Biological Engineeirng and Empire Innovation Professor in the RENEW Institute at the University at Buffalo (SUNY). His research focuses on development and application of both aerosol (vapor-phase) and colloidal (solution-phase) methods of nanomaterial synthesis, and on diverse applications of nanomaterials in health, energy, and the environment.
1. Introduction
Nanomaterials, as the building blocks of nanotechnology, have unique properties that can be used in numerous applications.1 Superparamagnetism,2–6 size-dependent photoluminescence,7–9 high specific surface area and surface curvature,10–13 nanoscale texturing,14–17 and reduced scattering of light,18–20 are just a few of many unique and interesting properties of nanomaterials. These properties, and others, make specific nanomaterials promising candidates for existing and emerging applications in biomedical,2,3,19 mechanical,10,14–16 bioseparation,4,11 optoelectronic,6 thermoelectric,5 bioimaging,18,20 catalytic,11,12 microelectronic,17 environmental,9,13 photocatalytic,8,13 and optical7,9 applications, in which they can often outperform the corresponding bulk materials. Nano-organization control through the development and application of nanosynthesis techniques, which is one of the main foci of this review, plays a substantial role in these applications, due to the size and morphology dependence of key properties of nanomaterials.21
One can categorize methods of the nanomaterial synthesis in many ways, but the most general is based on the state of the precursors from which the NPs are formed: top-down and bottom-up. Top-down methods include ball milling, electrochemical etching, photolithographic patterning, and other approaches that break or pattern larger materials to produce nanomaterials.22 In contrast, bottom-up methods start from atomic or molecular precursors.23 Two general subcategories can be considered for the bottom-up methods based on the phase in which nanomaterial synthesis occurs, namely solution-phase and vapor-phase. Particle formation in solution-phase methods is initiated by heating, by addition of a second precursor compound, or by addition of a reducing agent or other reactive species. Generally, in these methods, the precursor or mixture of precursors, which can be liquid or solid, reacts in liquid solution leading to nucleation and growth of solid particles. These primary particles can grow via surface reaction, and may aggregate. Many kinds of nanomaterials, especially metal nanocrystals and quantum dots are synthesized by solution-phase methods. Surfactants or ligands, molecules that bind to the nanoparticle (NP) surface, are used to control the growth of particles and limit the particle size. The most important solution-phase methods are sol–gel, hydrothermal, solvothermal, thermal decomposition, and micelle/reverse-micelle methods.24 In vapor-phase methods, precursors must be delivered as a vapor or as small droplets or particles that can be vaporized within the process. An energy source is generally required to initiate and sustain physical evaporation and/or chemical reaction in the vapor phase.25 The vapor phase should be supersaturated to drive the formation of a solid phase. At a sufficient level of supersaturation, nucleation occurs homogeneously. Often, this supersaturation is in the form of vapor-phase atoms or molecules present at a partial pressure above their vapor pressure. However, it can also take the form of “chemical” supersaturation, in which vapor-phase molecules are thermodynamically unstable relative to a solid that can form from them by chemical reaction. In any case, once particles have nucleated, the system should be rapidly quenched to stop particle growth and sintering. A wide range of materials can be synthesized by these methods, but non-toxic semiconductors and metal oxides have been particularly important. Vapor-phase methods can operate at much higher temperature and shorter reaction times than solution-phase methods, which allows crystallization of high melting point materials.26,27 However, solution-phase synthesis can directly produce a stable colloidal dispersion of the NPs, in which aggregation of particles does not occur. Ligands and surfactants can be used to control the size and shape of nanomaterials to a degree that is not generally possible in vapor-phase processes. Aerosols, dispersions of the product particles in a vapor, are inherently unstable, and primary particles invariably aggregate into larger clusters of particles. When this happens after quenching, it produces “soft” aggregates that can subsequently be broken up. However, when the particles become partially sintered into “hard” aggregates, the clustering is irreversible.28 Such aggregates may be desirable or undesirable depending upon the ultimate application. Table 1 compares key features of solution-phase and vapor-phase NP synthesis methods to concisely summarize the relative advantages and disadvantages of these broad categories of nanosynthesis. For those who may be less familiar with the terminology of vapor-phase synthesis and aerosol science and technology in general, a list of definitions of key terms is provided at the end of this review.

The main focus of this review is on vapor-phase processes for the synthesis of free-standing nanomaterials. Several rather comprehensive reviews of this field were published 15 to 25 years ago.27,29,30 More recent reviews have focused on particular subsets of vapor-phase synthesis methods or particular applications.31–38 Thus, a new review that takes a broad and relatively comprehensive approach, as presented here, is needed. That being said, there are some areas outside the scope of this review. The deposition of nanomaterials into films and growth of nanostructured films from the vapor phase is a very broad topic which we do not include, as is the literature on soot formation in flames, which we only touch on briefly. These and other aspects that we chose not to include are noted in relevant sections below.
To provide structure to this review, we categorize vapor-phase synthesis techniques into three classes of physically-based, plasma-processing-based, and chemically-based methods based on the similarity of chemical structures of the precursors to the final products. While this classification is only one of several possibilities, and there is some inherent overlap between categories, we believe it provides a valuable framework for organizing, comparing, and contrasting methods. Within this framework, we survey the history and recent advances in each method with a focus on the nanosynthesis technique and the pros and cons of each process. After initial introduction of each method, we consider the most important nanomaterials synthesized by each method in greater detail, to illustrate how to the method can be employed to drive interesting applications.
2. Vapor-phase production methods
Interest in vapor-phase (aerosol) nanomaterial synthesis is driven by several key advantages relative to solution-phase methods. The unavoidable impurities and liquid (solvent) wastes generated by solution-phase methods as well as the use of relatively expensive surfactants or ligands can be avoided in most vapor-phase methods. Production of nanomaterials without organic ligands on their surface can be particularly important for electronic applications. Complex structures such as high-temperature superconductors can be produced in the vapor phase, leveraging their ability to produce nanomaterials with complex compositions and crystal structure at high temperature. Vapor-phase methods are continuous, while solution-phase methods are usually conducted batch-wise. Continuous operation may enable improved control and reproducibility as well as productivity and scalability.29,39 There are various ways to categorize aerosol synthesis methods based on the energy source or based on the state of the precursors, but here we first classify the methods based on the similarity of chemical structure between the precursors and the product nanomaterials, into three classes of physically-based vapor-phase synthesis, plasma processing, and chemically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods. For example, if we produce silicon NPs from silicon wafers, that is a physically-based process, but if we use silane as the precursor, the method will be classified as chemically-based. Plasma processing methods can employ both types of precursors. Overlap among these categories is inevitable, but such classification is nonetheless useful.
2.1. Physically-based vapor-phase production methods
Physically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods are aerosol synthesis methods in which the product materials are of essentially the same composition as the precursors, or perhaps a simple compound (e.g. oxide) based on the precursors. In general, the main advantage of these methods is the synthesis of nanomaterials with a high degree of purity and the major disadvantage is the synthesis of small quantities of material per batch or per unit time. The most important methods that can be considered in this category are inert gas condensation (using conventional thermal evaporation, e-beam evaporation, or sputtering as the evaporation technique), spark discharge generation, and pulsed laser ablation.

2.1.1. Inert gas condensation. 
To achieve supersaturation in this technique, an inorganic material is heated to a temperature at which it will sublime or evaporate into an inert gas atmosphere at reduced pressure, typically 1–50 mbar. Mixing of this vapor with cooler gas induces nucleation by condensation. This method is suitable for producing metal nanomaterials because of their evaporation at acceptable rates at practical temperatures. However, outgassing due to undesirable heating of other reactor parts is a major disadvantage of the method. To produce oxides or non-oxide compounds, a reactive gas like O2 can be added to the cold inert gas. The type of inert gas, the gas pressure, and the vapor pressure of the evaporating materials can influence the particle size distribution, which critically depends on the residence time of the particles in the growth region of the process.29,30Table 2 provides a summary and Fig. 1 presents representative transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of materials synthesized using this approach.

2.1.1.1. Conventional inert gas condensation. 
2.1.1.1.1. Metals. 
Granqvist and Buhrman first developed the inert gas condensation (IGC) technique, using a temperature-controlled source in a low-pressure chamber to synthesize ultrafine metal NPs. They prepared oxide-coated Al NPs with average diameters from 3 to 6 nm, as well as Mg, Zn, and Sn NPs. A schematic of their process is shown in Fig. 2. They employed a chamber that was initially under ultra-high vacuum (∼2 × 10−6 Torr). They placed an alumina crucible in a stand-off position and heated it radiatively with a graphite element and a temperature controller with a maximum power of 20 kW. They introduced UHP Ar as the inert gas at a pressure of 0.5 to 4 Torr. In addition, they used a rotary water-cooled plate to collect the product NPs. By introducing oxygen during evaporation of Al, they grew Al2O3 as an insulating coating around the Al NPs.40
 Fig. 2  Schematic of inert gas evaporation set-up for the synthesis of ultrafine metal NPs using Granqvist and Buhrman strategy. Adapted from ref. 40 with permission from AIP Publishing, Copyright 1976. 
Birringer and Gleiter were also pioneers of the IGC method. Their work, like much early work on IGC, focused on producing bulk samples of nanocrystalline metals with improved or novel mechanical properties, e.g., deformation behaviour, compared to conventional metals. In one important study, they employed IGC to synthesize Fe NPs. To do so, they used helium as the inert gas at a pressure as low as 2 kPa and a tungsten heater to produce what they called a “gas-like solid” of Fe NPs with average diameter of about 6 nm.41 Nieman et al. used the same strategy to synthesize Cu and Pd NPs with average diameters below 10 nm, which they pressed into dense nanocrystalline samples. They observed four and two times increase in microhardness of nanocrystalline Pd and Cu pellets, respectively, compared to coarse-grained samples.42,43 In addition, they studied the tensile strength and creep properties and found the yield strength of nanocrystalline-grained materials increased substantially compared to the coarse-grained counterparts. They found no evidence of room temperature diffusional creep for the nanocrystalline material.43,44
Weertman's group developed a new IGC system to improve the powder purity and increase the density of the final nanocrystalline metal samples.45 A schematic is presented in Fig. 3. They decreased the system base pressure and made other engineering improvements that allowed them to reload the boat without opening up the chamber. Their efforts enabled synthesis of Pd NPs with hydrogen level and Cu NPs with oxygen level of less than 5 at%. These were compacted into solids that reached 98% of the bulk density and exhibited improved elastic and tensile behaviour.45,46
 Fig. 3  Schematic of the Weertman's group's novel IGC set-up with: (A) Resistively-heated evaporation source, (B) rotary liquid-N2 cold-finger collector, (C) scraper driver, (D) scraper, (E) large, viton-sealed flange, (F) boat reloader, (G) upper (moving) piston, (H) die (with heating capability), (I) sample remover, and (J) port to electron beam n-powder preparation system. Adapted from ref. 45 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1997. 
Rösner et al. worked on the IGC synthesis of Pd NPs, particularly focusing on the mechanical properties of the compacted nanocrystalline metals produced from them, and the mechanisms underlying these improved mechanical properties. They found extensive deformation twinning on (1 1 1) planes of the nanocrystalline materials.47 In addition, they investigated deformation mechanisms at large strain in the nanocrystalline Pd samples produced by compaction and found that for grains of less than 40 nm in characteristic dimensions, the deformation was controlled by a grain boundary-meditated process through twinning, dislocation slip, and grain boundary diffusion while it was controlled by a dislocation slip mechanism for grains of larger size.48
IGC-synthesized metal NPs have also been studied for their antibacterial properties. Baker et al. investigated the antibacterial properties of silver NPs synthesized by IGC and demonstrated antibacterial effects at low concentrations that were related to the total surface area of the NPs. They concluded that NPs with higher surface to volume ratio are more effective for antibacterial applications.49 Raffi et al. studied the antibacterial behaviour of Cu NPs with an average size of 12 nm synthesized by IGC and reached a similar conclusion.50
2.1.1.1.2. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Löffler et al. investigated the magnetic properties of IGC-synthesized Fe and Ni NPs and found that the magnetic properties of the NPs were affected by the grain size.65 Signorini et al. also worked on the IGC synthesis of magnetic NPs. To produce Fe/FeyOx core–shell NPs, they evaporated 99.98% pure Fe as the target in a UHP He atmosphere in a chamber with a base pressure of 10−5 Pa. After synthesis, they gradually introduced O2 to passivate the particle surfaces, reaching a pressure of 133 Pa after about 1 hour. Finally, they exposed the NPs for 24 hours to an atmosphere of 1.2 kPa of He. The oxidation process created an iron oxide shell. They controlled the average size of the NPs by adjusting the evaporator heating current to change the evaporation rate. Accordingly, they produced core–shell NPs with core average sizes from 7 to 21 nm, with oxide shells of 2–3 nm thickness. The shell comprised two phases: magnetite (Fe3O4) and maghemite (Fe2O3). Upon decreasing the dimensions of the core, maghemite became the dominant phase in the shell, as shown in Fig. 4.68 Baker et al. investigated the magnetic behaviour of Fe/Fe-oxide core/shell NPs synthesized by IGC and found an increase in coercivity with increased oxide thickness, both for NPs dispersed in a polymer and for NPs compressed into a dense pellet.67
 Fig. 4  Schematic structure of Fe/FeyOx core–shell NPs produced by Signorini et al. Reproduced from ref. 68 with permission from American Physical Society, Copyright 2003. 
Friedrichs et al. synthesized Mg NPs by IGC, with an average size of 40 nm and a 3–4 nm thick layer of MgO that helped to prevent further oxidation. For this purpose, they used resistive heating of Mg foil to 973 K in 3 Torr He in a chamber with a base pressure of 10−8 Torr.51 Calizzi et al. produced Mg–Ti NPs buy IGC with in situ hydrogenation at 150 °C and investigated their hydrogen storage activities. They evaporated 99.9% Mg ingots and 99.9% Ti powder in separate Joule-heated tungsten boats in a chamber with a base pressure of 10−7 mbar under a He flow rate of 7.0 sccm and pressure of 2.7 mbar. The He flow passed the hotter Ti source after the Mg source and finally reached the liquid N2-cooled rotating collection cylinder. Subsequently, the products were exposed to H2 in Ar for about 1 hour at a H2 partial pressure of 133 mbar and 150 °C to hydrogenate both Mg and Ti. They found that Mg–Ti NPs exhibited better hydrogen storage performance than MgH2 using Nb2O as a catalyst.57
Singh et al. produced Tb75Fe25 NPs by IGC in the apparatus presented in Fig. 5. First, they used arc melting of a mixture of Tb and Fe in an argon atmosphere to prepare an ingot of Tb75Fe25 alloy. Thereafter, they employed a resistively heated tungsten crucible in an IGC chamber under 5 mbar helium. They cold-pressed amorphous NPs to obtain nanoglass samples with improved magnetic, mechanical, and structural properties relative to the bulk alloy.103,104
 Fig. 5  Schematic of set-up for production of Tb75Fe25 NPs by IGC. Adapted from ref. 104 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2020. 
Wang et al. employed a laser evaporation source to synthesize a CoCrFeNiMn nanocrystalline high-entropy alloy though what they termed an IGC technique, but could also be considered a laser ablation approach. The laser source was a 50 W Yb:YAG picosecond pulsed laser at 1064 nm and 500 kHz. They first cast a Co20Cr20Fe20Ni20Mn20 target by arc melting a mixture of the pure metals and drop casting into a water-cooled copper mold. The laser beam evaporated the target in 500 Pa helium to form nanocrystals of ∼25 nm mean diameter. They employed two units for compaction: a low-pressure unit applied a pressure of ∼500 MPa to make 10 mm diameter pellets, followed by a high-pressure unit that applied 5 GPa for 3 min at ambient temperature. The high vapor pressure of manganese led to it condensing after other materials, and produced a final composition of (CoCrFeNi)93.5Mn6.5. They found that the nanocrystalline material exhibited ultrahigh hardness with exceptional thermal stability; the hardness increased incredibly from 484 HV to 791 HV after 1 hour annealing at 600 °C and this high hardness was retained after heating to 1100 °C.73
2.1.1.1.3. Ceramic oxides. 
Production of ceramic oxide NPs and nanocrystalline bulk ceramics was also an important driver for development of the IGC method. In particular, nanocrystalline ceramics were expected to be less brittle than conventional ceramics, overcoming one of the major limitations of ceramic materials. Skandan used IGC to produce non-agglomerated ZrO2 NPs with average sizes from 4 to 14 nm. Skandan used UHP He as the inert gas at a pressure of 2 to 20 mbar and evaporated ZrO as the source, and then oxidized the NPs after deposition. Using monoxide as the source was preferable to using metal because oxidation of the monoxide to the oxide is much less exothermic than oxidation of the metal, allowing better control of the post-synthesis oxidation process.118 Guillou et al. synthesized nearly monodispersed CeO2 NPs of 3 to 3.5 nm diameter. They employed an ultra-high vacuum chamber and a background pressure of 1 kPa He. They analysed NPs from different parts of the chamber and found that the quality was uniform. They then annealed the NPs at temperatures from 400 to 800 °C. They found that at 600 °C, 25 to 30% of the NPs retained their initial size. A critical change occurred upon sintering at 800 °C, with development of two populations of crystallites.108
Skårman et al. investigated factors controlling growth, structure, and morphology of CuOx/CeO2 nanocomposites from IGC, as well as their activity as carbon monoxide oxidation catalysts. They employed resistive heating to evaporate pure cerium and copper granules with He as the inert gas in a UHV chamber with a base pressure below 10−9 Torr. They placed two or three tungsten crucibles simultaneously in the reactor. They found when the Cu crucible(s) were placed closer to the collection region than the Ce crucible(s), only very tiny clusters were produced before the onset of collision and aggregation with larger Ce clusters, which allowed them to achieve a high Cu dispersion on CeO2 and prevented the formation of bulk CuO during the subsequent oxygen treatment. They reported that the inert gas pressure and the composition of the composite had the most substantial effects on the morphology of the NPs. By tuning these parameters, they found that an inert gas pressure of 1.0 Torr provided the best conditions to control the particle growth and morphology of the NPs. Although the crystallite size changed substantially in the pressure range between 0.5 and 5 Torr, the particle size produced by the aggregation of the crystallites dramatically increased at pressures higher than 5 Torr. They considered CuOx/CeO2 nanocomposites in four groups of compositions. In the first group with Cu content below 6%, they reported two different particle shapes: amorphous rounded particle shapes and crystalline block particle shapes. For the second group with Cu content between 6 and 25%, the synthesized nanocomposites predominantly comprised monodispersed Cu particles on the surface of CeO2 aggregates, which is appropriate for CO adsorption. In the third group with Cu content between 25 and 70%, oxidation produced core–shell structures, explained in terms of sequential oxidation of Ce and Cu, with similar lattice expansion upon oxidation, which maintained high CuOx dispersion. Finally, for the fourth group with Cu content of more than 70%, they found amorphous aggregates with evenly distributed CeO2 crystallites that were insufficient for the appropriate dispersion and oxidation of Cu. In addition, they concluded that the CuOx/CeO2 catalyst system has improved low-temperature catalytic activity in carbon monoxide oxidization due to the interaction of CeO2 crystallites with a fast reversible redox process of superficial Cu(I)/(II) couples.96,97
2.1.1.2. Ion sputtering. 
2.1.1.2.1. Non-oxide metal-containing nanomaterials. 
Hahn and Averback created Al, Mo, Cu91Mn9, Al52Ti48, and AlN NPs using IGC with DC magnetron sputtering of the appropriate targets, rather than thermal or e-beam evaporation. Some of these (Mo, Cu91Mn9, and Al52Ti48) could not be synthesized by conventional thermal evaporation. The experimental set-up is shown in Fig. 6. The reactor had three main parts: the sputter source, a collection cold finger, and a scraping device. Because of its small sputtering yield, they were not able to use He as the inert gas for the synthesis. For the pure metals (Mo and Al) they used DC power of up to 1200 W and currents of 2 and 4.5 A which corresponded to voltages in the range of 200 to 400 V. NPs were synthesized at Ar pressures in the range of 0.3 to 2 mbar. Sputtering of Mo and Al targets under these conditions produced NPs with average sizes of 20 and 30 nm, respectively. They synthesized Cu NPs with an average size of ∼15 nm at Ar pressures from 0.1 to 1 mbar and sputter currents of 0.5 to 3 A. If we consider the vapor pressures at evaporation temperatures (1300 °C for Cu and Mn and 1800 °C for Ti and Al) of Cu (4.0 Pa), Mn (400 pa), Ti (40 Pa), and Al (5320 Pa), we can understand that conventional thermal evaporation methods are not appropriate for the synthesis of CuMn (with magnetic properties of interest) and TiAl (with useful high-temperature mechanical properties) alloys. With their sputtering IGC method, they synthesized nanocrystalline Cu91Mn9 and Ti48Al52 with average sizes from 7 to 14 nm and 20 nm, respectively. Furthermore, they produced AlN NPs in an Ar–N2 atmosphere.52
 Fig. 6  Schematic of the synthesis set-up in Hahn and Averback's method. Adapted from ref. 52 with permission from AIP Publishing, Copyright 1990. 
Chang et al. used the same strategy as Hahn and Averback to synthesize TiAl NPs. Instead of a conventional thermal evaporation system, they used magnetron sputtering to vaporize a Ti42Al58 target disc at 220 V and 4.0 A. The processing chamber was evacuated to 2 μPa then backfilled to 70 Pa with a 1:1 mixture of Ar and He. This allowed them to produce nanocrystalline TiAl NPs with average diameters between 10 and 15 nm at a production rate of 20 to 40 mg h−1.58
Weertman's group synthesized Al–Zr alloy NPs using an electron beam as the source of energy to achieve higher temperatures for evaporation. The compacted Al–Zr nanocrystalline alloy had impressively low creep rates.78
Yamamuro et al. synthesized Cr NPs with average size between 7.6 and 13 nm by a combination of IGC and glow-discharge sputtering and investigated the effects of pressure and gas flow rate on the NP size distribution. They used flows of 200 to 1000 sccm, and the pressure of the system increased with increasing gas flow (from 120 to 360 Pa). At low flow rates of 200 sccm (120 Pa) to 400 sccm (170 Pa) sccm, they produced monodispersed Cr NPs. Upon increasing the flow rate to 600 sccm (280 Pa) or more, the monodispersity of the NPs decreased, and two modes of the size distribution, centered at 5 and 20 nm, were observed, suggesting two different mechanisms of particle growth at those flow rates. At higher pressure, nucleation occurs near the sputtering source at a relatively high rate, while at lower pressure, nucleation occurs nearer the collection surface. Fig. 7 shows TEM images of the Cr NPs produced using various Ar flow rates.63
 Fig. 7  Bright-field TEM images of Cr NPs generated at various Ar flow rates, (a) VAr: 3.3 × 10−6 m3 s−1 and PAr: 120 Pa, (b) VAr: 6.7 × 10−6 m3 s−1 and PAr: 210 Pa, (c) VAr: 1.0 × 10−5 m3 s−1 and PAr: 280 Pa, (d) VAr: 1.33 × 10−5 m3 s−1 and PAr: 330 Pa, and (e) VAr: 1.67 × 10−5 m3 s−1 and PAr: 360 Pa. Adapted from ref. 63 with permission from AIP Publishing, Copyright 1999. 
Pérez-Tijerina et al. investigated factors that affect the size of Au/Pd NPs prepared by IGC with magnetron sputtering. They were able to synthesize NPs with a very narrow size distribution by controlling both the magnetron power and chamber atmosphere. They used 99.99% pure Au50Pd50 alloy as the target and studied the effect of four factors on the size of the NPs: (a) type of inert gas (He or Ar), with Ar leading to faster erosion of the metal target, higher supersaturation of metal vapor, and larger NPs; (b) pressure (0.1 to 0.2 Torr), showing increasing NP size with increasing pressure, (c) magnetron power (32 to 130 W), with increasing power first increasing the size of NPs then saturating, and eventually slightly decreasing, and (d) length of condensation zone (50 to 130 mm), with particle size increasing as the distance, and therefore aggregation time, increased. They optimized these parameters to generate monodispersed NPs of 1.1, 3.0 and 5.0 nm diameter with a uniform (not core–shell) structure.98
Mattei et al. synthesized trimetallic Au–Pt–Pd NPs, as candidates for heterogeneous catalysis applications, by placing Au, Pd, and Pt targets side by side on an integrated magnetron-sputtering head in an equilateral triangle formation (Fig. 8). For the samples with significant amounts of all elements, they found Au/Pt-rich core@Pd-shell structures due to a fundamental difference between Pd and Au/Pt growth dynamics. Au/Pt electronic structures drive formation of dimer nuclei and this will reduce their growth rate, while larger Pd particles grow faster and surround small Au and Pt clusters.117
 Fig. 8  (a) Schematic of magnetron-sputtering nucleation process within the experimental set-up. Adapted from ref. 117 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2019. 
Soganci et al. made copper NPs with an average size below 5 nm by DC magnetron sputtering into an Ar/He atmosphere. They dispersed the NPs on a single-layer graphene film to create a non-enzymatic biosensor for glucose detection. The resulting device exhibited excellent performance based on their electrochemical investigations.80
Yang et al. produced metastable Au–Cu bimetallic NPs with a narrow size distribution and controlled shape and composition by IGC with magnetron plasma sputtering. They employed a sputtering current of 60 mA with an Ar flow of 60 sccm and controlled the NP composition by adjusting power applied to each target. They characterized NPs by a combination of TEM and atom probe tomography (APT). TEM analysis showed bimetallic NPs with various non-equilibrium shapes and structures. Fig. 9(a)–(f) presents the TEM analyses of Au–Cu NPs, Fig. 9(g) shows EDX composition maps for three individual Au–Cu NPs, and Fig. 9(h) shows a schematic of the composition distribution of the NPs. Although the composition mapping by TEM-EDX was mostly qualitative, the APT analysis yielded deeper information on the NP composition. For the NPs below 4 nm diameter, copper was an alloying element that stabilized the Ih structure and was randomly distributed, while it segregated to the surface to form a Cu-rich shell on larger particles. This dual-characterization technique is a powerful approach to reveal multiple characteristics of the NPs, including structure, size, composition, and the variation of these characteristics with IGC process parameters.99
 Fig. 9  (a–c) TEM images of Au–Cu NPs, (d–f) high-resolution high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) TEM images of Au–Cu NPs, (g) EDX maps of three individual Au–Cu NPs, and (h) schematic illustrating the composition distribution of the Au–Cu NPs. Adapted from ref. 74 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2019. 
2.1.1.2.2. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Stappert et al. employed IGC to synthesize FePt NPs. They used DC sputtering at a power of 250 W to vaporize Fe55Pt45 alloy and produce FePt NPs with an average size from 3 to 30 nm. They studied the effects of inert gas (Ar and He) pressure and sintering temperature on the morphology and crystal structure of the FePt NPs. The FePt NPs were monodispersed with no sign of agglomeration at 0.5 mbar, but showed a broad particle size distribution and evidence of agglomeration above 1.0 mbar. Icosahedral particles were produced at 0.5 mbar and retained their structure at temperatures up to 1273 K. However, single crystalline particles with L10-ordering formed at pressures higher than 0.5 mbar.106
Zhao et al. employed IGC with magnetron sputtering of a 99.9% Fe target to generate Fe nanocubes. They used magnetron powers of 50, 70, and 100 W which corresponded to sputtering currents of 200, 300, and 400 mA, respectively. They controlled the Fe NP growth by varying differential pressure between the source and aggregation regions, while keeping gas flow and aggregation distance constant at 55 sccm and 90 mm, respectively. With the main chamber at 5.6 × 10−4 mbar and the aggregation zone at 0.2 mbar, they synthesized monodispersed ∼13 nm diameter spherical particles at 50 W and monodispersed nanocubes with a cluster width of about 10.5 nm at 100 W, due to competition between the growth modes of low-index (1 0 0) and (1 1 0) facets, rather than surface formation energetics. Powers between 50 and 100 W produced a combination of both shapes. Based on experimental and theoretical studies, they constructed a deposition rate-temperature diagram of Fe NP shapes (Fig. 10) showing that the combination of atomic deposition rate and condensation temperature define the final shape of the NPs.69 In another study, this group investigated the gas sensor application of the Fe nanocubes, as well as the doping of other metals such as Au into to them. They attributed excellent sensing properties of Fe nanocubes to both their specific morphologies and the advantages of aerosol NP synthesis. To make FeAu nanocubes, they evaporated two Au pellets in addition to the Fe target.71 They also employed a single-step process of DC magnetron sputtering in an inert argon atmosphere to achieve selective wetting of specific surface sites of iron nanocubes by gold atoms. This allowed them to control size, shape, gold concentration, and elemental distribution within a single NP at sizes below 10 nm. For this purpose, they performed co-sputtering of two iron targets and one gold target, each one inch in diameter. Upon exposure to ambient air, the BCC iron NP cores were coated with an iron oxide shell. Fig. 11 shows the structure and chemical composition of the Fe–Au for two Au concentrations. The multicomponent nanocubes can potentially be used for catalysis, drug delivery, and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).72
 Fig. 10  Atomic deposition rate-condensation temperature of Fe NPs shapes. Reproduced from ref. 69 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2016. 
 Fig. 11  Structure and chemical composition of Fe–Au nanocubes: (a) HRTEM of a nanocube with low Au concentration, (b) HAADF image of a nanocube with low Au concentration, (c) HAADF image of a nanocube with increased Au concentration, and (d) qualitative false colour image of the nanocube shown at c. Adapted from ref. 209 under creative commons license. 
2.1.1.2.3. Ceramic oxides. 
Hahn and Averback considered three different strategies for the synthesis of metal oxides NPs like Al2O3, NiO and ZrO2: (a) post-oxidation of the synthesized metal NPs generated using DC sputtering in an Ar atmosphere, (b) using reactive RF sputtering in an Ar–O2 atmosphere, and (c) using metal oxide targets instead of metal target with RF sputtering. The first approach may lead to agglomeration, and the third one is problematic, while the second approach is quite interesting for the synthesis of non-agglomerated particles because there is no exposure to high temperatures in this approach. Using the second approach, they synthesized monoclinic ZrO2 NPs with an average size of 20 nm. In addition, they created Al2O3 and NiO by DC diode sputtering in Ar–O2 atmosphere.52
Because of the ability of IGC to synthesize nonstoichiometric ultra-highly-dispersed oxides, Tschöpe et al. employed it to produce nonstoichiometric cerium oxide-based nanocrystalline catalysts for SO2 reduction. They used magnetron sputtering to evaporate Ce targets in Ar at pressures as low as 50 Pa. At this pressure, nucleation occurs homogeneously in the gas phase. Accordingly, they generated nonstoichiometric NPs with an average size between 8 and 14 nm, which were more effective in SO2 reduction by CO, CO oxidation, and methane oxidation compared with the corresponding stoichiometric NPs.107
Brossmann et al. studied oxygen diffusion in ultrafine-grained undoped ZrO2 synthesized by IGC. They used DC sputtering to vaporize Zr in Ar at 30 Pa. They oxidized the Zr nanocrystals collected on a cooled steel cylinder by gradual exposure to 1 kPa O2 which generated mainly tetragonal ZrO2 nanocrystals with an average size from 5 to 10 nm. Subsequently, pressureless sintering at 950 or 1050 °C produced samples with average crystallite sizes from 80 to 300 nm and densities from 97 to 99% of the bulk density. The diffusivity of 18O in interfaces in this undoped ZrO2 was 103 to 104 times its value for the corresponding bulk material in the temperature range from 450 to 950 °C, and was independent of the crystallite size in the range between 70 and 300 nm. Furthermore, they compared the volume diffusivity of oxygen in undoped ZrO2 with other transition metal oxides and ZrO2 doped with Y, Ca, and Mg. They found that it was far higher than other transition metal oxides and less than the doped ZrO2 NPs.91 Verma et al. used reactive sputtering to produce ZrO2 NPs. They found an optimum working pressure of 25 mTorr using Ar:O2 at a ratio of 40:10. They tested four different sputtering powers of 40, 60, 80, and 100 W to investigate its effect on NP size and morphology and found that increasing the sputtering power increased the size of the NPs (from 3 to 8 nm) and induced aggregation. TGA analysis showed only mass loss due to physiosorbed water, indicating the high purity of the ZrO2. The ZrO2 NPs are promising candidates for water purification applications because of the high purity and small size achieved by this method.92
2.1.1.2.4. Nanomaterials for oxygen evolution. 
Paoli et al. synthesized Ru and RuO2 by magnetron sputtering IGC and investigated the oxygen evolution activity of the NPs. For Ru NPs, they evaporated a Ru target by sputtering and to make RuO2 NPs, they exposed the as-synthesized Ru NPs to 1 bar oxygen in a tube furnace at 400 °C for 1 minute. They found the NPs exhibited one order of magnitude improvement in oxygen evolution activities relative to the state-of-the-art.95
2.1.1.3. Instrumentation and modelling. 
In 1995, Flagan and Lunden investigated particle structure control in IGC and pointed out that operation at higher pressure and shorter growth times could dramatically increase the production rate of NPs. The growth time could be controlled by the effective cooling rate in the growth region of the reactor.119
The relative simplicity of conventional IGC of a single metal allows it to be simulated at an atomistic level. Kesälä et al. performed classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations to investigate the pressure dependence of cluster growth in IGC. They varied pressure by changing the simulation volume while holding other parameters, including initial temperature and the number of atoms, constant. The background gas in their simulations was argon, and the metal atoms were initially given a higher temperature than the background gas, which was thermostatted at 300 K, allowing them to simulate energy transfer from the metal vapor to the inert gas. Simulating systems of various metal vapours modelled with appropriate interatomic interaction potentials enabled them to investigate the effect of pressure by simulating the initial stages of the inert gas growth process at five pressures in each case. They observed an inverse relation between the initial metal vapor partial pressure and the condensation time. In addition, their studies showed that the molecular mass and lattice constant of the metal are the key factors determining the condensation time. Notably, they derived an analytical model for the condensation time based on the kinetic theory of gases with the basic assumptions of classical nucleation theory for a homogeneous vapor. They were able to predict the time scale of cluster formation for various metals with different atomic mass and lattice constant, showing that materials with larger atomic mass formed clusters more slowly, while faster clustering was observed with increasing lattice constant.120
Quensel et al. modelled the synthesis of NPs by magnetron sputtering-based IGC. They synthesized relatively monodispersed copper NPs from 3 to 10 nm mean diameter by adjusting the length of the collision path which, in turn, varied residence time of the NPs in the carrier gas. Using these experimental results, they developed a detailed model for the nucleation and growth of NPs based on classical nucleation theory. The model helped them understand the influence of various operating parameters on the synthesis process and identify the reactor temperature profile as one of the main factors determining the NP size distribution.121 Their model predictions agreed well with experimentally measured size distributions at sufficiently low operating pressure, despite the inherent wide uncertainties associated with the use of classical nucleation theory, and different possible formulations of it.122,123 At sufficiently low pressures and metal vapor concentrations, the assumption of NP growth only by addition of individual atoms is valid, and growth by aggregation of particles is negligible, leading to particle size distributions that are more nearly Gaussian (normal) rather than log-normal, as also evident from experimental measurements. This both simplifies modelling and allows production of relatively monodispersed particles. A key value of this model was its utility for identifying effective operating conditions for new materials, provided their thermodynamic properties are well established, prior to beginning experiments.
Akbarzadeh et al. employed MD simulations to study the various structural and thermodynamic properties of pure palladium, platinum, and PtxPd1−x nanoclusters formed during IGC. The evolution of the number of clusters with time was almost independent of composition, because it is governed by aggregation. However, the overall cohesive energy of the clusters increased with increasing Pt content, simply because the cohesive energy of bulk Pt is higher than that of Pd. Moreover, they found that smaller nanoclusters, which were approximately spherical, had sharper peaks in the radial distribution function compared to the larger nanoclusters, which adopted a variety of shapes, depending on their history. Furthermore, they proposed that the smaller cohesive energy of palladium could explain the observation of palladium atoms in the outer shells of the nanoalloy, while the inner shells were primarily composed of platinum.124 They performed the same type of molecular dynamic simulations for the IGC of Ni–Pt nanoalloys. They observed that increasing the platinum concentration in the nanoalloy decreased the stability and the sphericity of the nanoclusters. In this case, the cores of the particles were rich in nickel atoms, while the platinum atoms dominated the outer layers of the nanoclusters.125 This group also employed MD simulation to study the relative stability, morphology, surface energy, and growth path of IGC-synthesized Au–Cu, Au-Rh, Au–Pd, and Au–Pt nanoalloys. Among these nanoalloys, Au–Pd exhibited the lowest stability, while Au–Pt was the most stable. In all these nanoalloys, the surface of the clusters was dominated by gold atoms, while the other element formed the core, which can be attributed to the low surface energy of gold. The Au-Ru and Au–Pt nanoalloys adopted fcc- and hcp-like crystal structures in the core part of the nanoclusters, while the gold surface atoms were amorphous. In addition, Au–Cu nanoalloys had more spherical morphology compared to the other nanoalloys because of the greater mobility of Cu relative to the heavier elements.126
Li et al. studied the IGC synthesis of ternary Fe–Ni–Cr NPs by MD simulation to achieve a deep atomic understanding of the behaviour and the mechanism of the process. They observed four stages in the process: nucleation, surface growth, coalescence, and agglomeration, along with severe surface segregation of chromium in the final clusters. They investigated the influence of the number of metal atoms on the nucleation, growth, and phase transition of the NPs and found that the nucleation rate was nearly unaffected by the finite size of the system, while it had effects on the surface growth of sub-nm clusters and the aggregation of the larger clusters, at the system sizes considered. Furthermore, they observed that the liquid-to-solid transition initiated in the core and propagated toward the outer layers, producing lamellar fcc/hcp layers or multiple-fold twins through internal structural relaxation. Moreover, they reported a linear relationship between the temperature of solidification and the inverse of the cluster size and, accordingly, the inverse of the cube root of number of metal atoms.127
Wen and Xia developed an energy analysis model to predict the size of magnesium NPs as a determining factor for the properties of NPs. This approach involves computing the total energy of a cluster and the rates of energy deposition (due to condensation of atoms onto the cluster) and energy dissipation as clusters grow. This method employs analytical expressions for the various energies and rate processes involved, in contrast to the MD approaches described previously. The energy analysis was successful in predicting the size of IGC-synthesized magnesium NPs, in the range of 20–50 nm, to within 10%. The model was then used to identify operating conditions that would produce smaller NPs. Both increasing temperature and decreasing pressure can lead to a decrease in the size of NPs, and the effect of temperature is more significant.128 They also predicted the morphology of aluminium NPs synthesized by IGC using an extension of this method. Gibbs energy and the energy variation of clusters during the process (due to energy deposition and dissipation) were considered to work together to determine the morphology of NPs, driving the transition from quasi-spherical to faceted nanocrystals. They observed, in their model of IGC synthesis of Al NPs, that the energy change during the synthesis process played a more significant role in determining the morphology when the cooling rate was below 2 × 1011 K s−1. Moreover, they reported that although a spherical morphology is the most stable one at short times, a cubic morphology became more dominant as the NPs grew. This transition is driven by the greater rate of energy dissipation from a cube, compared to a sphere. The pressure of the condensation chamber was the most important factor to determine the point at which the morphology changed from spherical to cubic. Based on this model, they concluded that the pressure in the condensation chamber and the cooling rate were the main parameters controlling morphology in this IGC synthesis process.53
Janssens et al. performed a classical molecular simulation using Metropolis Monte-Carlo (MMC) importance sampling to study the thermodynamic equilibrium configuration of bimetallic silver-cobalt clusters synthesized by IGC with laser vaporization. They considered a canonical ensemble with two types of trial moves: random displacement of each atom in the bimetallic cluster from its actual position, and random site exchange between silver and cobalt atoms (chemically different atoms). They found that compact 3D configurations were favourable and these followed an icosahedral growth pattern for all compositions. For the clusters with silver as the major element, the strongly bound cobalt dopants adopted highly-coordinated positions. However, when cobalt was the major element, loosely bound silver atoms were poorly coordinated at the cluster surface. In addition, they performed photofragmentation experiments showing that the primary mechanism of fragmentation was silver atom loss, consistent with the model prediction of relatively loosely-bound silver atoms.129


2.1.2. Spark discharge generation. 
In this technique, the precursors take the form of two electrodes that are rapidly charged to high voltage, such that the electric field between them exceeds the threshold for breakdown field of the gas between them. A plasma spark is generated between the electrodes, with a temperature that can exceed 20000 K, which vaporizes small quantities of the electrode materials in the vicinity of the spark. Rapid cooling following the spark leads to nucleation of NPs of the electrode material. Because the volume of the spark is small, this approach typically yields very small quantities of nanomaterials. As in other aerosol nanosynthesis methods, the nanomaterials are mixed with an unheated inert gas to stop their growth and limit the particle size. Compared to other evaporation-condensation techniques, the small vapor cloud allows more rapid cooling below the boiling point which generates a high concentration of very small particles. By varying the composition of the electrodes and employing reactive gases, a wide variety of nanomaterials including pure metals, composites, alloys, oxides, hybrids, and semiconductors can be produced with this process. Spark discharge generation is an environmentally friendly technique, because it produces NPs of the same materials that comprise the electrodes, without producing waste such as reaction byproducts or solvents. Also, this technique can generate nanomaterials with good reproducibility and high purity. In addition, simultaneous spark evaporation of multiple materials can produce unique new phases. However, the synthesis of nanomaterials at very low production rates and difficulties associated with variation in the breakdown voltage, which leads to variation in the energy per spark, are notable disadvantages of this method.27,166,167 Although size, number concentration, chemical composition, morphology, and surface area of the NPs can be controlled in this method, these factors cannot each be controlled independently.131Table 3 provides a summary and Fig. 12 presents representative TEM images of nanomaterials synthesized using this approach.

2.1.2.1. Monometallics. 
Schwyn, Garwin, and Schmidt–Ott pioneered the development of this process for the synthesis of nanomaterials. They first demonstrated it for production of monodispersed Au NPs with an average size of about 1.3 nm. A schematic of the synthesis process is presented in Fig. 13. They used cylindrical electrodes with a diameter of 3 mm separated by 1 mm. The electrodes were mounted in jaw chucks that enabled fast exchange of electrodes and precise alignment and control of the spacing between them. They used a power supply with a maximum voltage of 12 kV in constant current mode, which could be varied between 0 and 1 mA (corresponding to spark frequencies from 0 to 50 Hz), to charge a 2.2 nF capacitor. Nitrogen was used as the carrier gas. To avoid loss of charged particles by electrostatic precipitation to charged insulators, they used a conductive Al housing and outlet tube.165 Their group also worked to scale-up the process to an industrial level for synthesis of ultrapure non-agglomerated singlet NPs.168 They also studied the synthesis of silver atomic clusters using an atmospheric pressure generation technique and were successful in producing atomic clusters ranging from 1 to 25 atoms in size.151
 Fig. 13  Schematic of the spark discharge generation process developed by Schwyn et al. to produce Au NPs. Adapted from ref. 165 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1988. 
Many metallic vapor-phase synthesized nanomaterials can be easily oxidized by impurities in the carrier gas. This is a problem when the nanomaterials are later pressed and sintered to produce bulk samples with nanoscale grains, because oxide layers are incorporated. This can be mitigated by adding hydrogen during sintering. Hallberg et al. proposed an alternative strategy to prevent oxidation during the synthesis process. They mixed 5% hydrogen into the nitrogen used as the carrier gas in spark discharge synthesis of Au, Co, Bi, and Sn NPs using electrodes that were separated by 2 mm and eroded by a spark generator with a 10-mA constant current power supply charging a 38 nF capacitor. They compared the resulting NPs with those synthesized using nitrogen as the carrier gas, sintering each in a hydrogen mixture to demonstrate the advantage of their strategy. They found that the crystal quality of Bi and Sn synthesized using their strategy was remarkably higher than that produced using the conventional strategy. Thus, they concluded that their strategy can improve sintering of NPs into compact nanocrystalline metals by preventing the generation of oxide layers on the as-synthesized NPs.134
Vons et al. synthesized palladium NPs with a size of about 4 nm that formed fractal-like agglomerates up to 100 nm in size and investigated their hydrogen storage properties. For the synthesis, they used 2 mm palladium electrodes with 3 lpm of argon and achieved a production rate of about 2 mg h−1 at a spark frequency of 300 Hz. They determined hydrogen storage properties by thermal desorption spectroscopy (TDS) and Sievert's (PCT) measurements and concluded that spark discharge generation is advantageous for metal hydride NP synthesis.149
Němec et al. developed a spark discharge generator for the synthesis of platinum and platinum oxide NPs. A schematic of their system is shown in Fig. 14. It comprised a stainless-steel cylinder fitted with several ports, including two horizontal ports to accommodate 2 mm platinum rod electrodes. A modified TIG plasma torch held each electrode, allowing both electrical connection and liquid cooling. They employed a 2 mm tungsten rod as an auxiliary electrode to initiate the high-power sparks and stabilize their frequency. The cathode was powered and the anode was grounded. High-voltage probes and a current probe were used to monitor the process. Two vertical ports served as inlet and outlet for the carrier gas. The nozzle was engineered to produce parallel uniform streams directed toward the spark zone. In the unipolar set-up, the cathode is evaporated as the main source of NPs and is eroded. To synthesize platinum NPs, they used a 95:5 mixture of N2:H2 (forming gas) as the carrier gas. Spark discharge in air produced platinum oxide NPs. The crystallinity of the platinum NPs was higher and their size was smaller than the platinum oxide NPs. No matter which carrier gas was used, spherical platinum particles hundreds of nanometers in diameter were also produced, and their weight fraction was greater for synthesis in air.161
 Fig. 14  Schematic of the spark discharge generation process employed by Němec et al. to produce platinum and platinum oxide NPs. Reproduced from ref. 161 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
Li et al. synthesized lanthanum-based NPs by spark discharge generation at a voltage of 1.38 kV and current of 8.1 mA. They used two vertically-aligned 8 mm cylindrical electrodes separated by a 1 mm gap. Ten lpm of a 98:2 mixture of N2:O2 flowed past them. The NPs were oxidized, and subsequent mixing with water vapor in nitrogen converted them to La(OH)3 NPs. They deposited the La(OH)3 NPs onto PAN nanofibers by electrospinning and used them for phosphate removal, for which they showed impressive performance.154
2.1.2.2. Bimetallics. 
Tabrizi et al. produced Ag–Cu, Pt–Au, and Cu–W bimetallic NPs by a spark discharge technique with a constant spark energy from a 20 nF capacitor. They used Ag/Cu electrodes to produce Ag–Cu NPs that exhibited solid solubility of Ag in Cu and vice versa, even though Ag and Cu are essentially immiscible in the bulk. They synthesized Au–Pt particles with the same strategy and observed intermediate phases and compositions in the miscibility gap of the bulk phase diagram. They produced NPs including both Cu and W by applying sintered electrodes of Cu/W in the generator.145
Byeon et al. employed spark discharge to generate monometallic (using identical electrodes) and bimetallic (using different electrodes) NPs. The electrodes were 3 mm diameter metal rods. They used UHP nitrogen at 3 lpm as the carrier gas. In their spark generator, a 10 nF capacitor was charged at 2 mA by applying a voltage of 3 kV, producing sparks at 667 Hz. The short spark duration was advantageous for rapid cooling downstream of the spark. They produced Ag, Pd, Pt, and Au NPs with primary particle sizes from 2 to 4 nm in agglomerates of 14.9, 26.7, 37.7, and 48.8 nm dimensions, respectively. The corresponding production rates were 0.12, 1.68, 16.00, and 40.30 μg min−1, respectively. They suggested that the generation rate increased with increasing ionization potential for each metal (Ag < Pd < Pt < Au). They found that the synthesized bimetallic NPs were agglomerated binary mixtures of two individual monometallic particles and not alloys. They investigated the effect of changing the polarity of the electrodes on the mass fraction of bimetallic NPs and the production yield and they found when Pd is the positive electrode, Pd:Pt, Pd:Au, and Pd:Ag NPs had mass fractions of 0.84:0.16, 0.86:0.14, and 0.44:0.57 with production rates of 3.16, 4.37, and 1.51 μg min−1, respectively while the corresponding values were 0.95:0.05, 0.96:0.04, and 0.27:0.73 and 6.60, 7.18, and 1.03 μg min−1, respectively when Pd was the negative electrode. Moreover, they found that the size distribution of bimetallic NPs was strongly affected by the polarity of the electrodes.148
Ahmad et al. developed a novel rod-to-tube type spark generator to generate monometallic and bimetallic NPs for use in catalysis. A schematic of their apparatus is shown in Fig. 15. It comprised a spark chamber made from a polytetrafluorethylene (PTFE) tee. Rod and tube electrodes were placed in opposing inlets of the tee, which provided electrical isolation. To synthesize monometallic NPs, these two electrodes were composed of the same metal, while for bimetallic NPs electrodes of two different metals were used. The rod and tube electrodes both had a diameter of 6.35 mm, but to place one end of the rod inside the tube at its center, that end was sharpened. The tube also served as the aerosol outlet. The third inlet of the tee was used to introduce the carrier gas (Ar or N2) at 0.5 to 3.3 lpm. They used a high-voltage (up to 20 kV) source and current of 10 mA to charge a high-voltage external capacitor of 0.1–1 nF capacitance, which in turn was connected in parallel to the spark gap by a 1 MΩ ballast resistor. With this reactor configuration, they synthesized Fe, Ni, and Cu as monometallic catalysts with average sizes of 5.17, 5.46, and 4.54 nm, respectively, and Co–Ni and Co–Fe as bimetallic catalysts with average sizes of 4.72 and 4.90 nm, respectively. When a copper electrode was used, higher capacitance was required, which they attributed to the high thermal conductivity of copper. They also studied the effect of carrier gas flow rate on the size and number concentration of NPs and found that increasing the carrier gas flow rate decreased particle size and increased number concentration due to faster cooling and reduced residence time. Changing the carrier gas from nitrogen to argon decreased both the size and the number concentration of the NPs because of lower discharge voltage and effective discharge energy per spark in argon. Notably, their process could maintain a constant size distribution of NPs for 24 hours.135
 Fig. 15  Schematic of the rod-to-tube spark discharge generator employed to produce monometallic and bimetallic NPs by Ahmad et al. Adapted from ref. 135 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2018. 
2.1.2.3. Semiconductors. 
Helsper et al. generated carbon NPs which they called “soot-like” particles with an average primary particle size of 5 nm in aggregates of 50 to 200 nm overall size. They used two 6 mm diameter graphite electrodes mounted in brass collets and maintained at 2 mm separation by using a small electric motor to keep the discharge voltage constant. A 20 nF capacitor was charged by a high-voltage power supply with adjustable output current. They measured a breakdown voltage of 2 kV, which led to the release of 40 mJ of electrical energy. They studied the influence of spark frequency on yield and found that upon increasing the spark frequencies from 2 to 300 Hz (charging currents from 0.13 to 20 mA) the yield increased from 20 μg h−1 to 7 mg h−1.130
Vons et al. studied the synthesis of silicon NPs by spark discharge generation to test the feasibility of its use for production of semiconductors. To do so, they used a DC high-voltage power supply operating as a constant current source of maximum 60 mA, which charged a 20 nF capacitor. Argon was used as the carrier gas. They used two different types of electrodes: (a) intrinsic silicon rods with a diameter of 8 mm and a length of 6 cm and (b) boron-doped silicon rods with a diameter of 6 mm and a length of 6 cm and they found the latter case led to a thousand-fold increase in mass production. Silicon can be easily oxidized upon exposure to oxygen. To avoid this, they made the system oxygen- and water-free by gas purification, outgassing, and the use of as-synthesized particles as getters. Accordingly, they were successful in producing pure and monodispersed silicon NPs with a primary particle size between 3 and 5 nm.136
2.1.2.4. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Vons et al. produced core/shell Mg/MgO NPs by spark discharge, yielding 10 nm Mg cores surrounded by 1–2 nm thick MgO shells. In addition, they performed thermal hydrogen desorption which started at surprisingly low temperatures of about 350 K due to the small sizes of the particles, but extended over a broad temperature range above 700 K because of the presence of oxide shell barriers. They found that in situ addition of palladium NPs led to a considerable acceleration of hydrogen sorption kinetics.133 This group also studied synthesis and hydrogen storage properties of Mg–Ti nanocomposites using the same set-up. They produced a metastable bcc Mg–Ti alloy that transformed into an fcc alloy of Mg1−yTiyHx upon hydrogenation and had more favorable hydrogen storage properties compared to bulk MgH2. The primary particle size of both MgTi and Mg1−yTiyHx was 10–20 nm and they both had a fractal-like porous morphology. TEM images of the as-produced NPs and the NPs after partial hydrogenation are presented in Fig. 16.137 Charvet et al. investigated the effective density and initial particle size of airborne NPs including Constantan® (CuNi alloy), copper, graphite, iron, silver, and titanium NPs synthesized by spark discharge generation. Because of its role in particle deposition in the human respiratory tract, effective density is one of the most important properties of airborne NPs, but its direct measurement is generally not possible. Accordingly, they employed tandem measurement of airborne NPs’ electrical mobility and mass and found effective densities of constantan®, copper, graphite, iron, silver, and titanium agglomerated NPs, showing that the effective density decreased with effective mobility diameter of aggregates, with a power-law exponent of d−0.67 to d−1.02, and was always much lower than the bulk density.169 Muntean et al. also employed a spark discharge generation process to synthesize Cu, Ni, and CuNi alloy using electrodes with various compositions (Cu/Ni: 100/0, 80/20, 60/40, 40/60, 20/80, and 0/100). The spark discharge occurred at atmospheric pressure using a generator with a 6 mA constant current power supply to charge a 15 nF capacitor. The electrodes were 3 mm diameter rods and separated by 3 mm. With less than 7% difference in the composition of the NPs and the electrodes, they concluded that there was no segregation. They found that increasing the Cu content in the electrodes lowered the melting point, which led to an increase in the primary particle size and decrease in evaporation rate, in a good agreement with the Llewellyn-Jones model (eqn (1), in Section 2.1.2.5. below). The synthesized NPs had a roughly spherical morphology with a diameter in the range of 2–10 nm.141
 Fig. 16  TEM images of the Mg–Ti NPs produced by spark discharge generation. (a) as-produced and (b) after partial hydrogenation. Adapted from ref. 137 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2013. 
Feng et al. synthesized LaFeSi NPs as the first rare-earth-based magnetic materials produced by spark discharge generation. They employed LaFe11.5Si1.5 rods as electrodes with a perpendicular flow of Ar or 2% or 5% H2 in Ar. The molar ratio of La to Fe in the product NPs was 1:11.46, closely matching that of the electrodes. The TEM analyses, elemental line-scan EDX profiles of a NP, HAADF-STEM and EDX maps are presented in Fig. 17. They studied the magnetic properties of the NPs and found that they could control the Curie temperature over a broad range of 200–340 K by adjusting the hydrogen content in the carrier gas mixture. 5% H2 in the carrier gas led to higher magnetization at low temperatures and a moderately sharp magnetic to nonmagnetic phase transition, while reducing the hydrogen content to 2% led to a shallow phase transition and decreased magnetization. Without hydrogen, there was no phase transition and the magnetization was much weaker (Fig. 18). They suggested two main reasons for these observations: (1) formation of hydrides La(Fe, Si)13Hx which prevents oxidation, and (2) modification of magnetic exchange coupling between iron atoms by incorporation of hydrogen. Accordingly, the Curie temperature could approach room temperature by adjusting the hydrogen content in the carrier gas without a substantial decrease in the large magnetocaloric effect, which made these NPs promising candidates for the energy conversion and efficient magnetic refrigeration.155
 Fig. 17  (a) TEM image of LaFeSi alloy NPs along with a histogram to show the monodispersity of the NPs. (b) TEM image of the NPs with the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern indicating the amorphous structure of the NPs. (c) Representative EDX line-scan profiles of a NP. (d) Images from HAADF-STEM and EDX elemental maps. Adapted from ref. 155 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2018. 
 Fig. 18  Magnetization phase transition of the NPs produced with various compositions of the mixture of argon and hydrogen in carrier gas. Adapted from ref. 155 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2018. 
2.1.2.5. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Tabrizi et al. modeled the synthesis of gold NPs by spark discharge generation. They found that particle heating has a strong effect on the particle growth under high-vapor-loading conditions because of the release of surface free energy upon coalescence. In addition, they reported that the gas flow rate does not affect the particle size for sufficiently high gas flow rates due to a very short primary particle synthesis process, after which the particle growth is completed and is not affected by dilution, while final product size is influenced by the gas flow rate at small flows because of slow coagulation at room temperature after primary particle production. They found that they can use the Llewellyn-Jones model (eqn (1)170) to estimate the mass production rate and the energy balance around hot spots: (1)where m (g) is the evaporated mass, ce (F) is effective gap capacitance, V (V) is the breakdown voltage, Tb (K) is the boiling point, b (J K−4) is equal to Aσt in which σ is Stefan-Boltzmann constant (5.67 × 10−8 J s−1 m−2 K−4), A (m2) is the surface area of electrodes, and t (s) is the time for energy transfer, k (W m−1 K−1) is thermal conductivity, g (m s) is equal to 2(πA)0.5t if (r2/Kt)0.5 ≪ 1, in which K is thermal diffusivity (m2 s−1) and r (m) is the radius corresponding to A, T (K) is the steady state temperature, cps (J g−1 K−1) is the average heat capacity of the solid, Tm (K) is the melting point, ΔHm (J g−1) is the enthalpy of melting, cpl. (J g−1 K−1) is the average heat capacity of the liquid, and ΔHv (J g−1) is the enthalpy of evaporation. They synthesized monodispersed and non-agglomerated Au NPs of sizes from 3 to 12 nm, which was controlled by the energy per spark (which can be influenced by capacitance, distance between electrodes, frequency, and carrier gas type and flow rate) with a typical mass productivity of 5 g kW−1 h−1. Based on the flow rates of the gas, the particles could be agglomerated as well. They found that the sintering of gold NPs could occur at unusually low temperatures due the cleanliness of the synthesized particles.166
Roth et al. used the same spark generator and the same strategy for the synthesis of silver, cadmium oxide, iridium, iron oxide, and carbon NPs with surface areas of 20, 50, 123, 300, and 750 m2 g−1, respectively. They reported the flow rate of argon should be at least 6 lpm to remove all NPs from the reaction zone and exchange the gas volume in the zone when the spark frequency is 300 Hz. The average sizes for the silver, cadmium oxide, iridium, iron oxide, and carbon NPs were 20 nm (f = 300 Hz), 35 nm & 50 nm (f = 6 Hz & 300 Hz), 18 nm & 22 nm (f = 140 Hz & 500 Hz), 35 nm (f = 20 Hz), and 25 nm & 70 nm (f = 3 Hz & 300 Hz), respectively. They found if they did not mix Ar (6.3 lpm) with N2 (17 lpm) for the synthesis of carbon NPs at a frequency of 300 Hz, the size of the agglomerates increased from 70 to 150 nm. They concluded that the size and the number concentration of NPs were variable while the morphology and the surface area were constant, independent of particle size for an identical chemical composition. They reported a high surface-to-volume ratio of 2800 m2 cm−3 for the iridium NPs. In addition, they found that although they could not produce highly-pure carbon NPs due to contamination within the spark generator, they could decrease the contamination content from 25% to 6% by using pure inorganic components instead of organic components.131
Domaschke et al. conducted a comprehensive investigation on the spark discharge synthesis of a wide variety of metals and amorphous carbon to isolate the effects of various parameters. They reported that decreasing the power produced smaller NPs. Increasing carrier gas flow decreased particle concentration, and therefore the rate of coagulation and, accordingly, the size of aggregates. They found that increasing energy per spark, and energy efficiency of the process, was possible by employing a higher capacitance at fixed input power. This increased the mass production rate with accompanying increases in both the size and concentration of NPs. Furthermore, using electrodes of the same material, but different diameters and using electrodes with different thermal conductivities allowed them to conclude that energy loss via thermal conduction away from the electrode tips was a significant factor in decreasing the mass yield and the size of NPs. In addition, they defined a modified Fourier number to correlate the mass production rate of various nanomaterials in terms of discharge time, electrode diameter, and thermal properties of the material being produced.171
Feng et al. developed a model to predict the composition of nanoalloys produced by spark discharge generation between two different electrodes. Their analysis provides a means to control composition by tuning the electrical characteristics of the spark-discharge circuit to adjust the ablation ratio between two different electrodes, which determines the average composition of the synthesized NPs. Development of the model relied on separation of circuit-dependent and material-dependent contributions. Their work further advanced the ability to use spark discharge generation to synthesize nanoalloys of immiscible materials over a range of compositions, at macroscopic scale.172
Kabirinia et al. developed a mathematical model based upon the aerosol general dynamic equation to predict the size of tungsten carbide NPs synthesized by spark discharge generation. They calculated all the necessary parameters to develop the mathematical model without any external data. They evaluated their model against experimental data on synthesis of tungsten carbide NPs in N2 and Ar atmospheres. Results agreed well, with an average error of 15% for particle size, compared with crystallite size from XRD analysis and the Scherrer equation.173


2.1.3. Laser ablation. 
Laser ablation is a laser-driven technique to synthesize nanomaterials from a bulk solid precursor. It involves vaporizing a plume of material using a laser. Most often, this is a pulsed laser that produces a transient vapor plume, but some continuous laser ablation processes have also been reported. Key advantages of the method are that it can vaporize materials that cannot otherwise easily be evaporated, can produce high-purity nanomaterials, can use many different target materials alone or in combination, and can produce NPs with various compositions.27,174 A major disadvantage of this approach is that only small amounts of nanomaterials can be produced.27,175 The requirement of a complex non-isothermal system, very limited demonstration of scale-up, and expensive equipment are other limitations of the method.175 In addition to aerosol generation of NPs, this approach has been widely used for the synthesis of nanomaterials by vaporizing solid precursors in liquid or supercritical fluid media and for the production of thin films, both of which are beyond the scope of this review. Table 4 provides a summary and Fig. 19 presents representative TEM images of nanomaterials synthesized in a gaseous atmosphere using this approach.
