2.1.3.1. Metals and metal oxides. 
Dietz, Duncan, Powers, and Smalley pioneered pulsed laser ablation with supersonic expansion for the synthesis of Al clusters. They employed a Nd:YAG pulsed laser producing 80 mJ per pulse to vaporize an Al plug within a pulsed supersonic nozzle. The vaporized metal atoms were entrained in the expanding helium which led to formation of a supersonic beam of Al clusters at 1.2 m downstream of the nozzle, which was operated at 10 pulses per second in a He atmosphere with a 28.5 atm backing pressure.176 Although their set-up was effectively generating the Al clusters, they found some problems regarding reproducibility and stability of the Al cluster signals. They found that the pulsed laser quickly drilled deep holes on the surface of the target which made vaporization at the same spot extremely difficult. Accordingly, they changed the set-up to move the target before each laser pulse. With this modification, they generated Cu clusters containing 1 to 13 Cu atoms with a high degree of reproducibility. During the pulsed vaporization in a He background using a Nd:YAG laser, they rotated and translated a Cu rod to achieve uniform vaporization at a frequency of 10 Hz with 5 to 50 mJ laser pulses. A simple schematic of the set-up is presented in Fig. 20.177 Svetlichnyi et al. produced iron oxide magnetic NPs using pulsed laser ablation of iron in air followed by annealing. They ablated an iron target using 7 ns pulses from a Nd:YAG laser at 1064 nm in air at atmospheric pressure. The primary product was a mixture of spherical monoclinic magnetite (mainly 12 to 15 nm in size) and iron nitrides in the form of lamellae and rolls. Upon thermal treatment, the size of their NPs increased and three subsequent phase transformations were observed: first, to cubic magnetite; second to a mixture of α-Fe2O3, γ-Fe2O3, and Fe3O4; and third, to the pure hematite phase.178
 Fig. 20  Schematic of the set-up employed by Powers et al. to synthesize copper clusters by laser ablation. Adapted from ref. 177 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 1982. 
Becker et al. synthesized Ag, Au, and Ni0.81:Fe0.19 NPs by pulsed laser ablation of microparticles. They employed a 248 nm pulsed KrF excimer laser with a 12 ns pulse width. As targets, they used silver and gold microparticles with a nominal diameter of 2 μm and Ni0.81:Fe0.19 with a maximum size of 45 μm, which was reduced to an average size of around 4 μm after a suspension in methanol and settling out of large particles. The average sizes of the synthesized NPs were from 10 to 100 nm. They found that increasing the laser power increased the average size of the synthesized NPs.179 Boutinguiza et al. employed pulsed laser ablation in open air conditions to generate silver NPs. They used highly-pure Ag plates as the target, with a nanosecond diode-pumped Nd:YVO4 laser with a wavelength of 532 nm, pulse duration of 50 ns, repetition rate of 20 kHz, average power of 6 W, and irradiance of around 100 MW cm−2. The targets were connected to a holder positioned 0.5 to 1.5 cm away from the glass substrate used for collection of the synthesized silver NPs. Ar was used in this experiment as the carrier gas to prevent oxidation. Accordingly, they produced spherical silver NPs with maximum diameters of 50 nm.180
Zhong et al. synthesized IrO2 NPs by pulsed laser ablation of ball-milled IrO2 powder spread on a substrate. A Nd:YAG pulsed laser with a pulse duration of 8 ns and frequency of 10 Hz ablated the powder for 10 s at a power of 1 W under ambient atmosphere and room temperature to produce core–shell NPs in which IrO2 cores were surrounded by ultra-small (∼2 nm) Ir spheres that aggregated into a shell. A schematic representing the in situ engineering of the Ir spheres as a shell around an IrO2 crystal as the core along with the TEM analysis of the nanomaterials is presented in Fig. 21. They used these NPs as electrocatalysts for the oxygen evolution reaction. The ultra-small Ir spheres in the shell increased the electrochemically active surface area and improved the electrical conductivity of the electrocatalysts, which accelerated the oxygen evolution reaction compared to the unmodified IrO2 structure. In addition, they reported that this nanomaterial showed the best electrocatalytic performance among all noble-metal electrocatalysts toward oxygen evolution reaction.239
 Fig. 21  (a) Schematic of the in situ engineering of the Ir shell surrounding the IrO2 crystal as a core. (b) Traditional TEM analysis show the morphology of the nanomaterials. (c) STEM analysis of the nanomaterials with an aberration corrector. Adapted from ref. 239 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2019. 
Song et al. applied pulsed laser ablation to produce core/shell structured Zn/ZnO NPs in high yield. They ablated a commercial pristine zinc oxide nanopowder in a chamber filled with Ar, using a 1070 nm ytterbium fiber laser (600 J cm−2, 1.1 MW cm−2). They proposed that in the aerosol produced by the laser ablation, metallic Zn would nucleate in the sealed Ar atmosphere, and further oxidization at the surface would produce core/shell Zn/ZnO NPs. Furthermore, they reported that the core/shell nanostructure exhibited enhanced photocatalytic degradation efficiency due to improved visible light absorption as well as inhibited recombination of carriers induced by the incorporation of metallic Zn.229
2.1.3.2. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Guo et al. first reported the synthesis of multiwalled carbon nanotubes (MWCNT) using pulsed laser ablation. They explained that carbon condensation in an inert atmosphere heated to 1200 °C will lead to self-assembly of MWCNT due to its inherent growth mechanism, which stabilizes the open conformation against closure. This contrasted with prior syntheses in which some extrinsic factor such as catalytic metal particles, high electric fields, hydrogen atoms, or a surface at low temperature helped to keep the MWCNT structure open at its growing end. To produce MWCNTs, they ablated a 1.25 cm diameter graphite rod in an Ar atmosphere using a Nd:YAG pulsed laser at 532 nm, emitting 250 mJ per pulse, with a pulse width of 10 ns, and repetition rate of 10 Hz. The ablation was conducted in a temperature-controlled quartz tube furnace at 1200 °C in high-purity Ar. They deposited the synthesized carbon species on a conical water-cooled copper rod. The MWCNT consisted of 4 to 24 layers with a maximum length of 300 nm.183 They used the same apparatus for the catalytic synthesis of SWCNT with yields much greater than the yields obtained by the metal-catalyzed arc-discharge method at the time of their research. For SWCNT synthesis, they evaporated metal-graphite composite rods as targets, with the following concentrations (in at%) of metals: Ni (0.6), Co (1.0), Pt (0.2), Cu (0.6), Nb (0.6), Co/Ni (0.6/0.6), Co/Pt (0.6/0.2), Ni/Pt (0.6/0.2), and Co/Cu (0.6/0.5). Among the single metal-graphite composite targets, Ni had the highest yield followed by Co while the yield for Pt was very small. Cu and Nb did not produce any SWCNT. Among the bimetal-graphite composite targets, Co/Pt and Co/Ni had the highest yields, which were 10–100 times the yields obtained using single metal-composite targets. The yield for Ni/Pt was slightly less, while the Co/Cu composite had a small yield. They found that the yields increased with increasing temperature of the tube furnace, up to a maximum temperature of 1200 °C. Notably, they did not observe any MWCNT.186 They also investigated the metallic resistivity of SWCNT with crystalline ropes as small as 10 nm in diameter and length exceeding 10 μm synthesized by the pulsed laser ablation of (Co,Ni)-doped graphite. They found that the synthesized SWCNT had lower resistivity and lower crossover temperature compared to unoriented bulk samples.242 In addition, they doped SWCNT with potassium and bromine, which enhanced their conductivity at 300 K by up to a factor of 30 while also enlarging the range of their positive temperature coefficient of resistance.191 Zhang et al. employed pulsed laser ablation to generate SWCNT in a nitrogen atmosphere and found that using a bimetallic mixture of Co–Ni as the catalyst produced SWCNT at the same yield as in a noble gas using the same catalyst.187 They used the same strategy to produce multiwalled B–C–N nanotubes by pulsed laser ablation of a powder mixture of carbon, boron nitride, cobalt, and nickel. They reported two types of nanotubes: thinner nanotubes of pure carbon and thicker nanotubes with B–C–N layers. Moreover, they observed no single-walled nanotubes in this case.190 Scott et al. studied the mechanisms of pulsed laser ablation synthesis of SWCNT. They proposed that the carbon as feedstock for nanotube synthesis comes from carbon clusters suspended in the reaction zone in addition to the direct ablation of the target. Fullerenes synthesized in the reaction zone could not only be fragmented into low molecular weight species like C2, but also play the role of feedstock for the synthesis of nanotubes. The laser beam confinement of the nanotubes in the reaction zone provides time for purification and annealing of the nanotubes.243
Iijima et al. employed laser ablation to produce aggregates of numerous horn-like sheaths of single-walled graphene sheets, which they called “carbon nano-horns”. Fig. 22 shows TEM images of the synthesized carbon nano-horns. They produced these using a two-part system with a separate energy source part and a reaction part. This configuration is similar to the laser pyrolysis synthesis set-up that is discussed in detail in a later section. The energy source was a high power 10.6-μm-CO2 laser with a maximum power of 5 kW, variable pulse duration from 10 ms to continuous-wave operation, and beam diameter of 10 mm. The reaction part included a plastic-resin reaction chamber with dimensions of 30 × 30 × 25 cm3 to which a ZnSe lens system was attached to adjust the beam intensity. The ablation target was a graphite rod with a diameter of 30 mm and length of 50 mm, which was placed at the center of the reaction chamber and rotated around its axis at a rate of 6 rpm to continuously expose a fresh surface. After pre-evacuation of the chamber, Ar at 760 Torr and a flow rate of 40 lpm entered the reaction chamber. The laser beam ablated the rod, and the synthesized particles were collected onto filters placed between the reaction chamber and the vacuum pump. The graphitic particles with a uniform size of about 80 nm were produced at a rate of around 10 g h−1.192 Note that the use of a high-power CO2 laser enabled much higher production rates than is typical for laser ablation. Iijima's group also synthesized nitrogen-doped, boron-doped, and boron- and nitrogen-codoped single-wall carbon nano-horns (SWCNHs) in addition to pristine SWCNHs. A schematic illustrating the structure of the synthesized SWCNHs is presented in Fig. 23. The CO2 laser with a power of 3.5 kW ablated the target, which was rotating at 2 rpm, for 30 s. To synthesize intrinsic SWCNHs, the target was a graphite rod, as used previously, and the process was conducted under argon. Use of nitrogen as the buffer gas produced nitrogen-doped SWCNHs from the same target. They followed the same strategy for boron-doped and boron- and nitrogen- co-doped SWCNHs using a target containing 2 at% boron. In this case, they obtained spherical SWCNH aggregates dominantly containing graphite-like thin sheets, at a rate of 300 mg min−1. The structures contained graphene lattices including BN bonds, demonstrating boron and nitrogen incorporation. These dopant atoms were present as three-fold coordinated (sp2 bonded) centers in the SWCNHs. The preferential formation of graphite-like thin sheets under argon atmosphere led them to conclude that boron facilitated the formation of these sheets. Based on these results, the authors proposed that large-scale production of these SWCNHs is possible and that practical synthesis of boron-doped and boron- and nitrogen- codoped SWCNTs at ambient temperature and pressure under argon and nitrogen could reduce production cost.196
 Fig. 22  (a) TEM image of the carbon nano-horns NPs produced by Iijima et al. which presents the relatively uniformity of the nanomaterials. (b) A magnified TEM image of the carbon nano-horns which exhibits aggregation of tubule-like structures. Adapted from ref. 192 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1999. 
 Fig. 23  Schematic illustrating the boron- and nitrogen-doped SWCNHs synthesized by Yuge et al. using laser ablation. Reproduced from ref. 196 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2017. 
2.1.3.3. Silicon nanomaterials. 
Okada and Iijima used pulsed laser ablation to produce silicon NPs with diameters from 20 to 500 nm. They did so in an argon atmosphere over a pressure range of 0.75 to 3 Pa using a pulsed YAG laser with an energy of 40 to 90 J per pulse, pulse width of 12 ms, and repetition rate of 1 Hz. Furthermore, they reported that the oxidation rate of the synthesized silicon particles was slower than that of a silicon wafer and decreased with decreasing particle size.206 Werwa et al. synthesized silicon NPs as small as 3 nm. They used a doubled Nd:YAG laser at 532 nm, energy per pulse of 3 mJ, pulse width of 7 ns, and repetition rate of 20 Hz with a 0.63 cm diameter polycrystalline Si rod as a rotating target. The target was positioned adjacent to a pulsed valve that controlled a flow of unheated He that served as an inert gas to carry as-synthesized Si NPs through a 1 mm diameter by 8 mm long channel into a vacuum chamber. Cooling upon expansion into vacuum stopped the growth and limited the particle size.207 Yoshida et al. employed pulsed laser ablation at constant pressure to produce ultrafine crystalline silicon NPs with a minimum size of around 3 nm and found that the size of silicon NPs could be controlled by the pressure of the inert gas atmosphere based on an inertia fluid model (dm ∝ p3/2). They used an ArF excimer laser at 193 nm, with an energy density of 1 J cm−2 per pulse, pulse width of 12 ns, and repetition rate of 10 Hz to evaporate a 2-inch single crystalline silicon wafer in a He atmosphere.208
Momoki et al. employed pulsed laser ablation of preheated ground silicon sludge to generate mainly amorphous silicon nanofibers with crystalline NPs on their tips. A pulse generator drove a quasi-continuous wave fiber laser with a maximum power of 500 W and a wavelength of 1070 nm as the source of energy. The duration of the pulse was varied from 1 to 10 ms. The 500 μm diameter laser beam at a power density of 178 kW cm−2 ablated the sludge target under argon. They studied the effect of gas pressure, laser pulse duration, and preheating condition of the target on the synthesis process. They found that the gas pressure affected the intensity, size, and growth speed of the laser-induced plume, while the laser pulse duration affected the size and morphology of the nanofibers. In addition, they could control the crystallinity and oxygen content of the nanofibers by adjusting the preheating conditions of the sludge. Fig. 24 shows a schematic diagram illustrating the production mechanism of the silicon nanofibers and a representative SEM image. Because the target was silicon sludge disposed from wafer back-grinding processes, this technique can be considered a cost-effective approach to synthesize silicon nanofibers which could be used in lithium-ion batteries.209
 Fig. 24  (a) Schematic illustrating the formation mechanism of silicon cluster nanofibers. (b) Representative SEM image of the silicon nanofiber clusters. Adapted from ref. 209 under creative commons licenses. 
Geohegan et al. performed time-resolved imaging to investigate the dynamics of NP synthesis, transport and deposition using pulsed KrF laser ablation of crystalline silicon into Ar and He as the background gases over a pressure range of 1 to 10 Torr. They reported that vaporization of silicon into Ar produced a uniform and stationary plume of NPs while ablation in much lighter He generated a turbulent ring of particles that spread and advanced forward at 10 m s−1.216
2.1.3.4. Nanorods, nanowires and nanocables. 
Morales and Lieber employed pulsed laser ablation followed by vapor-liquid–solid (VLS) growth to synthesize nanowires of semiconductors like Si and Ge. They used a pulsed frequency-doubled Nd:YAG laser to vaporize the target, which was a mixture of the source of nanowires and the catalyst. The target was positioned in a quartz tube furnace with variable pressure, temperature, and residence time. To make Si nanowires, they ablated Si0.9Fe0.1 that was preheated to 1200 °C in the furnace. This yielded silicon nanowires with diameters as low as 10 nm and lengths ranging from 1 to 30 μm. TEM imaging also showed spheres with diameters of 1.5 to 2 times that of the attached nanowires, consistent with a VLS growth process. Considering TEM and EDX results together, they concluded that the nanowires comprised silicon cores with diameters of 6 to 20 nm coated by amorphous silica. TEM images of the silicon nanowires at two different magnifications are shown in Fig. 25. Note that synthesis of silicon nanowires using iron catalyst happened at temperatures above 1150 °C. While this is below the 1207 °C bulk solidus melting point for Si–Fe, nanostructures have lower melting points than their corresponding bulk solids. They synthesized Si nanowires by ablating Si0.9Ni0.1 and Si0.99Au0.01 as targets. These nanowires grew from β-NiSi2 and Au nanoclusters, respectively. Following the same strategy, they generated germanium nanowires by the pulsed laser ablation of Ge0.9Fe0.1 as the target.210 Zhang et al. synthesized pure Si nanowires with diameters of 3 to 43 nm and up to a few hundred μm length in high yield. They achieved a growth rate of 10 to 80 μm/h by high-temperature laser-ablation of a target prepared by pressing silicon powder mixed with 0.5% iron. The energy source was an excimer laser at 248 nm emitting 400 mJ per pulse with a pulse-width of 34 ns and a frequency of 10 Hz. They used argon as the carrier gas at 50 sccm and 500 Torr in a quartz tube furnace at 1200 °C. They found twins and stacking faults in the cores of the nanowires.211 Later, they used silica instead of iron as the catalyst for the formation of Si nanowires by pulsed laser ablation.212 Yu et al. employed pulsed laser ablation with a 246 nm pulsed excimer laser producing around 350 mJ per pulse for the large-scale production of silica nanowires with diameters around 15 nm and lengths up to hundreds of μm. The disk-like target was a mixture of Si powder and 20 wt% of silica powder in which 8 wt% Fe powder was added as the catalyst. The target was located in a quartz tube furnace that was pre-evacuated to 20 mTorr and preheated to 850 °C for 4 hours. Prior to the ablation in an Ar atmosphere at 100 Torr, the target was heated at 1200 °C for 24 h. The synthesized silica nanowires stably emitted blue light at energies of 2.65 and 3.0 eV with an intensity two orders of magnitude higher than that of porous Si.214
 Fig. 25  (a) TEM image of silicon nanowires synthesized by laser ablation from a Si0.9Fe0.1 target. (b) Diffraction contrast TEM image of silicon nanowires in which the crystalline silicon core is darker than the amorphous silica shell. Adapted from ref. 210 with permission from The American Association for the Advancement of Science, Copyright 1998. 
Zhang et al. used pulsed laser ablation of a mixture of SiO, C, BN, and Li3N compressed powders to synthesize coaxial nanocables that were a few tens of nm in diameter and up to 50 μm in length. These combined silicon-based nanowires (including β-phase SiC core and an amorphous silica intermediate layer) surrounded by a graphitic outer shell (including separate carbon and boron nitride layers in the radial direction). They suggested that this semiconductor-insulator-semiconductor structure would be advantageous for nanoscale electronic device applications because of the self-assembly mechanism for the evolution of multielement nanotube.219 Shi et al. employed the same strategy by ablation of a SiC target to synthesize coaxial three-layer nanocables in which the 30 to 50 nm diameter core was made of silicon, the 12 to 23 nm thick intermediate layer was made of silica, and 17 to 31 nm thick outer shell was made of carbon. A low magnification TEM image of the coaxial three-layer nanocables and corresponding HRTEM image are presented in Fig. 26. They used a gaseous mixture of 95% Ar and 5% H2 as the carrier gas. They proposed that minimization of the surface and interfacial energies of the three materials led to the formation of the coaxial nanostructure via separation of thee three phases, but solid-gas chemical reactions played a central role in the evolution of the three phases.218
 Fig. 26  (a) Low-magnification TEM image of coaxial three-layer Si@SiO2@C nanocables synthesized by Shi et al. through laser ablation. (b) HRTEM image of a nanocable. Adapted from ref. 218 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2000. 
Woodard and Duncan developed a novel pulsed laser ablation reactor that included in situ ligand coating. A schematic of the production process is presented in Fig. 27. They employed a 248 nm high power KrF excimer laser producing 100 to 140 mJ per pulse at a repetition rate of 300 to 400 Hz under helium, in which a stream of 2 to 10% oxygen flowed, to synthesize molybdenum oxide nanorods. Thereafter, the nanorods were ligand-coated by evaporated acetonitrile, delivered in argon at 50 °C. Finally, the ligand-coated nanorods were collected in a liquid-nitrogen cold trap such that they were dispersed in the excess acetonitrile upon melting. Subsequent evaporation of the solvent yielded molybdenum oxide nanorods with a length of 100 nm, diameter of 20 nm and a narrow size distribution, as a blue powder. The semiamorphous nanorods crystallized upon annealing, but the as-produced nanorods exhibited higher activity as photocatalysts for the degradation of methylene blue. The pulsed laser ablated nanorods were smaller and accordingly more reactive compared to nanorods produced by other techniques. Notably, the ligand-coating is advantageous for the stable dispersion of the nanorods in common solvents, which facilitates their use in many applications.234
 Fig. 27  A schematic of the set-up developed by Woodard and Duncan to produce MoO3 nanorods by laser ablation with in situ ligand coating. Reproduced from ref. 234 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2019. 
2.1.3.5. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Bulgakova and Bulgakov synthesized graphite, Nb, and YBCO using a pulsed laser ablation technique. They ablated the target materials in a UHV chamber at a pressure of 1 mPa using a Q-switched Nd:YAG laser at 1064 nm, with pulse duration of 24 ns, and laser fluences in the range of 1 to 25 J cm−2. The targets were polycrystalline graphite, pure Nb, and YBCO superconductor. Based on evidence for the transition from normal evaporation to phase explosion, they found that the ablation rate substantially increased for graphite, Nb, and YCBO targets at 22, 15, and 17.5 J cm−2 as the threshold fluences, respectively, corresponding to the transition from normal vaporization to phase explosion.202
Stoian et al. considered a process called “Coulomb explosion” (CE) as the mechanism of ablation during low fluence ultrashort pulse laser ablation, providing important insight in to ablation mechanisms. CE is a process in which a particle or insulating surface becomes so highly charged that repulsive Coulomb forces are stronger than the cohesive forces holding the material together, and it spontaneously explodes. The energy source for this study was a Ti:Al2O3 oscillator-amplifier laser generating 800 nm light with a pulse duration as low as 0.1 ps. They ablated the target in a UHV chamber at a pressure of 1 mPa using pulse energy densities of 5 and 4 J cm−2 for durations of 0.1 and 0.2 ps, respectively, which were slightly higher than the single-shot ablation threshold. They considered two different regimes for ablation: “gentle” and “strong”. In the first one, the ablation rate is low and just a few monolayers are vaporized by each laser shot. In the “strong” regime, the ablation rate per pulse was an order of magnitude greater and “phase explosion” characteristics were observed that were qualitatively different from the ablation by nanosecond UV laser pulses. They found that ablation in the “gentle” regime led to equal ion momenta due to impulsive CE from the surface region lasting about 1 ps while ablation in the “strong” regime, which is associated with strong plasma optical emission and crater generation, led to equal kinetic energies of ions instead of equal ion momenta.204 While this study did not focus on the particle formation aspects, it was important in proving mechanistic insights into different ablation regimes for ultrafast laser ablation.
Material removal and particle formation in laser ablation is largely determined by an energy balance. The amount and location of energy deposition from the laser, and the rate and pathways of energy dissipation determine the material removal rate. Bulgakova et al. developed a refined thermal model of nanosecond pulsed laser ablation synthesis of niobium, graphite, YBa2C3O7 superconductor, and silicon NPs that could be evaluated for wide variety of laser fluences and included a comprehensive calculation of the solid-melt interface. The experimental data on the ablated mass was the key validation point for the model, in which plasma absorption was an important factor at high laser fluence. Notably, heat conduction in the target was considerable at low laser fluences. Moreover, they found that the laser fluence played a critical role in determining the maximum melt depth for conditions below the ablation threshold. Furthermore, they estimated the possible thermodynamic critical temperatures (related to the maximum temperature reached before the transition from normal ablation to phase explosion) using the refined thermal model.244
Lutey developed a precise laser ablation model based upon one-dimensional heat flow with consideration of temperature dependent material properties and thorough optical propagation. He employed established matrix methods for electromagnetic plane wave propagation in multi-layered media for the numerical optical calculation for the absorption of the laser beam in the target. To consider the effects of reflection from the dielectric-metal interface and the incident laser beam absorption by the products of ablation, he defined a simulated shielding coefficient which allowed him to obtain good agreement between the model predictions and experiments for the ablation of aluminum, in terms of both ablation threshold and melted depth. In addition, he studied the influences of pulse duration, laser wavelength, and target thickness on the aluminum ablation threshold. He found that increasing the pulse duration and the laser wavelength raised the phase explosion threshold, while increasing the sample thickness below 1.5 μm lowered the threshold. Thermal energy transport, optical absorption, and thermal saturation were responsible for the effects of pulse duration, laser wavelength, and sample thickness, respectively. Based on these observations, he concluded that both sample and laser characteristics affected the threshold, due to the significant role of thermal energy penetration into the target during the ablation process.245
The instantaneous material removal from the target surface during pulsed laser ablation continuously changes the position of the material boundary, which is a key challenge for modelling. Wang et al. developed a precise simulation model through finite element analysis of the pulsed laser ablation of aluminum over a wide range of laser fluences with explicit consideration of this boundary change. The finite element analysis was effective for modelling the strong coupling between shape evolution and thermal transport in this system. The simulation predicted both the steady change in the surface of the target at low laser fluences and the ablation depth resulting from phase explosion at high laser fluences. They predicted a threshold fluence of approximately 60 J cm−2 for the transition from target evaporation to phase explosion which was reasonably consistent with experimental data.246
Jiang et al. wrote a comprehensive review on electron dynamics control (EDC) for fabricating nanostructures using femtosecond pulsed laser ablation. In femtosecond ablation, electrons are absorbing the majority of the photon energy during this process, and then transfer this energy to ions on a picosecond timescale. Accordingly, localized transient electron dynamics should be controlled to truly control the ablation process. Their focus was on nanofabrication and micromachining using lasers, which differs from the laser ablation considered here, where particles formed from the ablated material are the desired product. Nonetheless, this study highlights the extent to which the nanofabrication research community has developed models and understanding of laser ablation processes that may be translatable for use by the particle synthesis community.247

2.2. Plasma processing methods
The energy required to drive evaporation or initiate chemical reaction to achieve a state of supersaturation that leads to particle nucleation can be delivered directly into the gas phase using a plasma. Solid, liquid, or vapor precursors can be injected into, or just downstream of, a plasma. In many cases, the droplets or particles injected must be very small to evaporate completely within a short residence time in the plasma or hot post-plasma gases. Plasma processing methods are conveniently divided into two general categories based on the local temperature equilibrium between electrons and ionized gas: thermal plasma processing, in which the electrons and ionized gas reach the same high temperature, and non-thermal plasma processing, in which the electrons have an effective temperature much higher than ions and neutral molecules in the plasma.

2.2.1. Thermal plasma processing. 
In thermal plasma, the electrons and ionized gas are at the same temperature. The rate of intermolecular collisions in the ionized gas is sufficient to maintain local temperature equilibrium. Thermal plasmas are produced by passing an electric current through an ionized gas at high enough pressure to maintain the needed collision rate for thermal equilibration. Two main types of thermal plasma discharges are DC arc jets and high frequency (microwave or radio frequency) induction plasmas. The range of operating temperature in these systems is broad: from 300 °C to the order of 104 °C. In DC arc jets, the source of current for plasma discharge is physical contact with a metallic electrode surface. Although the system is simple and inexpensive, the electrodes may contaminate the product. In high-frequency induction systems, there is no contact between the plasma and its power source. Accordingly, contamination from electrodes and reactor walls can be avoided. Plasma composition (most commonly with argon as the primary component) and frequency (between 200 kHz and 20 MHz) are controllable variables. Because the typical temperature can be on the order of 104 °C, materials with high melting points and solid powder feeds can vaporized.27,29,175,248,249 Use of inexpensive powder feed, rapid cooling rate, and demonstrated scale-up are advantages of thermal plasma processing. The major disadvantages in this method are difficulty of completely vaporizing solid reactants, a relatively complex process, agglomerated particles (often hard), and high energy requirements and costs.175 The temperature field in the plasma reactor, precursor concentration, type of plasma, plasma gas to precursor ratio, quenching rate, residence time, and extraction position are important factors governing production rate and final product properties and quality.250Table 5 provides a summary and Fig. 28 presents representative TEM images of materials synthesized using these techniques.
