2.2.1.1. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Although use of thermal plasma to generate fine materials began in the late 1950s251 and continued in the 1960s,252 1970s,253 and 1980s,254 based on the comprehensive review by Young and Pfender,255 we can consider Girshick and coworkers as the pioneers of thermal plasma processing for synthesizing non-agglomerated ultrafine NPs.256 They selected Fe for their study, to understand the nucleation mechanism, which was condensation of a supersaturated vapor followed by coagulation and sintering. Iron required the high-temperature provided by the plasma for evaporation, but could still be considered to undergo an evaporation-condensation process rather than more complex processes involving decomposition of molecular precursors and conversion into a product of different composition. A schematic of their plasma production system is presented in Fig. 29. They used a nominally 20 kW power supply to generate a RF current of 63 A at 2.9 MHz that led to inductive heating of the atmospheric-pressure plasma at a typical power of 4.5 kW. They introduced argon as the plasma gas at 28 lpm through a 45° oriented injection tube with 36 evenly spaced holes at the top of the plasma tube, which was advantageous to stabilize the plasma. They introduced 5 to 9 μm iron powder at a rate of 0.26 to 3.6 mg s−1 and delivered it in 3 to 4 lpm of argon. They reported that the location of the injection tube outlet with respect to the induction coil was important, because the characteristic recirculation vortex of the RF plasma was disrupted when the position was too high, but full vaporization of the precursor was not achieved if the position was too low. Accordingly, the best position for the injection tube outlet was at the midplane of the induction coil. Following the plasma discharge, they used a 64 mm inner diameter by ∼1 m long double-walled stainless-steel tube with a porous inner wall through which argon gas entered to cool the gas and reduce wall deposition of particles. Thus, the micron-size precursor particles first evaporated in the thermal plasma and then iron NPs nucleated homogenously as the gas was cooled in the porous tube downstream. This approach allowed them to produce spherical non-agglomerated iron NPs with average sizes in the range of 20 to 70 nm (increasing with increasing precursor feed rate). In addition, they developed a two-dimensional numerical model to predict the heating and evaporation rate of the injected precursor, nucleation and growth of the NPs, temperature and velocity distributions, and particle transport by convection, diffusion, and thermophoresis. They successfully predicted the average particle sizes, although the size distribution and the particle concentration profiles were not perfectly reproduced. This combination of thermal plasma synthesis with detailed product particle characterization using aerosol instrumentation and with aerosol dynamics modelling was a major advance in thermal plasma synthesis.256
 Fig. 29  Schematic of the set-up developed by Girshick et al. to synthesize Fe NPs through thermal plasma processing. Adapted from ref. 256 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1993. 
Mohai et al. synthesized zinc ferrite with an average size in the range of 10 to 30 nm using RF thermal plasma. They used two types of precursors: (a) ZnO and Fe2O3 powders mixed at a molar ratio of 1 to 2 and (b) ZnO and Fe(NO3)3·6H2O at a molar ratio of 1 to 6 that were first dissolved in water and then precipitated as hydroxides. They found that using co-precipitated precursors reduced the amount of unreacted compounds, which influenced the particle size. Furthermore, the partial pressure of oxygen was varied to facilitate the synthesis of zinc ferrous ferrites. They also observed a thin zinc oxide layer on the surface of the NPs.312
Son et al. synthesized Fe3O4 and NiFe2O4 nanocrystals using RF thermal plasma processing at a power of 50 kW and frequency of 3 MHz. The plasma and carrier gas were argon, the oxidation source was oxygen, and the sheath gas was a mixture of argon and hydrogen, which helped to confine precursors to the reaction zone. Using iron powder as the precursor for Fe3O4, they synthesized polygonal ferrite nanocrystals with an average size in the range of 20 to 30 nm. To synthesize nickel ferrite, they used three types of the precursors: (a) mixed nickel and iron powders at a Ni:Fe ratio of 1:2, (b) a NiFe permalloy powder with Fe powder added to achieve the same Ni:Fe ratio, and (c) a NiFe permalloy powder with a Ni:Fe ratio of 1:1. Going from precursor type (a) to precursor type (c), the nickel content in the synthesized NPs increased. Using type (c) precursor produced NPs with 40 to 48% nickel, exceeding the concentration in stoichiometric nickel ferrite (NiFe2O4), while the other two samples had Ni content lower than NiFe2O4. In addition, they investigated the magnetic properties of the synthesized nickel ferrite and found lower saturation magnetization for higher nickel content.304
Dash et al. employed thermal plasma processing to produce highly-pure MgAl2O4 NPs with uniform elemental distribution of Mg and Al using magnesite (MgCO3) and alumina (Al2O3) as precursors. They used argon as the plasma gas with a plasma current and voltage of 300 A and 50 V, respectively. They investigated the effect of changing the alumina concentration in the precursor mixture on the size of synthesized NPs and found that increasing the alumina concentration led to an increase in grain size.301
Bystrzejewski et al. used a 20 to 25 kW inductively coupled RF thermal plasma torch to produce carbon-encapsulated iron and iron carbide NPs of 5 to 80 nm diameter. They simultaneously injected iron powder and various liquid carbon sources into the thermal plasma torch. They employed two types of plasma gases, argon and a mixture of argon and oxygen. When using Ar/O2 mixture as the plasma gas, the liquid carbon source comprised only hydrocarbons such as hexane (C6H14) and toluene (C7H8) while both the hydrocarbons and alcohols, including methanol (CH4O), ethanol (C2H6O), and hexanol (C6H14O), were used when the plasma gas was Ar. They found that either using a mixture of argon and oxygen or using alcohols as the liquid carbon source with argon as the plasma gas substantially decreased amorphous carbon formation. They attributed removal of this undesired impurity to etching by oxygen. Moreover, they reported that using methanol or ethanol led to the complete removal of the amorphous carbon, with the maximum yield obtained using ethanol. Furthermore, they investigated the magnetic properties of the synthesized NPs and they found that they were ferromagnetic with saturation magnetizations of 64 to 96 emu g−1.298
2.2.1.2. Ceramic oxides. 
Lin et al. employed a novel DC thermal plasma reactor with an operating power of 70 kW at atmospheric pressure for thermal plasma production of zinc oxide NPs. They used zinc powder with an average size of 10 μm as the precursor with a plasma gas flow rate of 200 lpm and carrier gas flow rate of 10 lpm, followed by quenching with a flow of 3000 lpm to stop particle growth and sintering. Injecting nitrogen into the plasma and carrier gas led to nitrogen doping of the zinc oxide NPs. Zinc oxide has a band gap energy of about 3.2 eV and thus only absorbs UV light at wavelengths below 385 nm. Extension of ZnO absorbance to visible wavelengths is needed for practical photocatalytic applications, and nitrogen doping can provide this visible light absorbance. In an uncommon result for aerosol synthesis, they produced zinc oxide NPs with three different morphologies (Fig. 30): spherical, rod-like, and tetrapod-like. They found that the type of plasma gas played a substantial role in controlling the morphology. They used nitrogen as the plasma gas to produce spherical NPs while a 1:1 mixture of argon and nitrogen led to rod- and tetrapod-like NPs. A mixture of air and nitrogen was used as the quenching gas for synthesis of both spherical and tetrapod-like NPs, while air was the quench gas for the synthesis of rod-like NPs. Furthermore, they studied the photocatalytic activity of the NPs and found that the spherical NPs had better anti-microbial ability, while rod- and tetrapod- like NPs performed better for the photocatalytic degradation of methylene blue.306
 Fig. 30  TEM images of ZnO NPs with (a) tetrapod-like and (b) spherical morphologies. SEM images of ZnO NPs with (c) rod-like, (d) tetrapod-like, and (e) spherical morphologies. Adapted from ref. 306 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2005. 
Mishra et al. used a unique thermal plasma reactor for the synthesis of carbon-doped ZnO nanocrystals with an average particle size of 32 nm. A schematic of their synthesis reactor is exhibited in Fig. 31. Two vertically arranged graphite electrodes produced an extended arc plasma with the 15 kW DC power. Argon entered through a narrow hole in the upper electrode. Their thermal plasma reactor was unique in combining two different modes of precursor vaporization. The smaller particles in the precursor mixture were vaporized in-flight as in a non-transferred arc mode of operation. Subsequent chemical reactions initiated particle nucleation and growth. Larger particles that were not fully vaporized during transport through the plasma accumulated in a crucible where they effectively became part of the electrode and were vaporized in a transferred arc mode that typically uses one or both electrodes as the particle precursor. The precursor powder was a mixture of zinc oxide and graphite with a molar ratio of 1 to 8, which was injected into the thermal plasma at a rate of 1 g min−1. Additionally, they investigated the magnetic properties of the NPs and they found that carbon-doping increased the saturation magnetization of the zinc oxide NPs by a factor of 3.8. They observed zinc–carbon complexes that were surrounded by a zinc oxide matrix in carbon doped zinc oxide NPs and argued that generation of defects or the development of oxy-carbon clusters was the source of ferromagnetic behaviour in the doped NPs, even though ZnO itself is not ferromagnetic.309
 Fig. 31  Schematic of the DC extended arc inflight thermal plasma reactor developed by Mishra et al. to synthesize carbon-doped ZnO NPs. Adapted from ref. 309 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2013. 
Murali et al. employed thermal plasma processing to produce pristine zinc oxide and indium-doped zinc oxide NPs, both in the hexagonal wurtzite crystalline phase, in a single-step process. They used zinc nitrate hexahydrate (Zn(NO3)2·6H2O) as the zinc precursor and indium nitrate pentahydrate (In(NO3)3·5H2O) as the indium precursor. Argon (3.5 lpm) carried the precursors (0.5 g min−1) to the reaction zone in which the plasma arc was generated by a DC power supply with a constant power of 15 kW and argon (39 lpm) as the primary plasma gas. They synthesized doped zinc oxide NPs with two different doping levels, 4 at% and 8 at%, and studied the effect of doping on the photocatalytic activity for photodegradation of methylene blue under UV irradiation. They found that increasing the indium doping level led to a redshift in the UV-vis absorbance spectrum and that NPs with 4 at% indium doping provided superior photocatalytic activity compared to undoped or 8 at%-doped NPs. They attributed the good performance to a low recombination rate for photogenerated charge carriers and enhanced separation efficiency of electrons and holes.307
2.2.1.3. Semiconductors. 
Rao et al. developed a plasma expansion process using a DC arc for the synthesis of monodispersed silicon, carbon, and silicon carbide NPs with potential applications in photonics, electronics, engines, turbines, and machine tools. Compared to conventional plasma methods that used a large flow of cold dilution gas to quench the process, expansion through a converging nozzle provided superior control over the quenching process. A schematic of this DC torch and nozzle configuration is shown in Fig. 32. Their plasma reactor was a water-cooled vacuum chamber including a reactant injection system (25 mm long by 15 mm diameter, placed immediately downstream of the nozzle), a plasma torch (Miller SG-1B plasma gun), and a converging nozzle (50 mm long with an inlet inner diameter of 10 mm, outlet inner diameter of 5 mm placed on the top flange of the chamber). They used silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4) as the silicon precursor with methane (CH4) as the carbon source, delivered through separate inlets, and Ar and H2 as the plasma gases. After precursor injection, the gas immediately entered the converging nozzle, through which it expanded into a vacuum chamber maintained at 400 Torr. Notably, they measured the temperatures using thermocouples at the inlet and outlet of each component of the reactor to quantify heat losses from the plasma to each component. The arc current, arc voltage, nozzle inlet pressure, nozzle outlet pressure, silicon source flow rate, carbon source flow rate, argon flow rate, and hydrogen flow rate were kept at 200 A, 18–25 V for Ar and 38–65 V for H2, 500 Torr, 400 Torr, 50 sccm, 200 sccm, 30 lpm, and 7.5 lpm, respectively. With these conditions, they successfully synthesized silicon and silicon carbide NPs with average diameters of about 8.5 nm and 90% of the particles below 16 nm in diameter. Carbon particles were even smaller, with 90% below 15 nm in diameter. Based on measured size distributions along with modelling results, they suggested that nucleation of silicon clusters from supersaturated silicon vapor was the initial step in the production of silicon carbide, as well as silicon, NPs. In the case of silicon carbide, this initial nucleation was followed by surface reactions with carbon-containing species.265
 Fig. 32  Schematic of the plasma torch and nozzle developed by Rao et al. to synthesize Si, C, and SiC NPs. Adapted from ref. 265 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1995. 
Iijima and Ichihashi produced 1 nm-diameter SWCNT by spark discharge generation. Their anode was a simple 10 mm diameter graphitic carbon rod, while the cathode (bottom electrode in vertical arrangement) was a 20 mm diameter carbon rod with a shallow dimple to hold a small piece of iron. The background gas was 20% methane and 80% Ar. A DC current of 200 A at 20 V created the carbon arc discharge between the electrodes. The evaporation of the small piece of iron inside the cathode electrode led to the simultaneous formation of iron carbide above the cathode and nucleation of carbon soot particles by evaporation of the carbon cathode as well as from decomposition of methane. They found that it was impossible to synthesize SWCNT when any of the three components of Ar, iron, and methane was omitted. They suggested that the iron particles played an essential role in production of SWCNT even though there was no evidence of iron particles at the tubule tips.269 Iijima and Ichihashi's synthesis method produced SWCNT at a low yield. Journet et al. developed techniques for extending this method to large-scale production of SWCNT. They filled the cathode with a mixture of a metallic catalyst and graphite powder instead of filling it with iron. The catalysts were bimetallic mixtures including Ni–Co, Ni–Y, and Co–Y in different atomic percentages. They used a current of 100 A at 30 V to produce the spark between the electrodes in a He atmosphere. The highest yield was achieved with a catalyst mixture of 4.2% Ni and 1% Y. They produced SWCNT with diameters as low as around 1.4 nm at yields from 70 to 90%. SEM (scanning electron microscopy) and HRTEM (high-resolution transmission electron microscopy) images of these materials are shown in Fig. 33.270 Bethune et al. used almost the same strategy to synthesize SWCNTs, but they used cobalt as the catalyst which led to the formation of non-graphitic single-atomic-layer walls with a diameter of around 1.2 nm.271
 Fig. 33  (a) HRTEM and (b) SEM images of SWCNTs produced by Journet et al. Adapted from ref. 270 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1997. 
Li and Ishigaki employed thermal plasma processing to produce titania nanocrystals with an average size in the range of 10 to 50 nm. They used titanium carbide (TiC) powder microparticles as the titanium precursor. A schematic of their reactor is shown in Fig. 34. The plasma reactor consisted of a stainless-steel cylinder with an inner diameter of 250 mm with a conical exit section. It was held at a pressure of 53 to 80 kPa. A 2 MHz induction-plasma torch was employed at powers from 25 to 40 kW. The titanium precursor was injected at 1.6 to 1.8 g min−1 from the top of the plasma torch into the center of the plasma region, carried by Ar or an Ar/CO2 mixture at flow rates from 2.5 to 10 lpm. They used three different sheath gas mixtures: Ar–O2, Ar–He, and Ar–H2. In some cases, O2 was also added downstream of the plasma torch. When no O2 was added, CO2 in the carrier gas provided oxygen to the system. When the Ar–O2 mixture was used as sheath gas, O2 not only served as the oxidizing agent to produce titania NPs, but also increased the thermal conductivity of the gas mixture, enhancing heat transfer. The oxygen flow rate was variable from 2.5 to 20 lpm in the 90 lpm total gas flow to the system. This process produced a mixture of hexagonal rutile and spherical anatase crystals with an average size of 20 to 50 nm. They found that changing the oxygen flow rate at fixed total flow rate of the gaseous mixture led to a change in the anatase content from 20 to 75 wt%. In addition, they reported that rapid cooling had greater effect than introducing oxygen into the coagulated particles to synthesize oxygen-deficient rutile. Also, they adjusted the flow rate of the carrier gas to control the phase composition and they could synthesize NPs with above 90 wt% rutile content by increasing the flow rate of the carrier gas. For experiments with Ar–He sheath gas, O2 was injected at the tail part of the Ar–He plasma. Addition of He dramatically increased thermal conductivity of the plasma. This produced a dramatic decrease in the size of titania clusters and change in phase composition due to the very short oxidizing reaction time at the tail of the plasma. They concluded that cluster size could be controlled precisely by introducing the oxygen stream in various part of the plasma. For the Ar–H2–CO2 plasma, CO2 at a flow rate of 0.5 lpm mixed with 4.5 or 9.5 lpm Ar served as the carrier gas, while H2 was added to the sheath gas. Among the plasma gas mixtures tested, the mixture of Ar and H2 had the highest thermal conductivity. At temperatures above 5000 K, decomposition of carbon dioxide produced oxygen and carbon monoxide. This composition required a higher plasma operating power of 40 kW due to higher heat dissipation by this plasma. This plasma produced pure rutile titania NPs. Overall, this work showed that a thermal plasma oxidation process can effectively control both the size and the crystalline phase content of titania nanocrystals.290
 Fig. 34  Schematic of the plasma reactor developed by Li and Ishigaki to synthesize TiO2 NPs through thermal plasma processing. Adapted from ref. 290 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2004. 
Padmanabhan et al. investigated the photocatalytic activity of titania NPs, mostly below 20 nm diameter, synthesized using a reactive thermal plasma method. They used TiH2 powder as the precursor for titanium and injected it in the Ar thermal plasma jet along with air as a source of oxygen. The thermal plasma synthesized titania NPs were a mixture of anatase and rutile, but the anatase was converted to rutile by thermal treatment above 600 °C. The synthesized titania NPs exhibited promising results for the photocatalytic degradation of methylene blue.291
Lee et al. used a DC plasma jet to synthesize aluminium-doped titania NPs with high quality and an average size of 10 to 50 nm. A schematic of their reactor is presented in Fig. 35. It included a DC generator with an input power of 8 kW and current of 300 A as the source of energy, a reaction tube, two separate injection ports for the host and dopant precursors, a quench chamber, a filter, and a vacuum system to adjust the pressure to around 100 kPa. Ar at 10 lpm was used as the plasma gas. Titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) was introduced into the plasma region in Ar carrier gas while aluminum chloride (AlCl3) was dissolved in methanol and introduced by a metering pump. The concentration of AlCl3 in methanol was varied, using a constant flow rate of 0.3 sccm to produce Al-doped titania NPs with Al content of 1 to 8 wt%. In addition, they introduced oxygen at a flow rate of 1.5 lpm to ensure oxidation. They found that increasing the amount of dopant decreased the size of the NPs. Moreover, they investigated the optical properties of the synthesized NPs and found that increased doping shifted the absorption band edge from the UV region to the visible region.295
 Fig. 35  Schematic of the plasma reactor developed by Lee et al. to synthesize Al-doped TiO2 NPs. Adapted from ref. 295 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2004. 
Wan et al. used atmospheric pressure thermal plasma processing to synthesize nano-silicon/graphene composites in a one-step process. They used silicon powders with an average size of 6.5 μm and graphite power with a particle size of 200 mesh as the raw materials, and mutually ground them for 10 minutes. After grinding, the powder was delivered at 1 g min−1 in argon carrier gas into the thermal plasma torch. Argon also served as the plasma-forming gas. They used a DC power supply with a maximum power of 70 kW to generate the plasma. They asserted that the large graphene sheets with fewer than 5 layers were produced first, and then silicon NPs with an average size of 28 nm nucleated and grew on their surfaces. They found that the bonding between silicon and carbon played a key role in the synthesis of these nanocomposites. Based on this finding, they proposed that silicon vapor played a role as an active gas that prevented graphene from forming closed (fullerenic) structures. These nanocomposites could be used in lithium-ion batteries due to their ability to accommodate the volume changes of silicon during battery cycling.283
2.2.1.4. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Fabry et al. synthesized carbon black by hydrocarbon cracking in a pilot scale (100 kW) plasma reactor. A 100 kW 3-phase AC generator drove the plasma torch with three 13 mm diameter electrodes to produce a plasma flow in a large cylindrical graphite reaction chamber with a height of 2 m and inner diameter of 28 cm inside a water-cooled stainless-steel vessel with an inner diameter of 40 cm. The nominal power of the AC generator was 175 kVA with a maximum current of 266 A, short circuit voltage of 760 V, and frequency of 50 Hz. They used ethylene (C2H4) as the carbon precursor and N2, Ar, or a 0.95:0.05 mixture of Ar:H2 as the plasma gas. They found that the plasma gas dramatically affected arc voltage, arc current, and plasma enthalpy so that the values for N2, Ar, and a mixture of Ar and H2 were 195, 55, and 71 V; 199, 2031, and 198 A; and 20.2, 2.8, and 5.2 kW h Nm−3, respectively. Although they observed three populations of particles, the particles mostly had a spherical morphology with an average size in the range of 150 to 200 nm. They suggested that the particle size and structure resulted from internal mixing phenomena within the reactor. Recirculation of particles through the hot plasma region led to graphitization of initially amorphous carbon, while extended residence time associated with recirculation allowed particle growth.266 This group used the same set-up for synthesis of two different morphologies of carbon nanomaterials: carbon nanotubes with a “stacked-cup” structure and carbon nano-necklace structures formed by the repetition of multi-walled carbon spheres in a bell-like structure with encapsulated metal particles. These two different morphologies are exhibited in Fig. 36. In this case, carbon black coated with 3% Ni and Co was injected into the thermal plasma. After total vaporization in the plasma, catalyst (Ni and Co) particles nucleated first, then carbon structures grew over them before the plasma gas and the precursor mixture were quenched and the final products were extracted. The key advantage of this process compared to the conventional arc and laser processes was that the flow rate of solid carbon was not limited by the rate of electrode erosion and could be independently controlled along with the flow rates of plasma gas and metal catalysts. They used helium and nitrogen as the plasma gases and they found that the nature of the plasma gas affected the morphology of the NPs. The temperature of the reactor (wall temperature) was in the range of 1000 to 1300 °C (the metal catalyst was in the solid state in this range) using helium as the plasma gas, which led to carbon nanotubes while it was in the range of 1700 to 2400 °C (the metal catalyst was liquid in this range) using nitrogen as the plasma gas, which led to carbon nano-necklaces. This showed that the temperature field in the plasma reactor played a substantial role in the growth of carbon nanotubes with different morphologies.250
 Fig. 36  (a and b) SEM images of carbon black nanomaterials with (a and c) stacked-cup and (b and d) nano-necklace morphologies. Adapted from ref. 250 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2004. 
Kim et al. synthesized high-quality SWCNT at a high production rate of around 100 g h−1 by thermal plasma processing, demonstrating potential for commercial applications. Their set-up (Fig. 37a) included three parts: (a) a reaction chamber with a powder feeder through which carbon black of three different grades (M120, M280, and M880) with binary and ternary catalysts such as Ni/Co (0.6/0.6 at%), Ni/Co/CeO2 (0.6/0.6/0.2 at%), and Ni/Co/Y2O3 (0.6/0.6/0.4 at%), were delivered in Ar to the center of a plasma torch driven by a 60 kW power supply at 3 MHz. Delivery to the center of the plasma torch exposes the precursors to much higher temperature than that of the plasma jet (about 9000 K instead of below 7000 K). The plasma gas was argon, although other inert gases like He or hydrocarbon species like C2H2 and CH4 could also be used. A laminar flow of sheath gas was used to confine the plasma. To investigate the effect of the thermodynamic properties of the plasma gas mixture on the synthesis process, they employed two types of sheath gas, He and Ar/He mixture. Upon leaving the plasma, gases entered a double-walled cylindrical tube containing interchangeable graphite inserts that facilitated flexible control of cooling rate and temperature gradient, which play a key role in the synthesis of SWCNT. This was followed by a quenching chamber including two double-walled cylindrical sections for rapid cooling by the injection of additional inert gas and to initiate oxidation reactions to remove impurities using various oxidizing agents. Finally, a particle collection system including a filtration unit with three porous ceramic elements was used to collect the solid products and a heat exchanger cooled the residual gases before they were exhausted through a vacuum pump. With this large-scale process, they produced carbon soot containing ∼40 wt% SWCNT like those shown in Fig. 37b–d. The plasma composition, grade of carbon black precursor, and type of catalyst each affected the quality and the purity of the SWCNT. Although the quality of the SWCNTs was not as high as that of products synthesized by laser ablation, the thermal plasma method allows much higher SWCNT production rates.272
 Fig. 37  (a) Schematic of the synthesis reactor employed by Kim et al. to synthesize SWCNTs through thermal plasma processing. (b) SEM image of SWCNTs, (c) TEM image of SWCNTs, and (d) TEM image of SWCNTs at higher magnification. Adapted from ref. 272 with permission from IOP Publishing, Copyright 2017. 
2.2.1.5. High-strength nanomaterials. 
Ishigaki and coworkers also synthesized TaC NPs by thermal plasma processing. They atomized liquid tantalum ethoxide (Ta(OC2H5)5) and delivered it as small droplets directly into a thermal plasma. They employed a 40 kW, 2 MHz RF power supply with Ar as the plasma gas. Both the tantalum ethoxide precursor and TaC NPs are air sensitive and required special handling. They introduced a 0.95:0.05 Ar:O2 mixture to the product NPs for controlled oxidation and thereafter collected the particles in air from the filter and the reactor walls. They found that adding hydrogen to argon as the sheath gas enhanced the quality of the NPs by removing carbon from the reaction mixture through generation of hydrocarbons, but that excess hydrogen could suppress the synthesis of TaC. Accordingly, they reported that the optimum molar ratio of hydrogen to the tantalum precursor was about 25 which produced powder with 97.4 wt% TaC. Furthermore, they introduced Ar or He at a flow rate of 20 lpm at the tail of the plasma torch and found that mean NP size decreased from 12.3 to 7.8 nm upon injecting He.318
Ryu et al. synthesized tungsten NPs by injecting ammonium paratungstate (H40N10O41W12·xH2O) powder into a thermal plasma in a mixture of argon and hydrogen carrier gas. They investigated the effect of operating parameters inducing torch power (10 to 20 kW), argon plasma gas flow (29 to 57 lpm), and hydrogen concentration in the carrier gas (from no hydrogen to above the stoichiometric content required to reduce WO3 to W) on the particle size and properties. Torch power and plasma gas flow rate did not influence the grain size of W below 25 nm while increasing the hydrogen concentration in the carrier gas decreased the size of the NPs in that range. Increasing the hydrogen concentration above the stoichiometric amount led to nearly complete reduction of WO3. Without any H2, WO3 was the main product. Accordingly, they synthesized spherical tungsten NPs with diameters below 50 nm. They concluded that rapid evaporation of the precursor at the high plasma temperature and subsequent fast quenching to yield tungsten NPs in a single-step process, make this technique superior to conventional multi-step processes for this purpose.320
Oh et al. employed RF thermal plasma processing to produce W–Cu nanocomposites with varied copper content (5, 10, and 20 wt%). The precursors were tungsten oxide (WO3) and copper oxide (CuO), which were mixed to produce nanocomposites with the same Cu content as the precursor. They used oxide precursors because of their much lower boiling points compared to W and Cu, which facilitated vaporization at a power of 30 kW. Ar was used as plasma, sheath, and carrier gas while N2 served as the quench gas. To reduce the precursor mixture, hydrogen was added to the sheath gas at an Ar to H2 ratio of 6 to 1. The product composition matching that of the precursor mixture demonstrated the full evaporation and efficient reduction provided by the thermal plasma process.325
Tong et al. synthesized non-stoichiometric titanium carbide below 100 nm in diameter using thermal plasma processing. The precursor was titanium powder delivered to an Ar plasma in a mixture of argon and methane, which served as the carbon source. They studied the effects of the feed composition, flow rate, and plasma power and found that to avoid titanium and carbon impurities in the final products, the feed mixture should be equimolar, with a feed rate below 5 g min−1, at maximum power.288
Heo et al. used a two-step process involving synthesis of TiC–graphene nanocomposites by thermal plasma processing and subsequent conversion of titanium carbide to titania using a dielectric barrier discharge (DBD) and heat treatment, to remove impurities and produce highly-pure TiO2–graphene nanocomposites with TiO2 particles below 50 nm in diameter and 5 to 10 graphene layers. The plasma reactor comprised a non-transferred DC plasma torch with 30 kW DC power supply, a reaction tube, and a quench chamber. Titanium tetraisopropoxide was delivered through a bubbler at an optimum temperature of 473 K for producing TiC–graphene nanocomposites. Fig. 38 illustrates the synthesis mechanism and the morphology of TiO2–graphene nanocomposites. This study demonstrated a cost- and time-efficient synthesis method that could potentially also produce other metal oxide–carbon nanocomposites.293
 Fig. 38  (a) Schematic of synthesis mechanism and (b) TEM image of TiO2–graphene nanocomposites synthesized by Heo et al. Adapted from ref. 293 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2019. 
Fathalizadeh et al. synthesized boron nitride of varying morphologies using a plasma torch driven by a 60 kW RF power supply at 7 MHz. The plasma and carrier gas were nitrogen. They used hexagonal boron nitride powder or amorphous boron powder as the precursor and found that the latter gave higher conversion to BN NPs. At atmospheric pressure of nitrogen, boron nitride nanotubes (BNNTs) formed and substantial boron remained unreacted. In that case, boron nitrides formed with a “nanococoon” morphology, comprising a shell of boron nitride around boron particles. With increasing nitrogen pressure, the morphology changed from nanococoon to nanotube so that between 2 and 3 atm the amount of the nanocoons diminished, and above 3 atm, nanococoons could barely be observed. The different morphologies are shown in Fig. 39. Based on these observations, they proposed that after injection of the boron precursor into the thermal plasma and subsequent melting, its surface reacts with nitrogen within and at the outlet of the plasma plume. The collision rate between the N2 atoms and B droplets increases with increasing density of the plasma plume and, as a result, the reaction rate is accelerated and BNNTs form. They produced BNNTs at 35 g h−1 which was 300 times greater than that achieved by laser ablation.259
 Fig. 39  TEM images of (a) BNNTs and (b) BN with a “nanococoon” morphology synthesized by Fathalizadeh et al. through thermal plasma processing. Adapted from ref. 259 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2014. 
Mishra et al. used thermal plasma processing and subsequent acid leaching to prepare high-purity boron carbide NPs. They mixed boric acid (B(OH)3) with activated carbon at mass ratios of 4 and 5 in 5% dextrin, then granulated and dried it and injected the powder into an Ar plasma. After the synthesis of the initial NPs, they used acid leaching to remove impurities such as amorphous carbon and B2O3. In addition, they investigated the temperature-dependent thermal conductivity of the final products at temperatures up to 1000 °C.257
Oh et al. developed a novel triple DC thermal plasma reactor and produced tungsten carbide NPs at atmospheric pressure. Their set-up (Fig. 40) incorporated three plasma torches, three DC power supplies, seven water-cooled reactor segments, and a cyclone filter. Argon and nitrogen were mixed at a flow rate of 14 lpm each as the plasma gas. Tungsten powder with a typical size of 1 μm was used as the tungsten source, which they combined with three different carbon sources: MWCNT with a diameter of 10 nm, amorphous carbon with a size of 6 μm, and methane. The tungsten and carbon sources were mixed at a molar ratio of 1 to 1.5 and injected at a rate of about 500 mg min−1 through a tube placed between the torches, and into the high temperature region at the intersection of the three plasma torches, using argon as carrier gas. The input power for each torch was 9 to 10 kW. The raw materials evaporated in the high temperature zone, and subsequent condensation produced NPs below 100 nm in diameter. They studied the effect of carbon source on the morphology and crystal structure of NPs and found that W2C was the main product for all the carbon sources, although WC and WC1−x were also present. Using MWCNT as the carbon source produced tungsten carbide/MWCNT nanocomposites in addition to spherical tungsten carbide NPs. Synthesis using methane led to the formation of only spherical tungsten carbide NPs, while rod-shaped tungsten carbide NPs were produced along with the spherical ones when amorphous carbon was used. Moreover, they investigated the effect of W/CH4 composition ratio and found that increasing this ratio shifted the product from W2C to WC1−x.323 This group also used their triple DC thermal plasma configuration to generate boron nitride nanotubes (BNNTs). The precursor was hexagonal boron nitride powder with a size of 1 μm. The plasma gas comprised 4 lpm argon and 8 lpm nitrogen, and hydrogen was introduced as a reactant gas. Each torch was run at a power of 6.9 to 7.5 kW, at a 100 A constant current. The precursor was delivered from the top of the triple torch into the plasma, at a rate of 0.5 to 0.6 g min−1, in 5 lpm argon. This produced multi-walled (maximum 5 walls) BNNTs with an average diameter of around 7 nm and high crystallinity. Using the triple torch configuration, the plasma flame length reached 30 cm. Accordingly, the high temperature region was larger than that of conventional configurations. The triple torch configuration allowed direct injection of the precursors into the core of the plasma, increasing the BNNT productivity by avoiding precursor flow into the edges of the plasma flame as occurs in a single torch configuration, because of the high velocity and viscosity of the central plasma flame. In addition, they employed numerical analysis of flow in the system and thermodynamic equilibrium calculations to elucidate the role of hydrogen in the nanotubes’ growth. They found that adding hydrogen led to the formation of NH, NH2, and N2H2, which delayed the recombination of nitrogen into N2. B–H species were also generated, leading to B–N–H intermediates. The dehydrogenation of these intermediates with early boron nucleation in the quenching process led to the growth of BNNTs. In addition, the high thermal conductivity of hydrogen expanded the high temperature and high enthalpy zones that, along with the expansion of the vortex region, provided longer residence times of the B–N–H intermediates in the reaction zone. Ultimately, they produced 12.6 g h−1 of BNNTs at a power of 21.4 kW, which shows promise for economical production of BNNTs at industrial scale.260
 Fig. 40  Schematic of (a) the triple DC thermal plasma reactor along with images of (b) the upper part of triple plasma system and torch and (c) merged triple thermal plasma jet flame developed by Oh et al. to synthesize tungsten carbide NPs. Reproduced from ref. 260 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
2.2.1.6. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Girshick et al. developed one of the first realistic models for the nucleation and growth of NPs in thermal plasma processing. First, they derived a nondimensional form of the aerosol general dynamic equation, with appropriate simplifying assumptions, and solved it numerically. A key assumption was that in such a thermal plasma, the nucleated particles can be treated as electrically neutral, because most particle growth occurs after the gas has cooled to a temperature where ionization fractions, at thermal equilibrium, are small. They then used experimental data for the thermal plasma processing of Fe NPs to produce a dimensional form of the equation and its predictions. They also applied their model to SiC NPs. Their model predicted that reactant concentration and cooling rate (which determined the effective residence time for particle nucleation and growth) were the key factors controlling primary particle size, with increasing reactant concentration and decreasing cooling rate leading to larger primary particle size. They also noted that thermophoresis, electric charge effects, London-van der Waals forces, and diffusion across concentration gradients could all be additional factors affecting the collision rate in thermal plasma processing, but were not accounted for in their simplified model.327 More recent studies have gone on to relax many of these assumptions. Girshick and Chiu performed another modelling study for the thermal plasma processing of iron NPs that relaxed the assumption of irreversible coagulation of all clusters, down to single atoms, but instead included evaporation from small clusters. They explicitly incorporated evaporation and coagulation terms in a set of cluster-balance rate equations for these small clusters. They retained other simplifying assumptions such as one-dimensional flow field, completion of any relevant chemistry prior to homogeneous nucleation, and the condensation of a single species. This provided a more realistic description of the homogeneous nucleation event, which was found to be the main factor influencing the final size distribution of primary particles. The homogeneous nucleation occurs very quickly in thermal plasma processing, which leads to abrupt generation of stable clusters and particles, followed by Brownian coagulation. Furthermore, they found that the number concentration of the NPs, in addition to the size of NPs, is strongly dependent upon the cooling rate in the reaction zone and concentration of the reactants. Again, increasing precursor concentration and decreasing cooling rate led to production of fewer, but larger NPs.328 While these overall trends mirror intuitive, qualitative expectations, these modelling efforts represent a key step toward more quantitative understanding of the magnitude of each effect.
Ananthapadmanabhan et al. employed thermal plasma processing of aluminium metal in an oxygen atmosphere to synthesize γ-Al2O3 rather than more stable α-Al2O3 NPs, which was possible because of the highly-non-equilibrium nature of the process. Indeed, the high temperature of the reaction zone along with the highly-exothermic nature of the oxidation reaction led to the breakdown of the alumina NPs into aluminium and oxygen species which recombined in the cooler zones to generate γ-Al2O3 NPs with diameters of up to 30 nm. For sufficiently small Al precursor particles, complete oxidation and evaporation led to production of only nanosized γ-Al2O3 by gas-to-particle routes, but for larger precursor particles or lower plasma power, evaporation was incomplete. To understand this effect, they developed a numerical one-dimensional model of aluminum particle melting, oxidation, and evaporation that included the oxidation enthalpy of reaction in the energy balance, along with enthalpies of phase transitions and a phenomenological model of heat transfer to the particles. This model provided fair agreement with experimental observations of complete vs. incomplete conversion of aluminum precursor microparticles into γ-Al2O3 NPs.329 Note that this modelling effort focuses on an entirely different part of the process compared to that of Girshick and co-workers discussed previously. When solid feed particles are large or form a thermally-stable phase (like alumina), achieving complete precursor evaporation can be a significant barrier. In other cases, like those considered by Girshick, complete precursor dissociation into atoms can safely be assumed. Modelling of systems where these processes overlap, i.e. where new particles nucleate before all precursors have evaporated or dissociated, is an even greater challenge.
Shigeta and Watanabe developed a numerical growth model for thermal plasma processing of binary alloy NPs that considered the two-dimensional particle size and composition distribution of the product particles. The claimed advantages of their model included its simplicity and associated straightforward numerical solution algorithm. They discretized the particle size and composition distribution with 41 uniformly-spaced compositions and 54 logarithmically-spaced sizes. They took the smallest particle size to be a cluster of 10 atoms. Mass-conserving algorithms were adopted for treating growth by condensation and coagulation, which transfer particles between the discrete size-composition nodes. They employed a binary homogeneous nucleation model based on the work of Wyslouzil and Wilemski.330 Their heterogeneous condensation model considered cluster-size- and composition-dependent vapor pressures of small molten droplets, but required assumptions for unknown activity coefficients in the liquid and accommodation coefficients for atom-droplet collisions due to lack of data. Their treatment of Brownian coagulation spanned the full range of Knudsen numbers. Primary particle growth was considered to be complete when a particle size- and composition-dependent freezing point temperature was reached. They investigated metal-silicon systems as case studies. Employing the two-dimensional model, they were effectively able to simulate the simultaneous and collective combined processes of binary homogeneous nucleation, binary heterogeneous cocondensation, and coagulation of the clusters to understand the growth mechanisms of binary molybdenum-silicon and titanium-silicon systems based upon particle size-composition distributions. Furthermore, they understood that the growth behaviors and mature states in the binary nanoalloy systems were significantly influenced by the saturation pressures of the materials.331 This group used the same modelling framework in conjunction with an experimental study of the synthesis of TiB2 NPs with an RF induction thermal plasma. They introduced the precursor mixture of titanium and boron powders into the thermal plasma to produce TiB2 NPs with average sizes ranging from 10 to 30 nm after immediate evaporation and subsequent quenching. Decreasing the feed rate of precursor mixture and increasing the ratio of boron to titanium resulted in ideal conditions for the formation of TiB2 due to enhancement of the evaporated and nucleated boron molar ratio in the thermal plasma. Moreover, they found based upon the agreement between the experimental and modelling results that a decrease in the feed rate along with a decrease in the titanium concentration in the precursor mixture decreased the size of NPs because of the increase in the number concentration of boron nuclei. They concluded that thermal plasma processing is an effective technique to synthesize compound NPs in which a large difference in the saturation pressure of the elements would otherwise cause severe difficulties in controlling the cocondensation process.332
Shigeta applied a computational model appropriate for thermal plasma driven turbulent flows and sharp NP concentration distribution gradients to develop a three-dimensional time-dependent growth-transport model for the synthesis of nanomaterials by thermal plasma processing. His large-eddy-simulation (LES) of the turbulent flow included Joule heating and the Lorentz force. The subgrid-scale models considered both laminar and turbulent states because of their co-existence across the wide range of density and transport properties encountered in thermal plasma processing. His particle nucleation and growth model considered only concentration and average particle size at a given point (in space and time) within the reactor. Thus, its predictions are spatiotemporal profiles of mean particle size and particle concentration (of spherical particles) along with temperature and velocity. With this approach, three-dimensional distributions of the average size and the number density of the NPs were obtained at a manageable computational cost. Silicon was considered as a case study. He found that multiscale vortices were created by an argon thermal plasma jet even far from the plasma torch outlet. Low-temperature thin vortex rings in the upstream region surrounded the high-temperature thicker vortex rings with a double-layer structure. As they moved downstream, the thinner vortex rings transformed into smaller vortices, while the thicker vortex rings deformed. Moreover, he observed that the NPs were formed at the edge of the thermal plasma and that regions with lower number concentration of the NPs contained larger NPs, consistent with coagulation as the main growth mechanism of the NPs. Furthermore, he found that a longer and straighter thermal plasma jet was produced by applying a uniform magnetic field in the axial direction, due to the Lorentz force and Joule heating. This field also prevented turbulent diffusion of silicon vapor and NPs through vortices, which produced larger NPs around the thermal plasma. Based on cross-correlation analysis, he found the temperature fluctuation at the upstream plasma edge was a key factor controlling the NP size and concentration distribution far from the plasma jet.333 Shigata applied the same general approach to DC thermal plasma processing as well, without the Lorenz force and Joule heating, again showing complex spatiotemporal distributions of particle formation.334,335 Overall, these studies paint a much more complex picture of particle production from thermal plasmas than had generally been assumed in earlier 1- and 2-dimensional steady-state models, like those discussed above. Nonetheless, the earlier models proved valuable in rationalizing and, to a limited extent, predicting trends in particle formation.
Kim et al. performed numerical modelling along with in situ optical emission spectroscopy to investigate the role of hydrogen in the thermal plasma processing of hexagonal boron nitride powder to synthesize BNNTs. They found that hydrogen affected both chemical and physical pathways to enhance the rapid growth of BNNTs. The modelling studies revealed that the high thermal conductivity of hydrogen facilitated complete vaporization and rapid cooling of the precursor, leading to earlier formation of nanosized boron aerosol droplets to grow BNNTs. They predicted quenching rates of around 105 K s−1 for the initial cooling to the boron nucleation temperature of around 4200 K. Moreover, the release of the heat of reaction of hydrogen atom recombination, starting at about 3800 K helped to maintain the temperature of the plasma gas above the boron solidification temperature of around 2300 K. This longer residence time between boron droplet nucleation and solidification extended the active growth zone of BNNTs further downstream in the plasma reactor. Based on optical emission spectroscopy results, they observed that using hydrogen favored the formation of BH and NH radicals, by reactions involving H radicals, which slowed the formation of N2 or N2+ species and stabilized B or N radicals, which facilitated the formation of BN.262 Although this study did not explicitly model the BNNT growth process, the insights provided by the simulation of the flow and temperature fields proved extremely valuable in understanding the role of hydrogen in promoting BNNT growth. This illustrates how thermofluid modelling, even without explicit aerosol dynamics modelling, can contribute to the understanding of aerosol synthesis processes.
Kim et al. performed a simulation and experimental study on thermal plasma processing using a triple DC plasma torch to synthesize NPs. They analyzed the thermal plasma characteristics inside the reactor for plasma-forming gas compositions of Ar, Ar–H2, and Ar–N2 with different carrier gases (Ar, He, and N2) for introducing the precursor powder along the reactor centerline between the three torches. They found that adding nitrogen or hydrogen, with relatively high thermal conductivity, to argon enhanced heat transfer which expanded the region of high temperature to better vaporize the precursor. Among these two highly-thermally-conductive gases, adding nitrogen resulted longer residence time in the zones with a temperature higher than the vaporization temperatures of the tungsten powder and carbon nanotube precursors, which explained observed improvement in the vaporization degree using Ar–N2 mixtures. Compared to H2, adding N2 provided both a higher initial temperature and faster cooling rate. Both cooled to about 3000 K at the same point in the reactor, and beyond that point the temperature with H2 was higher. The effect of the carrier gas was relatively small, simply because its flow rate was much smaller than the gas flow rates through the plasma torches. Nonetheless, He, with high thermal conductivity, produced the smallest cold region near the point of injection of the precursor powder in carrier gas, because of its high thermal conductivity. This could result in somewhat improved vaporization. In general, they concluded that tuning the plasma gas concentration can enable processing of target materials with appropriate residence time in a specific temperature zone, and this control of the thermal environment can improve process performance for achieving desired materials.336


2.2.2. Non-thermal plasma processing. 
In a non-thermal plasma, the temperature of electrons (typically 20000 to 50000 K) is much higher than that of the ionized gas (near ambient temperature). A non-thermal plasma can be generated by application of an electric field to a low-pressure gas in short pulses or at high frequency, such that electrons respond to the time-dependent field, but do not efficiently transfer energy to atoms and ions. Accordingly, electrons and the ionized gas do not achieve local temperature equilibrium.36,175,249,337 This method enables synthesis of high melting point materials, even though the process gas remains relatively cool, because nanomaterials are heated by surface reactions. This method, unlike other aerosol methods, can limit agglomeration because the synthesized nanomaterials typically acquire a negative charge and thus repel one another. This can lead to narrow size distribution and the synthesis of very small nanomaterials with sizes between 2 and 10 nm. The negatively charged walls of plasma reactors also confine the nanomaterials, reducing losses to the reactor walls. This method is suitable for efficient doping during nanomaterial growth because of the irreversible, far-from-equilibrium nature of particle growth. In addition, non-oxide nanomaterials can be synthesized by this process.36,175,337 The requirement of relatively complex equipment, use of expensive gaseous or volatile reactants, and limited demonstrations of scale-up are the most important disadvantages of this method.175Table 6 provides a summary and Fig. 41 presents representative TEM images of materials synthesized using this technique.
