2.2.2.1. Semiconductors. 
Although there were some reports on the synthesis of non-agglomerated freestanding nanocrystals though continuous-flow non-thermal plasma processing in the 1990's,338 we can consider Mangolini, Thimsen, and Kortshagen to be the pioneers of this field based upon their development of a process for synthesis of luminescent crystalline silicon NPs with average size below 10 nm in high yield.339 In their set-up (Fig. 42), they used a cylindrical quartz tube with an inner diameter of 1/4” and an outer diameter of 3/8”. A mixture of Ar as the plasma gas and silane (SiH4) as the source of silicon flowed through this tube at pressures of 0.186 to 1.86 kPa. A matching network provided an RF power of 200 W at a frequency of 27.12 MHz to two copper ring electrodes, with up to 30 W actually coupled to the plasma. They divided the plasma in their set-up to two regimes: (a) an intense plasma regime in the region between the electrodes and the vacuum chamber placed 1 inch downstream of the electrodes, and (b) a dimmer plasma regime between the electrodes and the gas inlet. By considering the length of 1 in as the effective plasma zone they derived residence times for flow rates from 12 to 60 sccm ranging from 1.4 to 7.0 ms. They flowed silane as the precursor at flow rates from 0.4 to 2.4 sccm. The electron impact dissociation of silane and subsequent fragment clustering produced crystalline silicon NPs. They collected the NPs on fine stainless-steel meshes placed 1 inch downstream from the plasma region, or on the walls of liquid-nitrogen-cooled quartz tubes. Their set-up provided a unique plasma environment that prevented agglomeration of ultrafine luminescent silicon NPs with average sizes in the range of 2 to 8 nm and a yield of 14 to 52 mg h−1. They pointed out that parallelizing the process could be the best route to scale-up of this approach.339
 Fig. 42  Schematic of the synthesis set-up employed by Mangolini et al. to synthesize Si NPs through non-thermal plasma processing. Adaptced from ref. 339 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005. 
Xia et al. used the same strategy to synthesize silicon nanocrystals and, through functionalization of them with triplet-accepting ligands, made them useful as non-toxic upconverting NPs for potential biological imaging or environmental sensing applications. This would differentiate them from previously reported toxic nanomaterials with comparable upconversion properties. The surface hydride (SiHx) groups present on the silicon nanocrystals synthesized by non-thermal plasma processing made them more amenable to thermal hydrosilylation and post functionalization compared to those synthesized by many other techniques. They reported spin-triplet excitation transfer from silicon to anthracene ligands over 15 ns by a single Dexter energy transfer step with an approximately 15% yield upon photoexcitation of the NPs. Pairing with 9,10-diphenylanthracene emitters led to green and red upconversion with 7.0% ± 0.9% efficiency, which would make them appropriate for biological applications upon incorporation into aqueous micelles.340
The Kortshagen group used almost the same set-up to produce free-standing germanium NPs with a narrow size distribution and controllable size in the range of 4 to 50 nm by varying the plasma region residence time from about 30 to 440 ms. RF power of 50 to 90 W at 13.56 MHz drove the plasma at a pressure of about 266 Pa. Germanium tetrachloride (GeCl4) was delivered from a bubbler kept at a pressure of 200 kPa in Ar carrier gas with a GeCl4:Ar ratio of 5:95. Ar was used as the plasma gas as well, allowing control of the total gas flow rate at fixed GeCl4 delivery rate. Hydrogen was added to the Ar stream to remove chlorine generated by decomposition of GeCl4 through formation of HCl. They investigated the effect of plasma power on the crystallinity of the NPs and found that fully amorphous to fully crystalline NPs could be produced by varying the plasma power, independent of other process parameters.372
Gresback et al. further investigated the crystallinity, size, oxidation, and photoluminescence of silicon nanocrystals synthesized by non-thermal plasma processing. A plasma generated at a higher RF frequency of 70 MHz was used to decompose the precursor. The gaseous precursor mixture included 2.4% silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4) as the silicon precursor, 5 to 70% hydrogen as a chlorine scavenger and Ar as the plasma gas. The total flow rate of the gaseous mixture was varied from 20 to 368 sccm. The total pressure of the plasma reactor ranged from 1 to 12 torr. They found that the input power and the reactor conditions were the main factors affecting the crystallinity of the synthesized NPs. Higher total pressures in the reactor and higher hydrogen concentrations were necessary for higher power in the system, which led to the synthesis of crystalline NPs. Otherwise, the NPs were amorphous. The size of NPs depended on the residence time of the gaseous mixture in the plasma and ranged from 3 to 15 nm. Hydrogen and chlorine were both present on the surface of the silicon nanocrystals which made them more susceptible to oxidation than silicon NPs terminated only by hydrogen. Moreover, the synthesized silicon nanocrystals exhibited photoluminescence with a peak emission wavelength tunable from around 650 to 900 nm and a profile that was almost Gaussian.346
Yasar-Inceoglu et al. compared the synthesis of NPs using silicon tetrachloride vs. silane and found that using the former led to the presence of chlorine at the surface and within the core part of the NPs, which decreased the stability of the crystalline structure of the NPs. Accordingly, a higher power was necessary to synthesize high-quality silicon nanocrystals from SiCl4. In addition, as reported earlier, the synthesis of NPs using SiCl4 as the precursor required an additional flow of hydrogen to scavenge the chlorine from the precursor and led to mixed termination of the NPs with hydrogen and chlorine which increased susceptibility to oxidation. Nonetheless, they reported the successful synthesis of the silicon NPs with a maximum size of 10 nm at production rates of up to 140 mg h−1 using SiCl4 as the precursor.347 They modified their system by adding a bubbler to deliver a second precursor and produced silicon-germanium alloy with controllable alloy composition, intended for use in thermoelectric applications. For this purpose, they used silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4) and germanium tetrachloride (GeCl4) as the precursors instead of silane (which is pyrophoric) and germane (which is extremely toxic) for the sake of both safety and cost. They employed an RF power supply with a frequency of 13.56 MHz which provided 175 W power to the reaction chamber at a pressure of 3.5 Torr. They synthesized alloy NPs with average sizes in the range of 5 to 10 nm and alloy compositions of Si1−xGex (0 < x < 0.3).386
Rowe and Kortshagen synthesized undoped silicon-germanium alloy and boron- and phosphorus- doped silicon-germanium alloy using the same non-thermal plasma processing approach. For undoped SiGe alloy, they simultaneously decomposed silane (SiH4) and germane (GeH4) in an Ar plasma with a nominal power of 130 W. They added diborane (B2H6) and phosphine (PH3) to obtain B- and P- doped SiGe alloy, respectively. Both n-type and p-type doped materials are needed for a complete thermoelectric device. They reported that the crystalline silicon-germanium NPs were truly alloyed with compositions of Si1−xGex (0.16 < x < 0.24). In addition, they found that the concentrations of each dopant in the gaseous precursor mixture could be used to estimate the incorporation efficiency of each dopant for a range of alloy compositions.357
Ding et al. investigated the effects of specific energy input (SEI, defined as plasma power divided by total gas flow) and the H2 to SiCl4 ratio on the size distribution, crystallinity, and yield of silicon NPs synthesized using a frequency of 70 MHz and total reactor pressure of 400 Pa. They found that atomic hydrogen generation has a significant effect on the dechlorination of SiCl4 at low temperatures. Increasing SEI increased product yield due to greater decomposition of SiCl4 with more energy per unit volume deposited into the gaseous mixture. An optimum SEI of 10 J cm−3 and H2 to SiCl4 ratio of 10 produced a maximum yield of 50 wt%. Further increasing SEI beyond the optimum value dropped the yield to 10%. The crystallinity and size distribution are mostly affected by the input power and residence time. At the optimum values of SEI and H2 to SiCl4 ratio for yield, the crystal fraction was approximately 1% and the size distribution was broad. At SEI greater than the optimum value for the yield, the crystallinity and monodispersity improved.348
In a promising application of aerosol-synthesized semiconductor nanocrystals, Meinardi et al. produced silicon quantum dots using non-thermal plasma processing and applied them in luminescent solar concentrators, where the low cost, efficient photoluminescence, earth abundance, and large Stokes shift of silicon are advantageous. They flowed a mixture of argon and helium-diluted silane through a tube reactor and injected hydrogen downstream of the plasma. They applied a power of 45 W at 13.56 MHz. They generated high-optical-quality waveguides by incorporating the silicon quantum dots into a mass polymerized matrix. Large effective spectral separation between the luminescence and optical absorption of the silicon quantum dots eliminated optical losses due to reabsorption of the guided luminescence. These allowed fabrication of luminescent solar concentrators with almost ideal optical performance and an efficiency of 2.85%, comparable to that of state-of-the-art semi-transparent luminescent solar concentrators.349
Coleman et al. synthesized hollow β-SiC using a two-step non-thermal plasma processing approach. Their plasma reactor (Fig. 43a) consisted of two stages, each with a non-thermal plasma generated by a 13.56 MHz power supply. The first stage employed a quartz tube with an outer diameter of 2.54 cm at a pressure of about 800 Pa, through which 100 sccm of a mixture of 1.37% silane in argon flowed. A copper ring served as the powered electrode placed 7 cm from the downstream grounded metal flange to which the non-thermal plasma was coupled. The second stage comprised another quartz tube with an outer diameter of 2.54 cm at a pressure of about 130 Pa in which a coil electrode produced the second non-thermal plasma with a length of around 25 cm. The two stages were separated by a metal flange and an orifice with a diameter of 1 mm at its center. Immediately downstream of this orifice, methane as the carbon source at a flow rate of 5 sccm was introduced into the reactor. The second non-thermal plasma was optically found to be coupled to the metal separating the two stages. Accordingly, the nucleated silicon NPs in the first stage were accelerated through the separating orifice and the carbonization occurred immediately upon introduction into the second reactor. The collection filter was a metal mesh located downstream of the plasma volume. The power in the first stage was fixed at 100 W and in the second stage was varied from 8 to 40 W. At a power of 12 W, diamond crystalline silicon was produced. The particles (Fig. 43b) were spherical with a broad size distribution from 10 to 30 nm because of the long residence time of 250 ms in the first stage, as determined by the length of the plasma and the flow velocity. At a power of 20 W, the synthesized nanocrystalline particles were a mixture of silicon and β-SiC. The majority of the particles were hollow, although some spherical particles could be observed (Fig. 43c). At a power of 30 W, more silicon particles were converted to silicon carbide. Because of the of the reduction in specific surface area and subsequent breakdown in the hollow spherical structure, the hollow particles formed were less regular and there were no spherical particles (Fig. 43d). At a power of 40 W, there was a complete conversion of silicon particles to silicon carbide. The hollow spherical particles collapsed and smaller particles with a size near 15 nm (smaller than the original silicon NPs) with irregular shapes formed (Fig. 43e). Overall, control of morphology from hollow to solid was possible by adjusting the process parameters. The spherical geometry of the initial silicon particles along with the expansion of the silicon lattice during carbonization produced hollow structures. In addition, they conducted a theoretical analysis of the diffusion kinetics and found that the fast transport of the carbon into the silicon particle and the subsequent conversion to the β-SiC phase was enabled by significant NP heating via interaction with the ionized gas.354
 Fig. 43  (a) Schematic of the reactor employed by Coleman et al. to synthesize hollow β-SiC NPs by non-thermal plasma processing. TEM images of the NPs achieved by applying the following electrical input powers to the second reactor: (b) 12 W, (c) 20 W, (d) 30 W, and (e) 40 W. Adapted from ref. 354 with permission from AIP Publishing, Copyright 2015. 
Hunter et al. synthesized Ge/Si nanocomposites with a core/shell structure in a two-part vertical reactor (Fig. 44a). Ring electrodes, generating a non-thermal plasma at 13.56 MHz and 50 W, were connected to the first part. To produce Ge quantum dot cores, Ar (25 sccm), H2 (20 sccm), and GeCl4 (partial pressure of 50 mTorr at total pressure of 2 Torr) were injected through the top of a quartz reaction tube. Up to 5% silane in argon was injected through a stainless-steel vacuum fitting with 16 individual 1 mm diameter holes spaced evenly around the second part of the reactor to form the coating. Shell growth took place around non-agglomerated particles due to the in-flight negative charging of the nanocrystals. Fig. 44b shows HAADF-STEM images and elemental mapping of a single Ge/Si nanocomposite with a core/shell structure. They could control the thickness of the coating by adjusting the flow of the Ar/SiH4 mixture in the range of 0 to 10 sccm while another flow of Ar was added to maintain a total pressure of 2 Torr, varying SiH4 concentration from 0 to 5%. This epitaxial growth of a Si shell of tuneable thickness around the Ge quantum dots was advantageous to control the germanium band structure.374
 Fig. 44  (a) Schematic of the reactor employed by Hunter et al. to synthesize Ge/Si core/shell quantum dots. (b) HAADF-STEM image and elemental mapping of a single Ge/Si core/shell quantum dot. Adapted from ref. 374 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2017. 
Barragan et al. employed non-thermal plasma processing to produce crystalline TiN NPs with an average size below 10 nm. They used titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) and ammonia (NH3) as precursors, with a 180 W RF power supply at 13.56 MHz. They controlled product composition by adjusting the precursor concentrations to tune the particle optical properties. The localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR) optical absorbance peak was tuned from 800 to 1000 nm. They found that NPs with lower nitrogen content were more readily oxidized because of their higher vacancy concentrations, although even NPs with the stoichiometric composition were prone to oxidation upon annealing in air at temperatures as low as 150 °C. Oxidation led to a red-shift in LSPR peak position.360 Kortshagen's group also employed non-thermal plasma processing to synthesize TiN NPs as promising candidates for photothermal therapy. They used a mixture of tetrakis (dimethylamino) titanium ([(CH3)2N]4Ti) and ammonia as precursors to produce NPs 6 to 8 nm in diameter. They investigated the effects of plasma power and ammonia flow rate on the LSPR and identified an optimal plasma power of 100 W and ammonia flow rate of 1.2 sccm that yielded a narrower LSPR absorbance peak than had been obtained in previous research.361
Kortshagen's group modified the system they first developed for silicon339 to produce metal sulfide NPs as the first non-oxide compounds produced by this approach. A schematic of their set-up is presented in Fig. 45. First, they added bubblers to deliver a sulfur source and volatile precursors containing Zn, Sn, or Cu. Although gaseous H2S is an attractive sulfur source, its toxicity severely limits its use; nontoxic precursors are preferable in this case. Hence, they used elemental sulfur, a solid with a melting point of 115 °C and substantial vapor pressure that allowed it to be delivered as a vapor from a vessel held at 120–140 °C. In contrast to the conventional bubbling method in which a carrier gas bubbles through the liquid, they flowed Ar over the liquid sulfur surface to avoid clogging of the gas feed lines. The precursors for ZnS, SnS, and Cu2S were diethyl-zinc(II) ((C2H5)2Zn), tetrakis(dimethylamido)-tin(IV) ([(C2H5)2N]4Sn), and hexafluoro-acetylacetonate-copper(I) (CuC5H6F6O2), respectively, each delivered by bubbling argon through a separate bubbler. The second modification was addition of optical access to the plasma emission for in situ optical emission spectroscopy, which required moving collection and pumping systems off the axis of the plasma tube. They found that increasing the flow rate of Ar to the plasma, at a constant precursor feed rate reduced the size of NPs due to the shorter residence time and lower precursor partial pressure. In addition, the sulfur delivery to the plasma, beyond a small minimum amount, did not affect the elemental compositions of the crystalline NPs at a constant vapor feed rate, while this was affected by the feed rate of the metal precursor at constant sulfur feed rate. The overall metal atomic fraction increased with increasing metal precursor vapor feed rate with formation of metal-rich crystal phases. Accordingly, the crystal phase and the elemental composition of the metal-sulfides could be controlled by adjusting the precursor vapor feed rate. They reported production rates in the range of 100 to 300 mg h−1 with their set-up and suggested it could be scaled-up for bulk synthesis through parallelization or using reaction tubes with larger diameters.365
 Fig. 45  Schematic of the plasma reactor employed by Thimsen et al. to synthesize metal sulfide NPs. Reproduced from ref. 365 with permission from IOP Publishing, Copyright 2015. 
Uner and Thimsen developed a reactor and process based on a non-thermal plasma, which they termed“non-equilibrium plasma aerotaxy (NPA)”, to synthesize pure, crystalline, free-standing, and monodispersed gallium nitride NPs with a tunable size from 5 to 45 nm. Their experimental set-up is presented in Fig. 46. The growth of NPs by a surface-growth mechanism in NPA enabled the synthesis of NPs with high monodispersity, despite some aggregation. Their system comprised three units: the aerosol unit, the plasma unit, and the collection unit. In the aerosol unit, gallium was placed on a filament that was heated resistively by a DC power supply to generate gallium vapor that was carried by an argon flow to the plasma unit. Before introduction to the plasma unit, rapid quenching led to nucleation of gallium aerosol droplets. The gallium particles reacted with nitrogen in the plasma unit to produce gallium nitride. The plasma unit was a flow-through tubular reactor in which a 13.56 MHz, 80 W RF power supply with a matching network generated the plasma. Finally, product particles were collected on a removeable collection filter or deposited on a TEM grid. The residence time could be adjusted from 10 to 100 ms, which affected average particle size and geometric standard deviation. They produced both hollow and photoluminescent solid NPs with emission in the range of 300 to 400 nm. Although large NPs exhibited emission near the bulk band-gap transition wavelength, NPs below 7 nm diameter produced blue-shifted photoluminescence characteristic of quantum confinement.370
 Fig. 46  Schematic of the system employed by Uner and Thimsen to synthesize GaN NPs through “non-equilibrium plasma aerotaxy (NPA)”. Reproduced from ref. 370 with permission from IOP Publishing, Copyright 2019. 
2.2.2.2. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Moreno-Couranjou et al. synthesized carbon nanomaterials with various typologies through non-thermal plasma processing based on low current-high voltage discharges at atmospheric pressure. This plasma configuration (Fig. 47a) is quite different from those discussed above. Their set-up included two electrodes, an annular electrode and a high-voltage electrode, between which an electric arc discharge was produced and carried downstream by a gas flow introduced tangentially. This gas was pure Ar or 8% CO2 in Ar. The small annular electrode diameter of 6 mm along with the swirling injection to produce a vortex flow supported an electric arc to the wall. The set-up included a post-discharge zone made of an insulated stainless-steel enclosure or a Pyrex tube for visualization of the plasma. They employed two alternative power sources to produce the low current-high voltage discharges: (a) a high-voltage transformer and an impedance connected in series that allowed working in AC or DC configurations with a maximum power of 4 kW, controlled by adjusting the impedance when the power source was stopped. (b) A source with electronic components to operate as a current source (0 to 1 A) which supplied a maximum breakdown voltage of 5 kV. Acetylene (C2H2) or ethylene (C2H4) was employed as the carbon source which was introduced radially from the side of the annular electrode. They produced two main families of carbon products with different typologies: (a) concentric texture carbon NPs similar to acetylene-type carbon black (Fig. 47b), furnace-type carbon black (Fig. 47c), and filled carbon shells with metal NPs (Fig. 47d). (b) Isotropic texture carbon NPs like crumpled paper sheets with a microporous texture (Fig. 47e and f). They investigated the effects of the power, the type of plasma gas, the position of hydrocarbon injection, and the type of precursor on the synthesis process and found that the growth mechanism and nanomaterial type were governed by temperature. High temperatures led to textures of the NPs such as acetylene-type carbon black or crumpled paper sheets with a lateral-type growth mechanism, while low temperatures favoured a contribution of liquid hydrocarbon droplets in the structure of the NPs to yield furnace-type carbon black through a radial-type growth mechanism.341
 Fig. 47  (a) Schematic of the non-thermal plasma reactor employed by Moreno-Couranjou et al. to synthesize carbon NPs. TEM images of (b) acetylene-type carbon black, (c) furnace-type carbon black, (d) carbon nanoshells filled with iron oxide, (e) “crumpled paper sheet”-like carbon NPs, and (f) carbon NPs with microporous texture. Adapted from ref. 341 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2009. 
Woodard et al. worked on the graphitization of carbon particles by non-thermal plasma processing, employing an in situ in-flight Fourier Transform Infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy method to study the temperature variation of carbon NPs during the process. Their set-up (Fig. 48a) included intersecting horizontal and a vertical 1 inch outer diameter tubes. A mixture of Ar, H2, and C2H2 entered the reactor through the horizontal tube at 65, 1.5, and 2 sccm, respectively. An RF power supply provided 20–100 W at 13.56 MHz to dual copper ring electrodes. The vertical tube was used for in situ FTIR of the gaseous flow containing nucleated carbon clusters and precursor molecules. Based on the FTIR results, they found that increasing the plasma power led to production of carbon NPs with a low degree of hydrogenation and progressive particle incandescence. Carbon particle temperatures exceeded 2000 K at high plasma power. Moreover, they showed that low plasma power (20 W) produced fully amorphous spherical carbon NPs (Fig. 48b), while high plasma powers (100 W) generated highly-crystalline carbon NPs with well-defined collapsed graphene-like layers (Fig. 48c). Indeed, the amorphous carbon NPs transformed to highly-graphitic carbonaceous nanomaterials as the plasma power increased. In both cases, the carbon NPs exhibit a narrow particle size distribution. Surprisingly, they observed the size of carbon NPs decreased with increasing power so that the average size was around 12 nm for a power of 20 W, decreasing to around 6 nm at a power of 100 W. They suggested that faster nucleation rates at higher power resulted in smaller NPs.342
 Fig. 48  (a) Schematic of the reactor employed by Woodard et al. to synthesize carbon NPs. TEM images of carbon NPs synthesized by applying (a) 20 W (below the power threshold for graphitization) and (b) 100 W (above the power threshold for graphitization). Adapted from ref. 342 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 2018. 
2.2.2.3. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Woodard et al. synthesized nickel NPs from nickelocene (Ni(C5H5)2) by non-thermal plasma processing. They investigated the effects of adding hydrogen to the argon plasma gas and the plasma power on the process. A 13.56 MHz RF power supply drove the plasma. The resulting nickel NPs contained carbon, at a concentration that depended on plasma power and hydrogen addition. Without hydrogen, the carbon contents were about 40 and 85% for plasma powers of 20 and 100 W, respectively, while using 30% hydrogen in the plasma gas reduced the carbon content to below 20 and 60% for the same plasma powers. In addition, they found that increasing the power and adding hydrogen to the plasma gas decreased the yield, total mass conversion efficiency, and nickel conversion efficiency. Moreover, they observed core–shell structures for the carbon-containing nickel NPs (Fig. 41c) which could be advantageous for use as non-precious metal catalysts.363
2.2.2.4. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Kortshagen and Bhandarkar developed a model to investigate NP growth by coagulation in low pressure plasmas. Their model self-consistently described the plasma properties and particle charging, and simulated the evolution of the NP size distribution by solution of the aerosol general dynamic equation. They found that the attraction of oppositely charged NPs enhanced coagulation in non-thermal plasma processing compared to coagulation in neutral aerosols, although the temporal coagulation behavior followed the same power law time dependence in both cases, provided the NP concentration exceeds that of the positive ions in the plasma. Accordingly, they suggested that the density of positive ions is the critical factor for the onset of coagulation. Moreover, they observed that the mechanism of particle charging plays an important role in determining plasma properties, while it is a negligible factor in describing the coagulation dynamics.387
Bhandarkar et al. investigated particle nucleation in a low-temperature silane plasma by developing a silicon hydride clustering model containing neutral species such as silanes, silenes, silylenes, and silyl radicals along with anions of silylene and silyl. They used the available data for the reactions of small molecules to estimate the reaction rates and define the reactions. They performed simulations for typical discharge parameters in a capacitive plasma. They found that the anion-neutral reactions played the main role in the clustering of silicon hydrides. Dehydrogenation and ring formation mainly occurred through electron-induced dissociation, while SiH2 radical insertion was a determining factor only in the initial clustering stages, in structures with at most three or four silicon atoms. In addition, they observed that increasing the ion density resulted in faster clustering and more dehydrogenated clusters because of an increase in reactive radical generation.388 They also simulated the nucleation and growth of NPs in argon–silane plasmas by developing a quasi-one-dimensional model to investigate the particle nucleation delay associated with increased gas temperature. They found that the temperature dependence of the Brownian diffusion coefficient played the main role in delaying nucleation, due to an increase in diffusion loss rate and a decrease in particle growth rate. In addition, they proposed that other factors that can affect the diffusion coefficient, like employing a lighter gas such as hydrogen or helium instead of argon, which increases the diffusion coefficient, or increasing the pressure to decrease the importance of diffusion, could affect the nucleation delay.389
In non-thermal plasma processing, the aerosol particles are distributed among both particle sizes and charge states. However, the majority of dusty (particle-containing) plasma modelling studies treat the particles as monodisperse and all characterized by the same average charge. Agarwal and Girshick developed a sectional model to investigate, in an effective way, the main qualitative features of the time-dependent evolution of both particle size and charge distributions in non-thermal plasma processing such as co-emergence of tiny neutral NPs and larger negatively charged NPs, size and charge distribution effects on coagulation, expansion and structure of the particle cloud, and the nanodusty plasma afterglow dynamics. This work produced multiple new insights into the spatial and temporal variation of NP size, concentration, and charge within a non-thermal plasma.390
Agarwal and Girshick also presented a simulation study of the RF-coupled parallel-plate non-thermal plasma generation of silicon NPs based upon a one-dimensional numerical model with self-consistently-coupled models of the plasma, the chemistry, and the aerosol dynamics. Inclusion of chemistry of nucleation and surface growth was a major addition relative to their earlier study. The plasma and precursor gases were a mixture of argon and silane at a ratio of 30 to 1, and two RF voltages of 100 and 250 V were considered. The higher voltage of 250 V produced a plasma discharge with two spatially separated lobes containing mainly negatively-charged large NPs. These were separated by a region of lower particle mass and surface area concentration, in which nucleation occurred. Based upon the simulation results, they found that the nucleation in the region between the lobes resulted from several coupled phenomena such as NP transport, the potential profile of plasma, and the trapping of silicon hydride anions as a driving force for nucleation in the system.391
Le Picard and Girshick developed a model to investigate the effects of single-particle charge limits (size- and material-dependent maximum charge that a particle can accommodate) on the stationary NP charge distributions in non-thermal plasma processing. Their analytical model of stationary charge distribution was in good agreement with Monte Carlo charging simulations. They studied the influence of charge limits on the various values of electron-to-ion density ratio and the effect of ion mass on the charge distribution transient behavior, considering charging limits. They reported that a decrease in the charge limit led to a severe decrease in the required time to achieve a steady-state charge distribution. Moreover, they found that high-frequency charge fluctuations were relatively more important factors when the charge limits were accounted for, relative to simulations without charge limits.392
Although there is an overall tendency for the NPs to be charged negatively in non-thermal plasma processing, a minor fraction of the NPs can be neutral and therefore not subject to any electrostatic confinement in the plasma, or can even be positively charged, and thus electrostatically attracted to surfaces bounding the plasma. Mamunuru et al. performed Monte Carlo simulations to study the effects of various parameters on the non-negatively charged NP fraction of the stationary particle charge distribution in non-thermal plasma processing. They considered two deviations from the orbital motion limited theory of particle charging: electron tunneling that results single-particle charge limits, and increasing ion current to NPs because of charge-exchange collisions inside the NP's capture radius. They found that decreasing the size of the NPs, the electron temperature, or the positive ion temperature, and increasing the pressure or the electronegativity of the particle material were all factors that could increase the non-negatively charged NP fractions. Moreover, they reported that silica, with a low electron affinity of 1.0 eV, exhibited much higher non-negatively charged particle fractions compared to silicon, with a higher electron affinity of 4.05 eV. Notably, they assumed collision attachment of electrons and ions as the main mechanism for the NP charging in non-thermal plasma, while recognizing that additional electron-emissive effects, e.g. photodetachment or secondary electron emission, could further increase the non-negatively charged NP fractions.393
In a very recent review and analysis, Girshick surveyed the effects of the interaction between the negatively-charged and neutral ions on the particle formation process in non-thermal plasma processing. He found that the reaction between the negatively-charged ions trapped electrostatically in the non-thermal plasma and neutral molecules or radicals governed nucleation, while the reactions of the neutral molecules on the surface of negatively-charged NPs were responsible for particle surface growth, and coagulation was dominated by collisions between tiny neutral NPs with negatively-charged NPs. Thus, all three processes are dominated by interaction of a neutral species with a negatively charged one. He noted that induction of an electric dipole or image potential in a neutral molecule or NP by an anion or a negatively-charged NP enhances collision rates that are important for nucleation, particle surface growth, and coagulation.394
Woodard et al. characterized their non-thermal plasmas using a Langmuir probe that could accurately extract plasma parameters and electron energy distribution function (EEDF) in the presence of NPs, i.e., in a dusty plasma. While the Langmuir probe method is well-developed, its application in dusty plasmas is notoriously difficult. To overcome this challenge, they employed a two-plasma system. They first produced graphitic carbon NPs in an Ar–H2 low-pressure plasma. After complete conversion of acetylene (C2H2) to NPs in the first reactor, they introduced the dust into a second reactor in which they performed Langmuir probe measurements. By doing so, they were able to investigate the effects of various operating parameters on the non-thermal dusty plasma properties in the absence of any nucleation or growth of the NPs, which were not occurring in the second plasma reactor where the measurement was performed. They showed that the presence of the NPs did not interfere with their measurements. Notably, they found that increasing input power did not monotonically increase the charge carried by the NPs. In fact, it unexpectedly decreased at sufficiently high input power levels.395 This group employed the same technique to investigate the effects of electron emission from the NPs on the electron energy distribution in non-thermal dusty plasmas. They observed an unexpected broad peak in the EEDF at energies ranging from 2 to 4 V. Based on results of a self-consistent model that was in a good qualitative agreement with the Langmuir probe measurements, they inferred that distributed electron emission from the NPs resulted in the unexpected peak corresponding to the NP floating potential. Based on a NP energy balance analysis employing the measured NP concentration and plasma density, they inferred that hot NPs, which could not cool due to slow heat transfer at low pressure and insufficient radiative losses from tiny NPs, emitted electrons into the non-thermal dusty plasma by field-assisted thermionic emission. This electron emission influenced both the electron energy distribution and charge balance of the NPs in the plasma. They concluded that the plasma ionization balance can be strongly affected by the existence of dust in the non-thermal plasma, to a greater extent than previously expected.396

2.3. Chemically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods
Chemically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods are aerosol synthesis methods in which the precursors are chemically different from the product nanomaterials. In general, compared to the physically-based vapor-phase synthesis methods, the main advantage of these methods is the synthesis of nanomaterials in larger quantities per batch or per unit time and the major disadvantage is greater complexity and associated challenges in the synthesis of high-purity nanomaterials because of the nature of the processes (the precursors and the final products are not chemically similar). The most important methods in this category are chemical vapor condensation, flame-based aerosol synthesis (including vapor-fed aerosol flame synthesis (VAFS) and liquid-fed aerosol flame synthesis (LAFS)), spray pyrolysis, and laser pyrolysis.

2.3.1. Chemical vapor condensation. 
In chemical vapor condensation (CVC, also called chemical vapor synthesis (CVS) or hot-wall synthesis), vapor-phase precursors are introduced into a hot-wall reactor27,29,30 and heat transfer from the reactor walls supplies the energy required to initiate precursor decomposition. Rapid heating, high temperature, and resulting high supersaturation are factors that favor production of NPs in the CVC method over growth of films on the reactor walls by chemical vapor deposition (CVD).397
Because of its great importance for production not only of dense thin films, but also diverse two-dimensional and tailored nanomaterials, numerous valuable studies of CVD processes and materials produced by them have been published. For example, Beckermann et al. studied the production of multi-component oxide nanosystems through CVD,21 Tobacchi et al. investigated the effect of various precursors on the CVD of transition metal oxide nanostructures,398 and also presented a modelling study of the CVD process,399 Devi investigated precursor chemistry effects on the CVD of nanomaterials for microelectronics and optoelectronics,400 Kurek et al. reviewed the use of guanidinate ligands for developing CVD precursors,401 Qin et al. reviewed the role of substrates in the production of two-dimensional materials via CVD,402 and Cai et al. reviewed the applications and the heterostructures of two-dimensional nanomaterials produced through CVD in addition to their growth.403 That being said, reviewing the use of CVD processes to produce nanomaterials through deposition on substrates is beyond the scope of this review, where we focus only on the related CVC of nanomaterials in the gas phase.
As noted above, CVC is essentially the same as hot-wall CVD, but operated under conditions of relatively high temperature and pressure for which particle formation is favored compared to film deposition on the reactor walls. Thus, the large knowledge base related to precursor chemistries developed for CVD is available and of great value in guiding the synthesis of materials such as nitrides, carbides, and complex oxides by CVC. Simplicity, demonstrated scale-up to approximately 100 ton per day for some nanomaterials like titania, and relatively well-understood mechanisms are other advantages of the method.175 Control of powder characteristics to meet the needs of a specific application is an important feature of the CVC process.404 This method is suitable to synthesize non-oxide nanomaterials, because the synthesis atmosphere can be controlled to exclude oxygen. Not only can single-phase nanomaterials be formed from a single precursor, but doped nanomaterials can also be synthesized using multiple precursors.27,30 Although a wide variety of precursors can be used in this method, metal chlorides are the most common in large-scale industrial application due to considerations of cost, purity, and vapor pressure.175 In laboratory research, precursors that do not produce corrosive chlorine-containing byproducts are often preferred, as shown by the survey of materials below. As another key advantage, the method benefits from straightforward control of temperature and residence time, which is helpful in synthesizing nanomaterials of controlled shapes and sizes.405 As a disadvantage, the heating and cooling rates are limited by heat transfer from the walls of the reactor, which limits production rates of very small NPs that are free of hard aggregates.248 The need for relatively expensive precursors with significant vapor pressure and stability below the reaction temperature, the formation of hard agglomerates due to relatively long residence times and slow heating and cooling rates, and high energy requirements are key limitations of the method.175Table 7 provides a summary and Fig. 49 presents representative TEM images of some materials synthesized using this technique.

2.3.1.1. Ceramics. 
Chang, Skandan, Danforth, Kear, and Hahn pioneered the CVC method for synthesis of nanostructured SiCxNy and ZrOxCy. They started from an inert gas condensation set-up and modified it to use metalorganic precursors. They substituted the energy source in the IGC method with a molybdenum-wound tube furnace with a high-purity alumina tube to decompose the precursors and generate a continuous flow of nanomaterials in a carrier gas. The set-up (Fig. 50) consisted of two main parts connected by a needle valve: a reaction chamber at a pressure of 1 to 50 mbar and a precursor delivery system at atmospheric pressure. The needle valve controlled the flow rate of the precursor at 8.55 mmol min−1 in He carrier gas. Upon entering the tube furnace, the precursor decomposed and particles nucleated. The rapid expansion of the aerosol quenched the particle growth and sintering process. Finally, the particles were deposited on a rotating liquid nitrogen-cooled substrate for collection. They found three important factors for successful synthesis of nanomaterials using the method: (a) a low precursor concentration in the carrier gas to minimize the collision frequency between freshly nucleated NPs, (b) rapid expansion of the NP aerosol into an inert gas, and (c) rapid cooling at the outlet of the reactor. For the synthesis of loosely-agglomerated SiCxNy nanomaterials with an average primary particle size of 2 to 10 nm, they used hexamethyl-disilazane((CH3)3SiNHSi(CH3)3) as the precursor and controlled the temperature of the tube furnace in the range of 1100 to 1400 °C. Thereafter, they conducted heat treatment on amorphous SiCxNy in Ar to produce cubic β-SiC nanocrystals with a spherical morphology. Crystallization began at 1200 to 1300 °C and completed at 1400 °C. They employed heating in H2 to produce cubic β-SiC nanocrystals with a whisker-like morphology (Fig. 49a) which formed at 1400 °C, and heating in NH3/H2 (1:1 mixture) to obtain hexagonal α-Si3N4 nanocrystals with a whisker-like morphology. To generate amorphous ZrOxCy nanomaterials with an average size below 10 nm, they used zirconium tert-butoxide (Zr[OC(CH3)3]4) as the precursor with the furnace at 500 to 700 °C. They found that the amount of polymeric species in the product decreased with increasing temperature of the tube furnace. They suggested that trapping of volatile species in the particles caused the product to be amorphous. They sintered the nanomaterials in air to yield tetragonal and monoclinic nanostructured ZrO2. Crystallization began at around 400 °C and completed at 1225 °C.406 Kear and Strutt follow the same strategy to produce SiO2, SiC, and Si3N4 nanomaterials using different carrier gases, but the same silicon precursor (hexamethyl-disilazane). They used H2O, H2, and NH3 to produce SiO2, SiC, and Si3N4, respectively.407
 Fig. 50  Schematic of the synthesis set-up employed by Chang et al. to synthesize SiCxNy NPs by CVC. Adapted from ref. 406 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1994. 
Klein et al. investigated the effect of different operating parameters such as precursor compound, decomposition temperature, total pressure, precursor partial pressure, and reactor length (residence time) on the synthesis of SiC NPs by CVD with a maximum production rate of 20 g h−1. They found that using tetramethylsilane (Si(CH3)4) as the precursor, a decomposition temperature above 1400 K, total pressure of 1 kPa, precursor partial pressure of less than 600 Pa, and hot zone length less than 0.2 m yielded highly-crystalline β-SiC nanomaterials with small grain and particle sizes and a low degree of agglomeration. Notably, they used mass flow controllers instead of needle valves to control the delivery of the precursor and a silicon infiltrated SiC tube instead of alumina tube in the tube furnace when using tetramethylsilane as the precursor.408
Srdic et al. synthesized monodispersed non-agglomerated highly-crystalline ZrO2 with a crystallite size of approximately 5 nm by CVC. They thermally decomposed zirconium tert-butoxide as the zirconium precursor in a tube furnace at 1000 °C, combining flows of 100 sccm He as the carrier gas and 1000 sccm O2 to complete the oxidation. In addition, they studied the sintering behavior of the product nanomaterials and found that the evolution of pore sizes differed for sintering in air vs. a vacuum atmosphere. Vacuum sintering required lower sintering and densification temperatures.449
Gebauer et al. employed CVC for production of various monodispersed highly-crystalline metal oxides such as ZnO, CuO, SnO2, TiO2, and In2O3 NPs by thermal decomposition of diethylzinc, copper(II) acetylacetonate (C10H14CuO4), tetramethyltin (Sn(CH3)4), titanium(IV) tetraisopropoxide, and indium(III) acetylacetonate (C15H21InO6) at 800 °C in He. Oxygen was also introduced to the tube furnace to ensure complete oxidation. Finally, they collected the products in a thermophoretic collector which is advantageous for separation of particles from gases with minimal pressure drop. The morphologies of the synthesized NPs are presented in Fig. 51. They used inkjet printing to deposit these oxides after dispersion in ethylene glycol.397
 Fig. 51  SEM images of (a) In2O3, (b) SnO2, (c) CuO, (d) ZnO, and (e) TiO2 CVC-synthesized NPs reported by Gebauer et al. Adapted from ref. 397 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2018. 
Polarz et al. used a CVC process to synthesize zinc oxide nanocrystals with a high level of purity that improved their semiconducting properties. They decomposed the single-source precursor methylzinc iso-propoxide ([CH3ZnOCH(CH3)2]4) at 300 to 900 °C. The precursor was evaporated in another tube furnace at 40 to 100 °C and carried to the reaction zone by 1.5 lpm nitrogen. To achieve complete oxidation, a mixture of 1.2 lpm nitrogen and 0.3 lpm oxygen was added to the flow containing the precursor. Accordingly, they produced pure zinc oxide NPs with adjustable primary particle size from about 6 to 30 nm. Furthermore, they studied the gas-phase synthesis mechanism in detail, and contrasted it with the solid-state chemistry of this precursor. They found that if they did not introduce oxygen to the reaction chamber, then elemental zinc was produced by elimination of organic compounds from the precursor. This paper included a combined experimental and modelling study of the aerosol sintering process that governs the final product morphology. Also, they concluded that the synthesis of defect-free zinc oxide using CVC is possible based on photoluminescence spectroscopy.442
Kim and Kim synthesized loosely agglomerated tungsten carbide by CVC. They vaporized hexacarbonyl tungsten (W(CO6)) at 120 °C and delivered it to the reaction chamber using carbon monoxide carrier gas at 1200 sccm. They decomposed the precursor at 500 to 1000 °C to investigate the effect of the temperature on the particle size and found that the NP size decreased with increasing decomposition temperature. Moreover, they reported coexistence of pure W and WC1−x at a decomposition temperature of 1000 °C via initial formation of W followed by partial carbonization.457
2.3.1.2. Doped ceramic oxides. 
Konrad et al. employed CVC for synthesis of single-phase cubic Y2O3:Eu nanocrystals with a mean particle size as low as 10 nm. This was the first production of single-phase cubic nanocrystals of this material of such small size. To do so, they used tetramethyl-heptanedionato-yttrium (Y(C11H19O2)3) as yttrium precursor which played the role of host lattice and tetramethyl-heptanedionato-europium (Eu(C11H19O2)3) as the precursor for europium which was the optically-active dopant. The precursors were delivered from two separate bubblers in two separate oil baths held at 437 and 428 K for yttrium and europium precursors, respectively. They used Ar as the carrier gas with a flow rate of 200 sccm for each of the two precursor lines. The tubes between the bubblers and the reaction chamber were heated to prevent precursor condensation. Pure oxygen at 800 sccm entered the reaction chamber through a separate line. The temperature of the reaction zone was 1273 K. They used a water-cooled cylinder to collect the product NPs. They reported blue shifting of the absorption bands for the synthesized nanomaterials compared to coarse grained material.440
Srdic et al. followed the same strategy to produce ZrO2 doped with 3, 5, 15, and 30 mol% Al2O3 by decomposing zirconium-tert-butoxide as the host precursor and aluminum tri-sec-butoxide (C12H27AlO3) as the dopant precursor. Additionally, they performed a sintering study on the synthesized nanomaterials and found that the addition of alumina limited the grain growth of zirconia. For the first time, they reported the complete densification of ZrO2:Al2O3 nanomaterials with average grain sizes in the range of 40–45 nm and full transparency using pressureless vacuum sintering for 1 hour at temperatures as low as 1000 °C.451
Brehm et al. used CVC for synthesis of zinc oxide NPs doped with aluminum, gallium, and indium by thermal decomposition of tetramethyl-heptanedionate precursors at 1000 °C in He. They found that indium atoms reside substantially on zinc lattice sites while aluminum and gallium atoms reside on interstitial sites.444 Hartner et al. investigated the electrical properties of Al-doped-ZnO NPs synthesized by CVC from diethylzinc ((C2H5)2Zn) and trimethylaluminum (Al2(CH3)6) at 1000 °C in He. They reported that CVC enables incorporation of dopant atoms at concentrations much greater than the thermodynamic solubility limit. They investigated the electrical properties by impedance spectroscopy under hydrogen and synthetic air at 323–673 K and found that the conductivity under hydrogen is greater than under air, although both cases exhibit dependence of transport properties on temperature and doping level. Under hydrogen, they found that an increase in doping level produced a decrease in conductivity due to enhanced scattering associated with increasing disorder. They also found that increasing dopant concentration produced a change from positive temperature coefficient behavior to negative temperature coefficient. Under synthetic air, they concluded that an increase in the dopant level first increased conductivity by six orders of magnitude due to the production of free charge carriers with dopant incorporation, reaching a maximum conductivity at an Al concentration of 7.7% then decreased at higher concentrations.445
2.3.1.3. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Choi et al. decomposed iron carbonyl (Fe(CO)5) and cobalt carbonyl (Co2(CO)8) as iron and cobalt precursors, respectively, to generate Fe and Co NPs with average sizes from 5 to 13 nm and metal–metal oxide core–shell morphology. They used temperatures of 400 to 1100 °C and found that the NP size increased with increasing temperature.404 In another study, they investigated the effects of operating parameters of the CVC method on the metal–metal oxide core–shell nanomaterials. The average diameter of the NPs was from 6 to 25 nm. Increasing the decomposition temperature produced nanomaterials of larger size, broader size distribution, and less spherical morphology. Using Ar as the carrier gas instead of He also produced larger particles. In addition, they suggested that an observed increase in the lattice parameter of the core with decreasing particle size resulted from interaction between cores and shells.436 They also synthesized ferromagnetic carbon-coated iron and cobalt nanocapsules with a metallic core and amorphous shell via CVC using the same precursors, but with carbon monoxide (CO) as the carrier gas. Independent of the decomposition temperature and the carrier gas flow rate, the phases of carbon-coated Co nanocapsules included a combination of Co in both fcc and hcp crystalline structures and cobalt carbides. In contrast, those factors influenced the phases of carbon-coated Fe nanocapsules. Thermal decomposition at 500 to 1000 °C resulted in α-Fe and Fe3C as the majority phases, with the exception of 700 °C for which the only phase was Fe3C. Decomposition at 1100 °C led to α-Fe, γ-Fe, and Fe3C. They also investigated the magnetic properties of the synthesized nanomaterials and found that the saturation magnetization of the carbon-coated Co increased with increasing decomposition temperature and reached 90% of the value for bulk cobalt. For carbon-coated iron nanocapsules, the saturation magnetization was 75 and 33% of the value for bulk iron at decomposition temperatures of 400 and 1100 °C.413 Hong et al. produced Fe nanofluids by thermal decomposition of iron carbonyl in He, followed by sonication of the Fe NPs in glycerol. They reported that the thermal conductivity of Fe nanofluids increased after sonication.438
Guo et al. employed what they called viscous boundary layer chemical vapor synthesis, to generate MWCNTs filled with ferromagnetic nanomaterials such as iron carbide and iron cobalt nanocrystals. In this method, a laminar flow of pyrolyzed metallocene/argon vapor in a quartz tube led to the formation of a viscous boundary layer between the flow and the rough surface. Nucleation and subsequent growth of MWCNTs filled with ferromagnetic nanomaterials occurred within this growing boundary layer. For this, they evaporated 180 mg of ferrocene (C10H10Fe) or a mixture of 40 mg ferrocene and 40 mg cobaltocene (C10H10Co) at 450 °C to produce MWCNTs filled with Fe3C or FeCo, respectively. They used 11 sccm argon as the carrier gas and the synthesis temperature was kept at 990 °C in a quartz tube furnace with a length of 1.5 m and outer diameter of 22 mm. The process duration was 10 to 20 minutes. The product was radial nanotubes (multiple nanotubes growing outward from a central catalyst particle) that were filled with Fe3C or FeCo and exhibited saturation magnetizations of 30 and 80 emu g−1, respectively. The authors observed a surprisingly high coercivity of 1400 Oe at room temperature for both cases. They proposed that the small size of the ferromagnetic nanomaterials filling the nanotubes played the main role in achieving the high value of coercivity. At constant pyrolysis temperature and vapor flow rate, the high evaporation temperature of the precursors was the most important factor governing the synthesis of these small ferromagnetic nanomaterials with high coercivity.409
2.3.1.4. Semiconductors. 
Vasilyeva et al. synthesized WSe2 nanocrystals with a low degree of agglomeration by CVC. A schematic of their reactor is presented in Fig. 52a. They used hexacarbonyl tungsten and selenium as precursors. W(CO)6 was evaporated in a bubbler kept at 100 °C and carried by argon to a two-zone tube furnace. The first zone with lower temperature (maximum 600 °C) had a removable cartridge in which crystalline Se powder evaporated and mixed with the evaporated W(CO)6. Precursor decomposition and particle formation took place in the second zone at higher temperature (800–1000 °C). The particles had a S–W-S sandwich-like layered morphology with 5–10 layers and interlayer distance of 6.8–7 Å (Fig. 52b). Carbon and oxygen were the main impurities because of the chemical composition of the tungsten precursor, and could be reduced to 4 mol% by reducing the carbonyl concentration. Additionally, they used the WSe2 NPs as anodes in rechargeable Li-ion batteries and reached a high reversible capacity of 590–600 mA h g−1 with capacity retention for over 150 cycles.461
 Fig. 52  (a) Schematic of the experimental set-up employed by Vasilyeva et al. to synthesize WSe2 NPs and (b) TEM image of WSe2 NPs with a S–W–S layered morphology with 5–10 layers and interlayer distance of 6.8–7 Å. Adapted from ref. 461 with permission from Walter de Gruyter and Company, Copyright 2015. 
As an example of relatively large-scale synthesis of semiconductor NPs by CVC, Kessler et al. employed a pilot plant scale hot wall reactor (operated at 10–100 kPa to control NP size and morphology) to produce highly-boron-doped silicon. They used silane (SiH4) and diborane (B2H6) at a ratio of 1 to 0.005. The precursor mixture decomposed at 1000 °C and 25 kPa to produce NPs with a specific surface area of 39 m2 g−1 at a rate of 0.5 kg h−1. This high production rate of highly-doped silicon NPs is advantageous for thermoelectric applications which require large quantities of such materials compared to many other applications of nanomaterials, but which also do not require extremely small particles or extremely high surface area.418
Roy et al. decomposed a single-source precursor with a unique chemical design, a siloxy-substituted-ZnO tetranuclear heterocubane ((MeZnOSiMe3)4) that facilitates production of pure zinc silicates and benefits from reduced need for intermolecular processes. This approach enabled the synthesis of α-Zn2SiO4 nanocrystals with the smallest crystal size reported at the time. Their set-up (Fig. 53) included an evaporation tube furnace at 60 to 140 °C for delivery of the precursor, a tube furnace for the decomposition of the precursor at 900 °C, a particle charger, a particle precipitator, and an on-line system for particle-size measurement or size selection. They used nitrogen at 1 lpm as the carrier gas and introduced 0.5 lpm of oxygen to the second furnace to complete the oxidation.453
 Fig. 53  Schematic of the system employed by Roy et al. to synthesize α-Zn2SiO4 nanocrystals. Adapted from ref. 453 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2004. 
Le et al. studied the photocatalytic activity of TiO2-anatase nanocrystals synthesized by CVC using titanium(IV) alkoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) delivered from a bubbler at 95 °C by 700 sccm Ar. Oxygen was introduced into the reaction chamber at 7000 sccm to ensure complete oxidation. After mixing the titania NPs with coconut shell activated carbon for photocatalytic applications, they found that the titania performed better than commercial TiO2 for photocatalytic degradation of methylene blue.428
Park et al. synthesized Mn/TiO2 NPs by impregnation of Mn-acetate and Mn-nitrite precursors following CVC synthesis of titania NPs by thermal decomposition of titanium(IV) alkoxide at 900 °C and investigated the effect of sulfur on this catalyst for low-temperature NO reduction. They obtained good low-temperature activity and better resistance to S poisoning in the presence of SO2 for catalysts made using the CVC-synthesized titania compared to commercial titania.429 In addition, they used MnOx/TiO2 as a catalyst for oxidation of gaseous formaldehyde with ozone, and it also showed promising performance.432 In another study, they investigated the photocatalytic performance and photoluminescence of V2O5/TiO2 nanocomposites in which titania NPs were produced by CVC and V2O5 was loaded by impregnation. They found that photocatalytic activity was twice that of commercial titania. In addition, they reported that catalysts produced using the CVC-synthesized titania exhibited a higher specific surface area, more Ti3+ ions, more hydroxyl groups, smaller and amorphous particles, and a lower photoluminescence intensity.430 Moreover, they studied NH3-based selective catalytic reduction (SCR) using the CVC synthesized samples and observed enhancement at low temperatures. They concluded that the greater number of Ti3+ ions and hydroxyl groups resulted in more OH moieties on the catalysts to generate V–OH sites, along with fewer acidic sites than the catalyst made from commercial titania with primarily Lewis acid sites. Moreover, the higher surface area and smaller particle size had a synergistic effect for improved SCR activity at low temperatures because of greater availability of active sites. In addition, they found that the catalysts produced using CVC-synthesized TiO2 exhibited greater reducibility and were active at lower temperatures than those made using commercial TiO2. They attributed this to greater participation of chemisorbed oxygen in the reduction on the surface of the titania samples synthesized by CVC.431
Siedl et al. synthesized titania and tin oxide NPs by CVC and then produced SnO2–TiO2 nanocomposites that could be applied in heterojunction solar cells. They decomposed titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) for titania synthesis and tetrabutyltin (C16H36Sn) for tin oxide synthesis at 800 °C in a CVC reactor. Subsequent thermal treatment at around 600 °C followed by oxidation at the same temperature and cooling in oxygen removed any unreacted precursor to purify the product with the stoichiometric composition. The average sizes of the final NPs were 10 and 13 nm for SnO2 and TiO2 NPs, respectively. Thereafter, a mixture of these NPs was dispersed in formic acid to achieve a high concentration of TiO2-SnO2 heterojunctions by adjustment of the particles’ surface charge to promote heteroaggregation. After vacuum annealing of these materials, they observed an enhanced cross section for interparticle charge separation in this system of heterojunctions via interfacial charge transfer, which produced high concentrations of separated electrons and hole centers and excellent photocatalytic activity.434
2.3.1.5. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Schild et al. interfaced a commercial fluid mechanics code with a simple monodispersed aerosol dynamics model that tracked a single aggregate size, primary particles per aggregate, and specific surface area in a nonuniform temperature and flow field to elucidate the effects of various operating parameters on characteristics of the NPs, such as size and morphology, especially at very early stages of aerosol synthesis. The simulation tracked the chemical reaction of precursor, particle nucleation, and subsequent growth, aggregation, and sintering of NPs in addition to the detailed treatment of mass, energy, and momentum transfer. They considered the synthesis of titania NPs from titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) and oxygen in a hot-wall reactor as a case study. With this model, they were able to simulate the very early stages of the aerosol nanosynthesis process and compute the dynamic evolution of the size of aggregated nanoclusters, number of primary particles forming the aggregates, and the specific surface area as a function of position throughout the hot-wall reactor. This simulation was among the first to allow realistic modelling of the synthesis process of NPs in actual CVC reactors under realistically complex conditions.462
Sohn et al. developed a three-dimensional computational fluid dynamics (CFD) model to analyze phenomena occurring within a CVC process, including fluid flow, heat transfer, and chemical reactions along with the formation and growth of NPs. They considered the synthesis of aluminum NPs from aluminum chloride (AlCl3) by reaction with magnesium (Mg) vapor as a case study. Using the model, they reasonably calculated the temperature, velocity profile, and particle size distribution within the CVC reactor. They employed a population balance model and phenomenological chemical reaction rates to model the formation and growth of NPs under different operating conditions, including precursor concentrations and total gas flow rate. They used commercial CFD software (Fluent) for their simulations and employed the Quadrature Method of Moments (QMOM) to approximate the population balance (aerosol general dynamics equation). By adjusting the unknown rate constants for particle nucleation and growth, they were able to achieve good agreement with experimental data. In general, their spatial CFD model can be employed to predict the NP size distribution, to optimize the synthesis technique, and to visualize internal flow and reaction behaviors in a CVC process which could result deeper understanding of the process.463 In another study, this group performed two-dimensional multiphase CFD simulations to study the CVC process of generating tungsten carbide NPs from tungsten hexachloride (WCl6), methane, and hydrogen. Notably, they performed a parametric investigation to fit rate parameters for the nucleation and growth rates. Again, with these fitted parameters, the computed values for the average size of the NPs and the precursor concentration were in a good agreement with the experimental data.464 While the QMOM approach used to treat the aerosol dynamics in these equations is computationally efficient, well-suited for coupling with detailed CFD modelling, and provides values of particle concentration, mean particle size, and measures of the general shape of the size distribution, reconstruction of a size distribution corresponding to the computed moments can be difficult.465,466
Djenadic and Winterer developed a simple reaction-coagulation-sintering model to investigate the CVC process. They studied the effects of the time-temperature profile on the size, crystallinity, and agglomeration of titania NPs produced by thermal decomposition of titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) in a hot-wall reactor as a case study. Based upon the time-temperature profile, they synthesized pure anatase particles or mixtures of the three stable crystalline phases of anatase, rutile (≤11 vol%), and brookite (≤29 vol%) with primary particle sizes ranging from 1.7–10.5 nm and agglomerate sizes of 24.3–55.6 nm. They regulated the time-temperature profile in the hot-wall reactor with temperatures ranging from 600 °C to 1750 °C using an inductively heated furnace with variable inductor geometry. Using this novel system, they were able to control the size, crystallinity, and agglomeration of the NPs by tuning the time-temperature profile in a CVC process.467 In another study, Winterer employed Monte Carlo simulations to determine the optimized time-temperature profiles to improve the extrinsic properties of the NPs synthesized by CVC. This was an important step in moving toward an “inverse design” approach in which desired particle properties are specified and conditions required to achieve those properties are identified.468 Ognjanović and Winterer employed a simple reaction-coagulation-sintering model to study the effects of operating parameters including the reaction temperature and the system pressure on the particle characteristics of aluminum nitride NPs produced in a hot-wall CVC reactor using triethylaluminum (C6H15Al) and ammonia (NH3). They synthesized AlN nanocrystals in pure wurtzite crystalline phase with crystallite sizes of 2.7–11.5 nm and NP sizes of 4.3–12.4 nm. They were able to reduce the degree of agglomeration to a value of one at temperatures above 1400 °C and a system pressure of 100 mbar. This quality of synthesis is crucial for transforming the product powders into nanocrystalline bulk materials. They synthesized nanocrystals temperatures with crystallite size below 3.5 nm without any internal defects, employing milder synthesis conditions. Except at the lowest temperatures, their model predicted the characteristics of the CVC-synthesized NPs well.469
Wergen et al. synthesized germanium NPs through the thermal decomposition of germane (GeH4) in a hot-wall reactor. They employed an external seeding unit with a hot wire generator using niobium, tantalum, tungsten, or molybdenum as the seeding metal to establish a stable aerosol particle concentration. The hot wire generator produced small particles that served as heterogeneous nuclei and inhibited homogeneous nucleation of Ge particles. This prevented the production of hard agglomerates from those small germanium NPs. Among the seeding metals, tungsten and molybdenum produced the most uniform spherical germanium NPs with narrow size distributions (geometrical standard deviations below 1.1). Moreover, they employed a population balance model coupled with the reactor centerline temperature profile from a CFD simulation. The modelling results were validated by the experimental data and showed that the homogenous nucleation rate decreased with increasing concentration of the seeds. This reduced the total concentration of the NPs in the hot-wall reactor. Thus, the modelling provided key insight into the conditions for which heterogeneous nucleation can be expected to dominate homogeneous nucleation and how that translates into production of relatively monodisperse particles by surface growth rather than polydisperse particles associated with coagulation and sintering.470
The shape of nickel NPs is a signification factor in determining their performance in various applications. For instance, a cubic shape, which results in exposure of specific crystal facets and enhanced magnetic properties, may be preferable in catalysis and bioseparations applications, while in multilayer ceramic capacitors spherical nickel NPs are preferred because of their high packing density. While aerosol processes most often produce spherical particles, when the particles grow as a solid, rather than a liquid droplet, production of other shapes is possible. Accordingly, Yang et al. performed theoretical and experimental studies to control the shape of nickel NPs synthesized in a CVC process. Their density functional theory (DFT) modelling study focused on calculating gas adsorption energies and stabilization of Ni (1 0 0) and Ni (1 1 1) surfaces. Either kinetic or thermodynamic stabilization of particular crystal facets can change the shape of the growing NPs. Their modelling results helped to explain the experimental observation that the synthesis of nickel NPs via reduction of nickel chloride (NiCl2) vapor by hydrogen in a tube furnace with two heating zones can lead to a spherical morphology when the second zone employs a hydrogen atmosphere, while a nickel chloride vapor atmosphere favored the synthesis of nickel NPs with a cubic morphology. A fast reduction rate favored production of spherical NPs, while a slower reduction rate led to the formation of cubic NPs.471
Vazquez-Pufleau et al. developed a novel system for the measurement of stable high molecular weight silicon hydride clusters with sizes below 2 nm, generated in a furnace aerosol reactor. A schematic of their experimental apparatus is presented in Fig. 54. The precursor was silane (SiH4) diluted in helium and mixed with a second stream of pure helium before introduction into the furnace aerosol reactor. A sample stream was separated from the main flow leaving the reactor and diluted by a factor of about 45 with nitrogen. A high-resolution differential mobility analyzer with an electrometer was employed to measure the size distribution and better understand the initial stages of silicon NP nucleation and growth during silane pyrolysis in the aerosol reactor. They reported that the diameter of the silicon clusters at the initial stages was around 0.7 nm, which was the first measurement of subnanometer stable silicon hydride clusters from silane pyrolysis. Based on their observations, they studied four roles that subnanometer clusters could play in particle formation, although these observations were not sufficient to conclusively establish their role among these possibilities.416
 Fig. 54  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Roy et al. to synthesize silicon NPs through CVC and for the measurement of stable high molecular weight silicon hydride clusters with sizes below 2 nm. Reproduced from ref. 416 with permission from AIP Publishing, Copyright 2020. 


2.3.2. Spray pyrolysis. 
In spray pyrolysis, rather than delivering vaporized precursors into the reaction zone, as in chemical vapor synthesis, the precursor solutions are atomized and small aerosol droplets are directly delivered by a carrier gas to the reaction chamber, which is usually a tube furnace.37 In the reaction chamber, the solvent evaporates, the solvent vapor diffuses away from the droplet into the gas phase, the droplet shrinks, the temperature of the droplet increases, and solute diffuses toward the droplet center.27,472 Generally, the direct, effective, facile and continuous synthesis of dense NPs with high homogeneity and equiaxed shape along with precise control on the morphology make spray pyrolysis a valuable vapor-phase nanosynthesis technique.37,472,473 However, the generation of hollow particles or broken shells by droplet-to-particle conversion and poor productivity can be considered common limitations of the spray pyrolysis approach.472,473 Jung et al. categorized the benefits and limitations of the spray pyrolysis technique based on the possible aerosol generation techniques.473 The aerosol generation techniques for spray pyrolysis include pneumatic atomization (pressure, two-fluid, nebulizers), ultrasonic atomization, ultrasonic spray nozzle, electrospray, and filter expansion.472,473 Aqueous solutions are generally cheap, safe, and widely available. They are easily handled, and a broad range of water-soluble metal salts like metal chlorides and oxychlorides can be used as the precursors in this approach.472 This method has also been widely used for the synthesis of thin films or the deposition of nanomaterials on substrates, but those applications are beyond of the scope of this review. Instead, we focus on aerosol synthesis of free-standing NPs.
Spray drying is a process similar to spray pyrolysis which is used to synthesize microspheres. The key difference between spray pyrolysis and spray drying is that spray drying is done at relatively low (often ambient) temperatures and mainly involves solvent evaporation without chemical reaction. The atomizer used in spray drying processes is most often a two-fluid nozzle.474–476 The microspheres generated by spray drying have been widely employed for energy storage applications.476–483 Colloidal spray pyrolysis and colloidal spray drying are variations of these methods in which pre-designed colloidal particles are included in the precursor to template particular nanostructures within the product micro (or nano) spheres. In colloidal spray pyrolysis, the template NPs and chemical precursors are sprayed into a tube furnace to form various nanostructured materials.484,485

Table 8 summarizes and Fig. 55 provides representative TEM images of nanomaterials that have been synthesized by spray pyrolysis.

 Fig. 55  Representative TEM images of nanomaterials synthesized by spray pyrolysis. (a) Co-doped porous SiO2 nanosphere (adapted from ref. 531 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005), (b) NiO multiroom spheres (adapted from ref. 524 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2018), (c) CNT-(NiCo)O/C microspheres (adapted from ref. 503 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2019), (d) Nanostructured MoS2 (adapted from ref. 559 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005), (e) Hierarchically porous carbon microspheres (adapted from ref. 501 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020), (f) ZnS:Ni2+ NPs (adapted from ref. 543 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2008), (g) hollow ZnS spheres (adapted from ref. 540 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2015), (h) Cu/Cu2O/CuO ternary composite hollow spheres (adapted from ref. 535 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2018), and (i) yolk@shell TiO2−x particles (adapted from ref. 507 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2019). 
2.3.2.1. Ceramic oxides. 
Ruthner pioneered the spray pyrolysis approach and was the first to commercially synthesize nanomaterials using this method. In the “Ruthner process” (Fig. 56), the aerosol droplets of metal chloride solutions are injected into a vertical furnace from the top. The furnace temperature was 700 °C to 1000 °C. The synthesized particles were delivered to a cyclone to be separated from the gas. The hydrogen chloride byproduct was separated from the exhaust gas by a wet scrubber. The overall size of the synthesized particles was in the range of 25 to 400 μm which meant that they required post-processing steps, like ball milling, to reach nanoscale dimensions.486,487 He employed this process to synthesize alkali-free Al2O3;488 MgO, MgO–Cr2O3, and MgO–Al2O3;489 and multi-component metal oxides at industrial scale.490
 Fig. 56  Schematic of the “Ruthner process”. Reproduced from ref. 487 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2010. 
Liu et al. used an ultrasonic atomizer with the spray pyrolysis technique to make spherical monodispersed zinc oxide NPs. The precursor was zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2) dissolved in methanol (CH3OH). A schematic of their experimental set-up is presented in Fig. 57. The ultrasonic atomizer produced aerosol droplets that were delivered by a mixture of air and nitrogen to a furnace at the low temperature of 200 °C because they wanted a temperature between dehydration temperature (water boiling point) of 100 °C and the decomposition temperature of the precursor solution which was 240 °C. Thus, the aerosol droplets were converted into zinc acetate particles that were delivered to a second furnace at a much higher temperature of approximately 500 °C where they were converted into zinc oxide NPs with a mean primary particle size in the range of 100–200 nm and a specific surface area of around 50 m2 g−1. Dissolving the precursor in alcohol instead of water allowed the synthesis of spherical solid particles rather than hollow shells. They found that the flow rate of carrier gas and the precursor solution concentration strongly influenced the characteristics of the final products. Increasing the flow rate of the carrier gas produced smaller NPs and increasing the precursor solution concentration generated larger quantities of ultrafine zinc oxide NPs. They considered the high vapor pressure of the precursor solution as the main reason for the synthesis of ultrafine NPs.491
 Fig. 57  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Liu et al. to synthesize ZnO NPs by spray pyrolysis. Adapted from ref. 491 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1986. 
Vanheusden et al. also synthesized zinc oxide NPs using spray pyrolysis. They thermally decomposed droplets of aqueous zinc nitrate (Zn(NO3)2) generated by an ultrasonic atomizer and delivered at a carrier gas flow rate of 10 lpm to a ceramic tube furnace with a diameter of 9 cm and length of 1.52 m, held at various temperatures in the range of 700 to 900 °C. The carrier gas was nitrogen, air, or a N2:H2 (93:7) mixture. Notably, they performed a reductive post-treatment at 800 °C for 20 min. They investigated the optical and magnetic properties of the NPs and found a strong correlation between green emission intensity at 510 nm and the concentration of paramagnetic isolated oxygen vacancies.
