Furthermore, both the intensity and the oxygen vacancy density were observed to peak at a free-carrier concentration of approximately 1.4 × 1018 cm−3, showing that the paramagnetic isolated oxygen vacancy is responsible for the green photoluminescence in their zinc oxide.492
Zhang et al. employed spray pyrolysis for synthesis of 4 mol% yttria-stabilized zirconia nanomaterials. They investigated the effect of the relative solution saturation and the type of precursor on the synthesis process. They used five different zirconium precursors with different decomposition temperatures, including zirconyl hydroxychloride (ZrO(OH)Cl), zirconyl chloride octahydrate (ZrOCl2·8H2O), zirconyl nitrate hydrate (ZrO(NO3)2·xH2O), zirconium acetate (Zr(CH3COO)4), and zirconyl hydroxyacetate (ZrO(OH)0.5(CH3COO)1.5). The yttrium precursor was yttrium nitrate hexahydrate (Y(NO3)3·6H2O). A two-fluid atomizer was employed to produce aerosol droplets that were carried into a furnace with overall length of 1.2 m and four separately-heated zones with equal lengths and temperatures of 300, 500, 750, and 750 °C, with the lowest temperature at the furnace inlet side. They could synthesize solid spherical particles with a high degree of homogeneity when the initial relative solution saturation was limited to about 0.01, which allowed them to keep the concentration of salt homogeneous before precipitation, because the droplet volume decreased dramatically before precipitation. Indeed, high solubility of the precursor was not an intrinsically important factor for the synthesis of dense nanomaterials, provided the initial relative solution saturation was low. At higher initial relative saturation, the solubility of the precursor and the oxide content play important roles in the synthesis process. They reported that the best results for the synthesis of zirconia NPs were achieved by decomposition of zirconyl hydroxide and zirconyl chloride.493 Lenggoro et al. also synthesized zirconia NPs by spray pyrolysis. They employed an ultrasonic nebulizer with a frequency of 1.75 MHz to atomize the aqueous precursor solution of zirconyl hydroxyl chloride (ZrO(OH)Cl) to form aerosol droplets of 4.5 μm mean diameter. They used an alumina tube furnace with an inner diameter of 13 mm and length of 1 m, with 5 independent heating zones of equal length. The tube furnace was oriented vertically and from the top the zones had temperatures of 100, 200, 300, 400, and 500 °C, respectively. They investigated the effect of various operating parameters on the morphology of the synthesized NPs and found that high reactor temperature, steep temperature gradient, high carrier gas flow rate, and low initial precursor concentration in the solution led to hollow particles; otherwise, solid dense particles could be synthesized. In addition, they developed a numerical simulation of the spray pyrolysis process which yielded predictions in good agreement with the experimental results.558
Rulison and Flagan synthesized yttria particles from concentrated precursor solutions of about 400 g L−1 using electrospray pyrolysis. In addition to getting higher production rate, using a concentrated precursor solution is advantageous for producing particles of higher purity due to the lower concentration of nonvolatile solvent impurities. They used yttrium nitrate pentahydrate (Y(NO3)3·5H2O) or yttrium nitrate hexahydrate (Y(NO3)3·6H2O) dissolved in in 1-propanol (C3H8O) as the precursor. It was injected into a stainless-steel capillary with a diameter of 1.57 mm as the electrode. The electrode was located 8 mm above a stainless-steel plate with a 10 mm diameter hole as the counter-electrode. They applied a potential to the electrode using a high-voltage power supply. A Taylor cone formed at a critical value of potential applied to the electrode. The potential was increased to 1.1–1.5 times the critical potential value to launch and maintain stable electrospraying. They used a SF6 gas flow to purge the top part of the atomizer to avoid corona discharge at the Taylor cone. The SF6 flow was saturated with 1-propanol to avoid evaporation of the solvent at the cone and the formation of a solute crust. They used a corona needle located beneath the counter-electrode with a high potential opposite to the potential of the electrode to deflect the charged droplets away from the counter-electrode and discharge them. When the corona needle was adjusted such that the net current to ground was negligible (below 10 nA), which produced charge-free aerosol droplets, a maximum throughput efficiency of 50% was achieved. The aerosol droplets were delivered by air as the carrier gas to an alumina tube with an inner diameter of 56 mm and length of 1.5 m in an electric furnace to be pyrolyzed. The temperature of the furnace was 550 °C and the residence time in the reaction zone was about 5.7 s. They used two concentrations of 41.1 and 441 g L−1 in the solvent for the yttrium nitrate pentahydrate and 93.4 g L−1 for yttrium nitrate hexahydrate. The average size of the synthesized particles was in the range of 170–290 nm. The type of precursor played a substantial role in controlling the particle morphology. Using yttrium nitrate pentahydrate as the precursor produced particles with a dense spheroidal morphology due to formation of a particle surface that was permeable to alcohol vapor, while a hollow inflated spherical morphology was produced when yttrium nitrate hexahydrate was the precursor, due to alcohol vapor trapped within the particle, whose surface was impermeable in this case.548
Lin et al. produced novel ternary Cu/Cu2O/CuO hollow sphere nanocomposites by spray pyrolysis. They dissolved copper nitrate hemipentahydrate (Cu(NO3)2·2.5H2O) and citric acid (C6H8O7) in DI water, and after adding ammonia (NH3), atomized the solution in a Collison nebulizer and delivered it in nitrogen to a quartz tube furnace at 850 °C, where the residence time was ∼1.2 s. Ammonia played the role of template for the formation of hollow structures. A schematic of the synthesis mechanism of these nanomaterials is shown in Fig. 58. They used these nanocomposites as non-enzymatic electrochemical biosensors for glucose detection, achieving an impressive sensitivity of 8726 μA (cm2 mM)−1 and a detection limit as low as 0.39 μM.535
 Fig. 58  Schematic of the synthesis mechanism of novel ternary Cu/Cu2O/CuO hollow sphere nanocomposites by spray pyrolysis. Reproduced from ref. 535 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2018. 
Park et al. employed a two-step process, using spray pyrolysis to generate CoSex–C nanocomposites followed by thermal oxidation in air at 300 to 500 °C, to synthesize CoSeO3 microspheres. A schematic of the synthesis mechanism is presented in Fig. 59a. In the spray pyrolysis step, they atomized an aqueous precursor solution of cobalt nitrate hexahydrate (Co(NO3)2·6H2O), selenous acid (SeO2), and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) ((C6H9NO)n) as a template for the microsphere structure. The aerosolized precursor, in argon and hydrogen carrier gas, flowed through a tube furnace at 800 °C. Fig. 59b shows elemental mapping images of the CoSex–C nanocomposites. After thermal oxidation, the microspheres were employed as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries. Metal selenides are effective in this application because of their efficient transformation into the metal selenide and oxide nanocrystals, with rapid surface reaction kinetics and charge transport provided by this heterostructure. Accordingly, they reported that the synthesized CoSeO3 microspheres retained a high reversible capacity of 709 mA h g−1 for the 1400th cycle, at a current density of 3.0 A g−1 and a reversible discharge capacity of 526 mA h g−1 at exceptionally high current density of 30 A g−1.534
 Fig. 59  (a) Schematic illustration of the two-step synthesis of CoSeO3 microspheres, and (b) elemental mapping images of CoSex–C microspheres. Adapted from ref. 534 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2019. 
Although tin oxide has high theoretical capacity as a lithium ion battery anode material, challenges including low electronic conductivity and a huge volume change during cycling limit its use. Synthesis of engineered nanostructures of such oxide materials can provide high specific surface area and improved structural stability to overcome these challenges. Accordingly, Leng et al. synthesized tin oxide–carbon nanocomposites in a novel dried plum-like yolk–shell structure using spray pyrolysis. They dissolved tin oxalate (SnC2O4) in water and added nitric acid (HNO3) to form a stable tin precursor solution. Moreover, they added polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) ((C6H9NO)n) as a template to form a carbon matrix and plum-like yolk–shell structure. They atomized the solution using an ultrasonic nebulizer with a frequency of 1.7 MHz and delivered the aerosol droplets in argon to a 3-zone vertical furnace at 750 °C. In addition, they generated tin oxide microspheres with dense and porous structure when they did not add PVP to the precursor solution, or when they added oxygen to the argon. The synthesis mechanism and SEM images of the nanomaterials with various morphologies are presented in Fig. 60. Among the synthesized nanomaterials, the SnOx/C nanocomposites with yolk–shell morphology provided the best performance as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries, with an impressive reversible discharge capacity of 1142 mA h g−1 at a current density of 0.2 A g−1 and an excellent rate capability of 509 mA h g−1, even at a current density of 5 A g−1, along with nearly 100%, capacity retention after 600 cycles.565
 Fig. 60  (a) Schematic of the synthesis mechanism of nanomaterials with various morphologies synthesized by Leng et al. through spray pyrolysis. SEM images of (b) yolk–shell SnOx/C, (c) porous SnO2, and (d) dense SnO2 particles. Adapted from ref. 565 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2019. 
2.3.2.2. Doped ceramic oxides. 
Kang et al. directly synthesized non-agglomerated spherical europium-doped yttria particles by spray pyrolysis of yttrium nitrate (Y(NO3)3) and europium nitrate (Eu(NO3)3) using an ultrasonic atomizer with a 1.7 MHz resonator. They investigated the effect of the precursor solution concentration and pyrolysis temperature on the size, morphology, crystallinity, and photoluminescence of the NPs. The overall precursor concentration was varied from 0.03–1 M in water to control the size. They delivered the aerosol droplets in nitrogen at 2 lpm to the reaction zone at 500 to 1400 °C. The corresponding residence times varied from 1.1 to 2.4 s. Synthesis at temperatures higher than 600 °C produced highly-crystalline particles, and the crystallinity increased with increasing temperature of the reaction zone. As shown in Fig. 61, increasing the concentration of the precursor solution increased the size of the particles in the range of 0.34 to 1.2 μm. Without annealing, the as-synthesized particles exhibited strong red emission at 612 nm under UV illumination, which the authors attributed to small size, non-aggregation, pure phase, and uniform dispersion of the dopant in the host lattice. Increasing the temperature of the reaction zone led to higher brightness of as-synthesized particles.550 In another study, they employed colloidal seed-assisted spray pyrolysis to produce europium doped yttria particles. They used yttrium hydroxycarbonate (C3H6O12Y) and gadolinium hydroxycarbonate (CH2GdO4) as nucleation seeds for yttrium nitrate (Y(NO3)3) and europium nitrate (Eu(NO3)3) in this approach. As illustrated in Fig. 62, using small amounts of yttrium hydroxycarbonate or gadolinium hydroxycarbonate solutions generated doped spherical particles with a dense morphology in contrast to the hollow and porous structures obtained without using the hydroxycarbonate solutions as the colloidal seeds.551
 Fig. 61  SEM images of Y2O3 particles synthesized by Kang et al. by spray pyrolysis with precursor solution concentration of (a) 0.03 M, (b) 0.3 M, and (c) 1 M. Reproduced from ref. 550 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1999. 
 Fig. 62  SEM images of (a) porous and hollow Y2O3 particles and (b) dense Y2O3 particles synthesized from (a) conventional aqueous solution and (b) 250 nm Gd colloidal solution, both after post-treatment. Adapted from ref. 551 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2000. 
Kim et al. employed ultrasonic spray pyrolysis to synthesize pristine and Sn-doped NiO. The precursor was a mixture of nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O), tin oxalate (SnC2O4), and dextrin ((C6H10O5)x·nH2O) in water. Dextrin serves as a template to form a “multiroom” nanostructure from the Sn-doped NiO after it pyrolyzes to leave large pores with thin walls. To synthesize pristine nickel oxide, they used a precursor solution without tin oxalate, and to form a dense structure they employed a solution without dextrin. Five 1.7 MHz piezoelectric transducers were used to atomize the precursor solution, and the aerosol droplets in 15 lpm N2 that flowed through a tube furnace at 400 °C. Post-annealing in air at 500 °C for 3 hours produced the final structure by removing the dextrin template. The synthesis mechanism and the SEM images of the nanomaterials are presented in Fig. 63. They used the synthesized NPs in a resistive gas sensor to detect xylene and found that the tin-doped NPs with the multiroom structure exhibited an excellent resistance ratio of 65.4 at 300 °C which was 9.0 and 50.3 times those of the doped NPs with dense morphology and pristine NPs, respectively. They proposed that the high gas accessibility provided by the unique multiroom structure and change in the oxygen adsorption, in addition to the charge carrier concentration provided by tin doping, were the main factors underlying the strong response to xylene. Moreover, they found that the Sn-doped NiO with multiroom structure exhibited a high selectivity towards xylene, due to the high catalytic activity of the tin doped NPs for decomposition of xylene within the multiroom structure.524
 Fig. 63  Synthesis mechanism and SEM images of (a) NiO multiroom spheres, (b) Sn-doped NiO multiroom spheres, and (c) dense Sn-doped NiO spheres. Adapted from ref. 524 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2018. 
2.3.2.3. Doped mixed metal oxides. 
Kang et al. studied the synthesis of cerium-doped yttrium-aluminum-garnet (YAG) by the same approach. They used aqueous nitrate precursors for yttrium, aluminum, and cerium. The yttrium and aluminum precursors were mixed in a stoichiometric ratio, while the concentration of cerium nitrate varied from 0.5 to 3 at% to achieve optimal doping that maximized photoluminescence intensity, which was found to be 1 at% of cerium. Furthermore, they reported that increasing the overall precursor concentration increased the average particle size. The particles absorbed light from 403 to 510 nm with a maximum at 470 nm, and showed a broad emission from 480 to 650 nm with a maximum at 528 nm. Increasing the annealing temperature produced a monotonic increase in the photoluminescence intensity. Moreover, they observed spherical non-agglomerated particles after annealing at 1300 °C. Indeed, annealing at lower temperatures compared to the conventional synthesis processes produced higher purity and more crystalline Y3Al5O12:Eu particles, due to the microscale confined reaction within the micron-sized aerosol droplets. Notably, they observed significant formation of hard aggregates after annealing at 1600 °C.555 They also employed the spray pyrolysis method to produce spherical fine Tb-doped Y2SiO5 particles with a narrow size distribution. The precursors were tetraethyl-orthosilicate (Si(OC2H5)4), yttrium nitrate (Y(NO3)3), and terbium nitrate (Tb(NO3)3). They performed the synthesis at temperatures in the range of 600–1200 °C and found that temperatures below 1100 °C produced particles with low crystallinity due to the short residence time of the particles in the reaction zone. However, the as-synthesized particles had high crystallinity at 1200 °C. In addition, they calcined their product at temperatures above 800 °C and observed a change in the crystalline phase for temperatures above 1400 °C. No formation of hard aggregates was observed, even for the particles calcined at 1200 °C. The as-synthesized doped particles absorbed light from 220 to 325 nm with a peak near 240 nm. The main emission peak was at 538 nm. Doping with 4 at% Tb maximized brightness. The dopant was directly activated in the hot wall reactor even at short residence times, demonstrating that calcination at high temperatures was not necessary for its activation. The photoluminescence of particles synthesized at 1200 °C was 96% of the maximum intensity achieved after calcination.553
Kang and Park synthesized spherical non-agglomerated Mn-doped Zn2SiO4 particles by spray pyrolysis using a filter expansion aerosol generator (FEAG) to atomize the precursor solution. The FEAG included a pneumatic nozzle, a vacuum pump, and a porous glass filter. The precursor solution was sprayed through the pneumatic nozzle on the porous glass filter by carrier gas. A thin film of precursor solution was generated on the surface of the porous glass filter. Subsequently, the thin film of precursor solution passed through the filter, driven by the carrier gas, and expanded into the hot-wall reactor at a low-pressure of 60 Torr and temperatures of 700–1000 °C. They performed post-calcination at temperatures of 1000, 1100, 1200, and 1300 °C to optimize crystallization and activation of the dopant. The precursors were zinc nitrate (Zn(NO3)2), tetraethyl-orthosilicate (Si(OC2H5)4), and manganese acetate (Mn(CH3COO)2). Synthesis at 600 °C produced amorphous particles, but crystalline particles were synthesized above 700 °C. Orthorhombic β-Zn2SiO4 was produced at 1000 °C. Calcination for 5 hours at 1200 °C with manganese doping of 1.3 at% produced the maximum cathodoluminescence intensity of 198 cd m−2. Using 80 to 90% of the stoichiometric amount of zinc nitrate in the precursor solution produced phase-pure material with the willemite structure.538 Lenggoro et al. employed the same strategy for direct synthesis of photoluminescent cerium- and terbium-doped lanthanum phosphate particles with green emission at 543 nm. Aqueous nitrate precursors were used, with added nitric acid (HNO3) to form a uniform solution. That solution was added to an aqueous phosphoric acid (H3PO4) solution. This final precursor solution was sprayed into the reaction zone, held at 600 to 1550 °C. They found that increasing the temperature of the reaction zone increased the crystallinity and photoluminescence intensity of the particles. They produced pure crystalline doped lanthanum phosphate at temperatures above 1000 °C, and the maximum photoluminescence intensity was observed for particles synthesized at 1500 °C. Morphologies of the commercial and as-synthesized cerium- and terbium-doped lanthanum phosphate particles are compared in Fig. 64.571
 Fig. 64  SEM images of (a) commercial and (b) as-synthesized Ce- and Tb-doped LaPO4 particles synthesized at 1550 °C. Adapted from ref. 571 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2001. 
Santiago et al. used ultrasonic spray pyrolysis for fast and simultaneous doping of Sr0.9−x−y−zCa0.1In2O4:(xEu3+,yTm3+,zTb3+) as a single-phase white phosphor containing various cations. The precursors were nitrates for the host matrix elements, including strontium nitrate (Sr(NO3)2), calcium nitrate tetrahydrate (Ca(NO3)2·4H2O), and indium nitrate (In(NO3)3), and oxide precursors for dopant elements including europium oxide (Eu2O3), terbium oxide (Tb4O7), and thulium oxide (Tm2O3). The solvent for the host precursors was water, and for water-insoluble dopant precursors was nitric acid (HNO3). A 2.4 MHz ultrasonic nebulizer generated droplets, the carrier gas was oxygen, and the thermal decomposition occurred in a tube furnace with two independent heating zones of 700 and 1050 °C. They investigated the photoluminescence characteristics for the co-doped NPs and reported that undoped Sr0.9Ca0.1In2O4 exhibited a near blue color emission, while all the co-doped materials exhibited white emission with similar chromaticity coordinates to standard white. Moreover, they hypothesized that energy transfer between various dopant ions played a significant role in producing a particular emission color.547
Park et al. synthesized NiMoO4 NPs with a yolk–shell morphology and unique hollow-yolk internal structure through ultrasonic spray pyrolysis. Nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) and ammonium molybdate tetrahydrate ((NH4)6Mo7O24·4H2O), with sucrose (C12H22O11) as a templating agent, were dissolved in water and atomized by an ultrasonic nebulizer. Upon pyrolysis in air within a tube furnace at 800 °C, the droplets were converted into yolk–shell NiMoO4 NPs. For use as high performance anode materials in lithium-ion batteries, these NPs were coated with nitrogen-doped carbon and the internal “yolk” particles were converted into hollow particles. For this, the NPs were first selenized under H2Se for 6 hours at 350 °C to produce NiSe2–MoSe2 nanocomposite yolk–shell structures. The re-oxidation of these microspheres produced overall yolk–shell microspheres. Dispersing these hierarchically-structured microspheres in tris-buffer at pH 8.7, adding dopamine hydrochloride, stirring for 12 hours, washing with DI water and ethanol, drying in a vacuum oven and finally, post-annealing under argon yielded the final N-doped C-coated NiMoO4 hollow-yolk–shell microspheres. These anode materials exhibited a high discharge capacity of 862 mA h g−1 for the 500th cycle at a current density of 3.0 A g−1 and an excellent final discharge capacity of 757 mA h g−1 at 10 A g−1. They proposed that the high lithium-ion uptake and rate capability exhibited by these anode materials resulted from synergy between the hollow nanospheres forming the yolk–shell microspheres and the N-doped C-coating layer. While multiple processing steps were required to achieve the final structure in this case, controlled formation of the overall yolk–shell structure was enabled by the spray pyrolysis process.530
2.3.2.4. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Taniguchi et al. employed ultrasonic spray pyrolysis for the synthesis of LiMn2O4 and its substituted forms LiM1/6Mn11/6O4 where M was Co, Ni, or Al. They used nitrate precursors at 800 °C, which generated spherical particles with a densely-packed interior structure, a crystallite size of around 30 nm, and specific surface areas in the range of 5.7–12.7 m2 g−1, at a production rate of 500 mg h−1. They investigated the charge/discharge properties of the NPs as cathode materials for Li-ion batteries and found that the LiMn2O4 had a first discharge capacity of 129 mA h g−1 at 0.2 mA cm−2 with more stable cycling performance compared to the same material synthesized by solid-state reaction. Among the compositions tested, LiCo1/6Mn11/6O4 had the minimum capacity loss of 2% after 30 cycles.494 In addition, they investigated the effect of temperature profile and flow rate of carrier gas on the process. Lithium nitrate (LiNO3) and manganese acetate tetrahydrate (Mn(CH3COO)2·4H2O) were dissolved in water to make the precursor solution. A schematic of the apparatus is presented in Fig. 65a. They used a tube furnace with 6 independently adjustable heating zones, wither with constant temperature at 800 °C in the tube furnace or with an axial increase in the temperature of the zones so that the temperatures were 200, 200, 400, 600, 800, and 800 °C. The flow rate of gas was also varied from 500 to 4000 sccm. As evident in Fig. 65b and c, both the flow rate of carrier gas and the axial temperature profile influenced the morphology of the particles. Decreasing the flow rate of carrier gas and the use of an increasing temperature profile resulted in dense particles with shrinkage, whereas high gas flow rates and a uniform high temperature produced hollow particles. Upon using the synthesized particles as cathode materials in lithium-ion batteries, they found that morphology, crystallite size, and specific surface area of the synthesized particles affected the discharge capacity.495 Matsuda and Taniguchi employed additional precursors for the synthesis of LiMn2O4 with various morphologies and interior structures by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis. They thermally decomposed aqueous lithium nitrate (LiNO3), lithium acetate dihydrate (Li(CH3COO)·2H2O), or lithium formate monohydrate (Li(HCOO)·H2O) with manganese nitrate hexahydrate (Mn(NO3)2·6H2O), manganese acetate tetrahydrate (Mn(CH3COO)2·4H2O), or manganese formate dihydrate (Mn(HCOO)2·2H2O) in a tube furnace at 800 °C. The as-synthesized LiMn2O4 particles had varied morphologies, including dense particles with porous surface structures, hollow particles with shrinkage-induced surface structure, hollow particles with porous surface structure, and hollow particles with smooth surface structure. In addition, they again used the particles as cathodes in lithium-ion batteries and found that the particles with larger crystallite size and smaller specific surface area exhibited better cycle performance. The dense particles were most appropriate for use as the cathode active material in lithium-ion batteries.496
 Fig. 65  (a) Schematic of the experimental set-up employed by Taniguchi et al. to synthesize LiMn2O4 particles. SEM images of the LiMn2O4 particles synthesized at (b) a constant temperature profile and (c) an increasing temperature profile with different flow rates of carrier gas (G) ranging from 0.5 to 4 lpm. Adapted from ref. 495 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2002. 
Park et al. synthesized (Ni1/3Co1/3Mn1/3)O2 powder by spray pyrolysis. They dissolved a mixture of metal nitrate hydrate precursors in water and thermally decomposed the aqueous solution in a vertical tube furnace at 500 °C. Notably, they added citric acid (C6H8O7) to the precursor solution to facilitate polymerization. Thereafter, they mixed the pyrolyzed powders with lithium hydroxide monohydrate (LiOH·H2O) and heated it to 900 °C at a rate of 1 °C min−1 to synthesize Li(Ni1/3Co1/3Mn1/3)O2 for lithium-ion battery applications. They observed that the synthesized electrode delivered a promising discharge capacity of 188 mA h g−1 in the voltage range of 2.8–4.6 V at a high current density of 20 mA g−1 with impressive cyclability.528
Lenggoro et al. synthesized nickel oxide NPs using a low-pressure spray pyrolysis approach. In their set-up (Fig. 66a), they used a two-fluid nozzle to spray the precursor solution. A carrier gas delivered the liquid to a porous glass filter surface to form a thin layer of liquid film. After passing through the pores of the glass filter, the liquid film was expanded to a liquid jet and aerosol droplets formed. These were carried into a low-pressure quartz tube furnace with an inner diameter of 38 nm and length of 800 mm, with 5 independent heating zones of equal lengths. The pressure was 10 or 40 Torr. They used nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O), nickel chloride (NiCl2), or nickel formate dihydrate (Ni(HCO2)2·2H2O) as the nickel precursor. The effect of precursor choice is presented in Fig. 66b. Only nickel nitrate produced a nanoparticulate product. In addition, they operated at temperatures of 400 to 1200 °C. As shown in Fig. 66c, increasing the temperature reduced the particle size, and at 900 °C the submicron particles were mainly converted to aggregates of ∼20 nm NPs. Furthermore, they varied the residence time in the reaction zone by changing the flow rate of carrier gas and adjusting the pressure difference between the upper and lower surface of the glass filter. Decreasing the residence time, which increased the heating rate of precursor droplets, led to breakage into smaller particles in the low-pressure spray pyrolysis reactor (Fig. 66d). Overall, they concluded that the rate of solvent evaporation, precipitate diffusion, and solubility of the precursor are the main factors that must be controlled for synthesis of non-agglomerated NPs in the low-pressure spray pyrolysis system.521 They also investigated effects of carrier gas flow rate, precursor solution concentration, pressure, and type of reducing agent on the synthesis of nickel NPs using the same low-pressure spray pyrolysis reactor. The nickel precursor was nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) and the reducing agents were ethanol (C2H5OH), formic acid (HCOOH), and hydrogen. They found the flow rate of the carrier gas and the pressure of the reaction zone were key factors governing the reduction of nickel to its metallic state and the size of nickel NPs. Decreasing the carrier gas flow rate and increasing the pressure increased the possibility of producing submicron particles rather than NPs. In addition, they observed that increasing the precursor solution concentration facilitated the synthesis of NPs, while precursor solutions with a very low concentration, like 0.15 M, yielded submicron particles. Moreover, they successfully reduced the nickel oxide to nickel using ethanol and formic acid as safer alternatives to hydrogen as a reducing agent. Furthermore, they reported that the nickel NPs synthesized by low-pressure spray pyrolysis are more prone to oxidation compared to the submicron particles synthesized by conventional spray pyrolysis, which could be explained by either their higher surface area or incomplete reduction due to decreased exposure to hydrogen (lower hydrogen partial pressure).519
 Fig. 66  (a) Schematic of the experimental set-up employed by Lenggoro et al. to synthesize NiO particles, and comparison of morphologies of NPs when (b) the precursor was changed (at 1000 °C and 40 Torr, precursor concentration: 0.15 mol l−1, carrier gas flow rate: 2 lpm), (c) the temperature was varied (precursor: nitrate, pressure: 40 Torr, precursor solution concentration: 0.15 mol l−1, carrier gas flow rate: 2 lpm), and (d) the residence time was varied (precursor: nitrate, pressure: 40 Torr, precursor concentration: 0.15 mol l−1). Adapted from ref. 521 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2003. 
Oh et al. produced crystalline transition metal oxides including nickel oxide, cobalt oxide, and copper oxide in nanoparticulate form by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis. The precursor for each was the corresponding metal nitrate hydrate dissolved in water. Citric acid (C6H8O) was added to promote polymerization, while ammonium hydroxide (NH4OH) was added to adjust the pH. After atomization by an ultrasonic nebulizer at 1.7 MHz, the precursor droplets thermally decomposed in a vertical furnace at 500 °C. The product particles were calcined at 300 to 900 °C, and increasing the calcination temperature increased the crystallite size and significantly affected the morphology of the final NPs (Fig. 67), which in turn affected their electrochemical performance as the active component of lithium-ion battery anodes. In addition, they found optimum calcination temperatures, based upon the electrochemical performance, which were 500, 700, and 500 °C for NiO, Co3O4, and CuO, respectively.522
 Fig. 67  SEM images of NiO particles synthesized by Oh et al., after calcination at various temperatures as indicated. Adapted from ref. 522 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2007. 
Kundu et al. synthesized hollow spherical NiCo2O4 nanospheres, with sizes in the range of 300 to 500 nm, by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis. The aerosol droplets of aqueous precursor solutions of nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) and cobalt nitrate hexahydrate (Cu(NO3)2·6H2O) were thermally decomposed in a furnace at 1200 °C. They used the hollow spherical particles as electrodes in lithium-ion batteries where they exhibited high capacity after 1000 cycles and in supercapacitors where they provided superior specific capacitance even after 3000 charge–discharge cycles.529
Basak et al. employed a novel electrospray pyrolysis system to synthesize iron oxide NPs. Their experimental set-up (Fig. 68) included four main units, which were electrospray, reaction, size distribution measurement, and collection. To achieve suitable aerosol flow rates to synthesize NPs of varied size, they employed two different electrospray units. The first comprised a stainless-steel capillary tube with an inner diameter of 0.16 mm which was 5 mm from the ground electrode. The second was a commercial electrospray with an inner diameter of 0.04 mm and a nebulizer inside it. To avoid generation of a corona, they applied a positive polarity to the capillary needle. The aerosol droplets of the precursor solution were delivered in air to a high-temperature tube furnace for reaction. Notably, they added carbon dioxide to the carrier gas as another way to retard the formation of a corona. A scanning mobility particle sizer was used to characterize the aerosol at the inlet and outlet of the tube furnace. The collection unit used an electrostatic sampler for deposition of particles onto TEM grids for analysis. The precursor solution was iron nitrate (Fe(NO3)3) dissolved in water or ethanol (C2H5OH) and the furnace temperature ranged from 20 to 900 °C. Pure iron oxide NPs with a minimum size of 6.8 nm and average size of 11 nm were synthesized at temperatures above 700 °C. In addition, they developed a general scaling law relating the NP size to the operating parameters. Based on data from the literature compared to the size of iron nitrate NPs synthesize by the electrospray unit, the developed scaling law appeared reliable, which facilitated selection of operating parameters to synthesize NPs of desired average size.515
 Fig. 68  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Basak et al. to synthesize iron oxide NPs. Adapted from ref. 515 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2007. 
Suh and Suslick produced silica-encapsulated cobalt from colloidal silica (SiO2) and dicobalt octacarbonyl (Co2(CO)8) as precursors, styrene (C8H8), ethylene glycol dimethacrylate (C10H14O4), 1,4-dioxane (C4H8O2), azobisisobutyronitrile (C8H12N4), and sodium dodecyl sulfate (NaC12H25SO4) as organic monomers, initiator, and surfactant, and water as solvent. A schematic of their experimental set-up is provided in Fig. 69a. The precursor mixture was atomized by a low-cost 1.7 MHz ultrasonic nebulizer from a household humidifier. The aerosol droplets were carried into a tube furnace at 200 °C to initiate polymerization. Then, the composite of silica-encapsulated cobalt and the organic polymer flowed to a second furnace at 700 °C to pyrolyze and remove the polymer. Finally, the particles were collected in water-filled bubblers. The final particles had a porous morphology that was controlled by adjusting the ratio of silica to organic monomers. A schematic of the synthesis mechanism is presented in Fig. 69b. Based upon magnetization measurements, they found the nanocomposites were resistant to oxidation in air.531
 Fig. 69  (a) Schematic of the apparatus employed by Suh and Suslick to synthesize SiO2-encapsulated Co nanospheres. (b) Formation mechanism of the SiO2-encapsulated Co nanospheres. Adapted from ref. 531 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005. 
Du et al. employed a gas-templated ultrasonic spray pyrolysis technique and subsequent calcination in air for synthesis of porous hollow cobalt oxide microspheres. The precursor was an aqueous solution of cobalt nitrate hexahydrate (Co(NO3)2·6H2O) at a fixed concentration and various concentrations of urea (CH4N2O). After atomization with a 1.7 MHz ultrasonic nebulizer, the aerosol droplets of the precursor solution entered a tube furnace at a temperature of 480 °C. The product particles were then calcined at 600 °C. The synthesized porous hollow cobalt oxide microspheres were composed of many smaller NPs. Urea decomposition into gaseous components led to gas generation within the droplets, which the authors considered formation of a gas template. The amount of gas escaping from the aerosol droplets played a key role in the formation of morphologies with varied porosity, while the urea to cobalt source ratio was important to control the size of the NPs. They used the hollow porous microspheres as anode materials in lithium ion batteries. The porosity of the microspheres and the size of NPs affected their performance in the application, and the control of porosity was advantageous to test the relationships between structure and function for the development of cycling stability of electrodes. The microspheres with the optimal porosity possessed superior first charge capacity, charge cycle performance, and high rate performance in lithium-ion batteries.533
Im et al. produced cobalt-doped zinc oxide hollow spheres by spray pyrolysis using cobalt(II) chloride (CoCl2) and zinc nitrate hexahydrate (Zn(NO3)2·6H2O) in a mixture of methanol and water, atomized by a 1.7 mHz ultrasonic nebulizer with nitrogen carrier gas at 500 °C. They then employed pseudomorphic replication to produce cobalt-nitrogen–carbon catalysts by first growing a zeolitic imidazole framework (ZIF-8) on the hollow microspheres (using ZnO in the microspheres as the Zn precursor) then pyrolyzing these in Ar at 800 °C to produce highly-porous cobalt-doped metal–nitrogen–carbon (M–NC) microspheres. A schematic of the mechanism of hollow Co–NC microsphere formation is presented in Fig. 70. They used the final products as electrocatalysts for the oxygen reduction reaction in alkaline media, achieving a high half-wave potential of 0.904 V and an impressive power density of 271 mW cm−2. Notably, this did not require activation by leaching, which is typically required to remove excess metal and activate such M–NC catalysts.537
 Fig. 70  Schematic illustration of the process of forming hollow Co-NC spheres synthesized by Im et al. by spray pyrolysis and pseudomorphic replication. Adapted from ref. 537 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
2.3.2.5. Semiconductors. 
Skrabalak and Suslick produced porous MoS2 particles using colloidal silica as a sacrificial agent in ultrasonic spray pyrolysis. The precursor mixture included ammonium tetrathiomolybdate ((NH4)2MoS4) and colloidal silica which were atomized by an ultrasonic nebulizer and delivered to a furnace at 700 °C. They then removed the silica using hydrofluoric acid (HF). For comparison, they prepared MoS2 materials by spray pyrolysis without a template and by conventional thermal decomposition. The silica template significantly increased the surface area, which varied with template size and concentration, while non-templated MoS2 particles had a surface area as low as conventionally-prepared material. SEM images of the MoS2 particles are presented in Fig. 71. In addition, they investigated the catalytic activity of the materials for thiophene (C4H4S) hydrodesulfurization, and found that the highly-porous MoS2 materials had higher catalytic activity, although the catalytic activity of the non-templated MoS2 materials from ultrasonic spray pyrolysis was also greater than that of conventional MoS2. Moreover, they reported that incorporation of cobalt into the ultrasonic spray pyrolysis-synthesized catalysts enhanced their catalytic activity to a value even greater than that of RuS2.559
 Fig. 71  SEM images of MoS2 produced by (a) conventional thermal decomposition, (b) ultrasonic spray pyrolysis without a template, (c) ultrasonic spray pyrolysis using 20 nm silica template, and (d) ultrasonic spray pyrolysis using 80 nm silica template. Adapted from ref. 559 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005. 
Suslick's group also synthesized Ni2+-doped ZnS using the same strategy. They used zinc nitrate (Zn(NO3)2), thiourea (CH4N2S), nickel nitrate (Ni(NO3)2), and colloidal silica (SiO2) in water and delivered it in argon to a tube furnace at 700 °C, which produced ZnS:Ni2+ mesoporous hollow microspheres after leaching with HF to remove the silica template. Increasing the temperature of the reaction zone from 700 to 1000 °C resulted in agglomerated ZnS:Ni2+ NPs, although without using the silica template, the synthesis at 1000 °C resulted only in micron-sized solid microspheres. The synthesis mechanisms and morphologies of mesoporous hollow ZnS:Ni2+ microspheres and ZnS:Ni2+ NPs are presented in Fig. 72. They investigated the optical properties of the particles under UV-vis illumination and found that all three morphologies exhibited broader absorbance compared to pristine zinc sulfide, due to a new energy level in the band gap arising from doping with Ni2+. Increasing the synthesis temperature led to an increase in the absorbance from doping with Ni2+ and the crystallite size of the NPs, which enhanced their photocatalytic hydrogen production performance compared to both hollow and solid microspheres. Furthermore, they found that the ultrasonic spray pyrolized NPs were more stable and active compared to particles made by the conventional co-precipitation method, illustrating the benefits of ultrasonic spray pyrolysis for producing photocatalysts.543
 Fig. 72  Synthesis mechanism, SEM and TEM images of mesoporous hollow ZnS:Ni2+ microspheres and ZnS:Ni2+ NPs synthesized by Bang et al. Adapted from ref. 543 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2008. 
Sharma et al. used spray pyrolysis to synthesize porous and hollow zinc sulfide nanospheres and microspheres using zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2O), thiourea (CH4N2S), and water as zinc precursor, sulfur precursor and solvent, respectively, at 500 to 700 °C. They added templates such as polyethylene glycol methyl ether (PEG) (CH3(OCH2CH2)nOH), polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) ((C6H9NO)n), ethylene oxide (C2H4O)/propylene oxide (C3H6O) block copolymer (Pluronic F-38), and cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) (C19H42BrN) to form zinc sulfide nanospheres and microspheres with varied internal morphology. The zinc and sulfur precursors reacted to form a bis-thiourea zinc acetate (BTZA) complex. The complex precipitated upon solvent evaporation, then decomposed to zinc sulfide upon further heating. Among the templates, PEG and Pluronic F-38, which precipitated after the complex formed, produced structures with a zinc sulfide shell and a hollow core, while the other two templates significantly interacted with the complex and zinc sulfide, such that the template and zinc sulfide remained intermixed. The templates could be pyrolyzed to form hollow or porous zinc sulfide microspheres made of many smaller nanocrystals. These relatively large and well-defined pore structures can provide high surface area and access to catalytic sites, which could make them advantageous for photocatalysis applications.540
Li et al. used spray pyrolysis to produce fluorine-doped titania particles from hexafluorotitanic acid (H2TiF6), which was nebulized and delivered to a tube furnace at 900 °C. This generated spherical F-doped TiO2 powders with doping levels of 2.76–9.40 at%, strong surface acidity, and a rough surface morphology (Fig. 73). They tested the photocatalytic activity of the particles for the both visible and UV light-driven degradation of acetaldehyde and found they had greater photocatalytic activity compared to that of commercial titania due to greater surface acidity, greater number of active sites, and oxygen vacancy creation, which were enabled by fluorine doping. Enhanced photocatalytic activity under visible light was attributable to creation of surface oxygen vacancies, rather than oxygen vacancies in the bulk, which could be a unique feature of particles produced by this method.508 In another study, they dissolved titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) and ammonium fluoride (NH4F) in water and thermally decomposed them at 800 °C in a spray pyrolysis process to produce titania particles co-doped with nitrogen and fluorine at 0.10–0.26 and 0.11–0.40 at%, respectively. Doping level was controlled by adjusting the decomposition temperature. The synthesis mechanisms and morphologies of the co-doped titania particles and pristine titania particles are compared in Fig. 74. The co-doped particles were highly-porous and spherical with high acidity, which improved their photocatalytic activity. They suggested that gas evolution from ammonium fluoride decomposition and incorporation of ammonium chloride (NH4Cl) as a template generated the highly-porous nitrogen- and fluorine-co-doped titania particles.509
 Fig. 73  SEM images of (a) prisitine and (b) F-doped titania particles synthesized by Li et al. Adapted from ref. 508 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2005. 
 Fig. 74  Synthesis mechanism and SEM images of (a) F- and N-co-doped and (b) pristine titania NPs synthesized by Li et al. Adapted from ref. 509 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005. 
Zhao et al. produced silver/titania nanocomposites by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis of aqueous silver nitrate (AgNO3) and commercially-available titania NPs. Precursor droplets with a mean diameter of 1.8 μm were produced by a 2.4 MHz ultrasonic nebulizer and delivered in nitrogen into a diffusion dryer to evaporate water and concentrate silver nitrate on the surface of titania NPs, then introduced into a horizontal quartz tube furnace at 550 °C for thermal decomposition. They used the nanocomposites for simultaneous photocatalytic hydrogen production and carbon dioxide reduction and compared the results with those of nanocomposites synthesized by a conventional wet-impregnation method and with those of bare titania NPs. They found that 2 wt% silver in the nanocomposite yielded the best photocatalytic activity, which was 6 times that of nanocomposites synthesized by wet-impregnation and 60 times that of the bare titania NPs. They suggested that this superior photocatalytic activity could be related to the better dispersion of silver on the surface of titania, the synthesis of smaller silver NPs, and greater production of metallic silver species.511
Chen et al. produced anatase titania microspheres with a yolk@shell morphology by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis followed by annealing in air. An air flow delivered droplets of the aqueous precursor solution, containing titanium(IV) bis (ammonium lactato) dihydroxide (C6H22N2O8Ti) and sucrose (C12H22O11), into a quartz tube furnace at 900 °C. The resulting particles were microspheres with titania as shell and a composite of titania/carbon as yolk. Subsequent annealing in air at 600 °C converted the products to yolk@shell anatase titania microspheres. They reported that annealing in a mixture of air and hydrogen rather than pure air yielded TiO2−x microspheres with a yolk@shell morphology (Fig. 55i). These final products were applied for sodium storage in sodium-ion batteries with a high reversible capacity and significant long-term stability. In addition, the introduction of oxygen vacancies into anatase titania reduced the sodium ion insertion barrier, facilitated the diffusion kinetics of sodium cations, and enhanced electrical conductivity, which made the synthesized materials advantageous for sodium-ion battery applications.507
Kuai et al. employed spray pyrolysis to synthesize pristine titania and titania-supported palladium, with the Pd in the form of single atoms (Pd1), NPs (PdNPs), and a mixture of single atoms and NPs (Pd1+NPs) and tested their catalytic activity for room temperature ketone and aldehyde hydrogenation. In general, they used an aqueous precursor solution of tetrabutyl titanate (Ti(C4H9O)4), palladium nitrate (Pd(NO3)2), hydrochloric acid (HCl), and pluronic F-127 ((C3H6O·C2H4O)x) and sprayed it into a tube furnace at 600 °C. They calcined the products in air at 400 °C then washed them to obtain the final catalysts. To synthesize Pd1+NPs, they used more palladium nitrate than was used to make Pd1. To produce the PdNPs catalyst, they reduced the palladium nitrates to palladium NPs by flowing ultrahigh purity hydrogen through the precursor for 5 minutes at room temperature before using it in spray pyrolysis. They also synthesized mesoporous titania by the same process without adding palladium nitrate, as a basis for comparison of catalytic activity. They reported enhanced catalytic activity of Pd1+NPs\TiO2 compared to the TiO2, Pd1\TiO2, and PdNPs\TiO2 catalysts, which they attributed to the activation of CO groups by Pd1 sites and H2 dissociation on PdNPs.510
Konarava and Taniguchi combined ultrasonic spray pyrolysis with subsequent ball milling and heat treatment to create carbon-coated LiFePO4 particles. They thermally decomposed aqueous aerosol droplets containing lithium formate monohydrate (Li(HCOO)·H2O), iron chloride tetrahydrate (FeCl2·4H2O), and phosphoric acid (H3PO4) in a tube furnace at 500 °C, then sintered the product particles at 600 °C, and performed ball milling followed by a secondary sintering at 500 °C, which produced the final carbon-coated products of relatively small size. They investigated the performance of the particles in lithium-ion battery applications and found that the carbon coated particles had superior rate capability and improved discharge capacity.497
Dunkle et al. synthesized nanostructured BiVO4 particles with unique morphologies from thin hollow and porous shells to ball-in-ball morphologies by a scalable ultrasonic spray pyrolysis technique. The precursor solution, prepared by dissolving bismuth nitrate pentahydrate (Bi(NO3)3·5H2O) and ammonium metavanadate (NH4VO3) in dilute nitric acid (HNO3), was atomized by a piezoceramic transducer at 1.65 MHz and carried by air into a tube furnace at 700 °C. They suggested that the primary formation of an oxide shell on the surface of small droplets followed by gas formation within the shell (from both solvent evaporation and precursor mixture decomposition) produced these unique morphologies. The synthesis mechanism and various morphologies of the BiVO4 particles are presented in Fig. 75. They investigated the oxygen evolution reaction photocatalysis activity of the nanostructured particles and compared it to the commercial BiVO4 and WO3, demonstrating enhanced photocatalytic activity for the ultrasonic spray pyrolysis synthesized BiVO4 probably because of the shorter diffusion length of electron–hole pairs to reach the shell surface.573
 Fig. 75  Formation mechanism and SEM images of BiVO4 particles with unique morphologies from thin hollow and porous shells to ball-in-ball structures synthesized by Dunkle et al. Adapted from ref. 573 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2009. 
Huang et al. employed ultrasonic spray pyrolysis to generate porous spherical Bi2WO6 particles. To make the precursor solution, they first dissolved bismuth citrate (BiC6H5O7) and tungstic acid (H2WO4) separately in concentrated ammonia (NH3OH), then mixed them and, finally, diluted the solution with deionized water. The presence of citrate anions as capping agents along with in situ formation of carbon residues as temporary templates resulted in a porous morphology. They employed temperatures of 400, 500, and 600 °C and found a strong influence of temperature on the morphology. The porous particles were highly crystalline which produced fewer traps and resulted in superior photocatalytic activity for NO removal under both visible and simulated solar irradiation compared to bulk Bi2WO6.574
To address the drawbacks of using silicon NPs as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries, such as large volume change during their alloying/dealloying reaction, Park et al. produced three-dimensional Si-CNT composite microspheres by spray pyrolysis and carbon-coated them using pitch as the carbon source, through a pitch infiltration process (Fig. 76). For spray pyrolysis, they dispersed silicon NPs along with polystyrene ((C8H8)n) nanobeads and acid-treated MWCNT in a mixture of nitric acid (HNO3) and sulfuric acid (H2SO4) at 70 °C in distilled water and atomized the precursor with device using six 1.7 MHz transducers. Nitrogen carried the aerosol through a quartz tube furnace at 700 °C. After impregnation of pitch powder dissolved in tetrahydrofuran (THF) ((CH2)4O) into the spray pyrolysis products, they performed a thermal treatment under argon for 3 hours at 900 °C to produce the final anode nanomaterials with excellent electrochemical properties arising from the stable adhesion of silicon NPs and carbon nanotube backbones, the stable SEI layer formed during cycling, and fast electron transport pathways. The resulting anode provided a high reversible discharge capacity of 1209 mA h g−1 at a current density of 1 A g−1 after 200 cycles, which was much higher than the discharge capacity of 51 mA h g−1 obtained using Si-CNT nanocomposites synthesized by the same strategy, but without carbon-coating via pitch impregnation.504
 Fig. 76  Schematic of the formation mechanism of three-dimensional porous pitch-derived carbon-coated Si-CNT composite microspheres synthesized by Park et al. using spray pyrolysis followed by a pitch infiltration process. Reproduced from ref. 504 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
Yang et al. synthesized CoTe2–C composite microspheres using spray pyrolysis. To make the precursor solution, they first dissolved tellurium oxide (TeO2) in nitric acid (HNO3) and then added water to yield a clear solution after 30 min stirring. Then, they added cobalt nitrate (Co(NO3)2), sucrose (C12H22O11), and a small amount of water. Aerosol droplets of the precursor were delivered in argon and hydrogen at a flow rate of 5 lpm and thermally decomposed in a tube furnace at 550 °C. Post-annealing at 300 °C under an argon/hydrogen atmosphere yielded CoTe2–C composite microspheres with uniform composition and CoTe2 as the only crystalline phase present. They applied these composite microspheres as an anode material for potassium-ion batteries, achieving a discharge capacity of 189.5 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g−1. Notably, they performed in situ and ex situ measurements to investigate the conversion reaction mechanism of the composite microspheres for potassium-ion storage and demonstrated a reversible reaction mechanism from the second cycle of the reaction of cobalt telluride with potassium ions.575
2.3.2.6. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Skrabalak and Suslick synthesized mesoporous carbon particles with pore sizes from 2 to 20 nm and macroporous carbon particles with pore sizes above 20 nm using ultrasonic spray pyrolysis of various alkali metal chloroacetate salts, including lithium chloroacetate (C2H2ClLiO2), sodium chloroacetate (C2H2ClNaO2), potassium chloroacetate (C2H2ClKO2), lithium dichloroacetate (C2HCl2LiO2), sodium dichloroacetate (C2HCl2NaO2), and potassium dichloroacetate (C2HCl2KO2). These precursors can readily be decomposed to generate a highly-cross-linked carbon network by the in situ formation of inorganic salts that serve as temporary pore templates that were subsequently dissolved in water. Interestingly, these structurally similar precursors produced a variety of morphologies (Fig. 77). They considered four possible relevant processes upon heating aerosol droplets of this type of precursors including: (a) melting of the precursor, (b) electrochemical decomposition of the precursor to release alkali metal chloride, (c) endothermic decarboxylation of the precursor, and (d) melting of the alkali metal chloride part of the precursor. Based on this relative order and kinetics of these processes, the final morphologies were different for these precursors with similar structures. The generation of discrete atomized droplets limited the crystal growth and consolidation of alkali metal chloride in a manner governed by the precursor solution concentration and the residence time in the reaction zone. This facilitated better dispersion of alkali metal chloride throughout the network of growing carbon. The varied pore structures and surface area ranges of these mesoporous and macroporous carbon particles could make them appropriate for application as adsorbents and gas-storage materials.499
 Fig. 77  SEM images of porous carbon particles produced by Skrabalak and Suslick using: (a) lithium chloroacetate, (b) sodium chloroacetate, (c) potassium chloroacetate, (d) lithium dichloroacetate, (e) sodium dichloroacetate, and (f) potassium dichloroacetate. Adapted from ref. 499 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2006. 
Jung et al. synthesized hierarchical porous carbon (HPC) with mesopores and macropores surrounded by outer micropores using ultrasonic spray pyrolysis of an aqueous precursor containing sucrose (C12H22O11) as the carbon source and sodium carbonate (Na2CO3) as a base catalyst for thermal decomposition of the carbon source. They used an ultrasonic atomizer with 6 droplet outlets and a frequency of 1.7 MHz and a tube furnace with a temperature of 800 °C for the thermal decomposition. Though the residence time of their system was only around 5 s, two distinct pore formation mechanisms were observed. The inner mesopore and macropore formation was meditated by water-soluble salt biproducts, while the generation of gases such as H2O, CO, and CO2 during the thermal decomposition of the carbon source or the removal of the sodium salt made from the chelated compound between the carbon source and the base catalyst or their complex led to the formation of the outer micropores. Adjusting the ratio of carbon source to base catalyst in the precursor solution played an important role in controlling the sizes of mesopores and macropores. They tested these particles for lithium–sulfur battery applications by incorporating sulfur in their structure, which enabled current delivery of 1412 mA h gsulfur−1 by the sulfur electrode along with outstanding capacity retention of 77% after 500 cycles.500
Kim et al. also employed spray pyrolysis to synthesize HPC without using heavy metal salts. They employed sodium chloride (NaCl) as the template to make the hierarchical porous nanomaterials, sucrose (C12H22O11) as the carbon source, and sulfuric acid (H2SO4) as the catalyst for dehydrogenation of sucrose. They atomized the aqueous precursor solution and delivered it in nitrogen to a quartz tube furnace at 900 °C. They annealed the product particles for 3 hours under argon at 800 °C. To remove NaCl from the products, they washed them with distilled water, collected the product by filtration, and dried it under vacuum. A schematic of the synthesis mechanism of NaCl-activated carbon microspheres is presented in Fig. 78. They also generated carbon microspheres by the same strategy without adding NaCl. They applied the synthesized nanomaterials in electrical double-layer capacitors and found that HPC nanomaterials exhibited enhanced energy and power densities, while microporous carbon without the hierarchical structure did not perform well at high rate. A high specific surface area of 1704 m2 g−1 and pore volume of 1.81 cm3 g−1 resulted from interconnected micro/mesoporous network that provided ion transport channels with low ionic resistance and a short diffusion length. The HPC supercapacitor electrodes provided specific capacitance of 102 F g−1 at a high current density of 30 A g−1. Moreover, they provided capacity retention of 92% after 10000 cycles at a current density of 10 A g−1.501
 Fig. 78  Schematic of the synthesis mechanism of NaCl-activated carbon microspheres produced by spray pyrolysis and post-treatment. Reproduced from ref. 501 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
Oh et al. produced hierarchical CNT-(NiCo)O/C with a yolk@shell morphology by ultrasonic spray pyrolysis as illustrated in Fig. 79. The aqueous precursor solution contained nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) as nickel precursor, cobalt nitrate hexahydrate (Co(NO3)2·6H2O) as cobalt precursor, poly(vinylpyrrolidone) (PVP) ((C6H9NO)n) as a polymeric agent, polystyrene ((C8H8)n) nanobeads as a template, and carbon nanotubes (CNT) as the core source. The tube furnace temperature was 700 °C. During thermal decomposition, the frame of the yolk with a link to CNTs was generated by mutual binding of the CNTs and polystyrene nanobeads followed by selective decomposition of the nanobeads. Phase separation of the nanobeads led to the generation of hollow shells. They investigated the electrochemical properties of the materials by testing them as anode materials in lithium-ion batteries and found that the microspheres exhibited superior reversible discharge capacity due to their high electrical conductivity provided by their unique structure.503
 Fig. 79  Mechanism of formation of hierarchical CNT-(NiCo)O/C microspheres with a yolk@shell morphology synthesized by Oh et al. Adapted from ref. 503 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2019. 
2.3.2.7. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Wang et al. used laser diffraction measurements to study the effect of operating parameters including spray volume rate, carrier gas flow rate, precursor temperature, and physicochemical properties of precursors in detail on the aerosol size distribution and the average size of aerosol droplets made by an ultrasonic generator with a frequency of 1.7 MHz. They used nickel nitrate hexahydrate (Ni(NO3)2·6H2O) and zirconyl nitrate dihydrate (ZrO(NO3)2·2H2O) dissolved in water or ethanol, or the solvents alone, as prototype precursor solutions in a recirculating, temperature-controlled precursor delivery system. They found that increasing the precursor temperature and concentration along with addition of ethanol decreased the average size of aerosol droplets, while increasing the flow rate of carrier gas and the volume rate of spray not only increased the average size of aerosol droplets, but also produced a bimodal aerosol size distribution that they attributed to the onset of coagulation. In addition, they found critical values of viscosity for atomization and of aerosol droplet number concentration for coagulation, which were 2 (mN s) m−2 and 3 × 1012 droplets per m3, respectively. They then used a T-type impactor and a cyclone to remove the larger droplets and control the size distribution and average size of aerosol droplets. They introduced the precursors into a vertical tube furnace with five separately heated zones at 200, 300, 400, 500, and 600 °C to expand their investigation on the size distribution of the NPs from ultrasonic spray pyrolysis and found significant influence of the aerosol droplet size characteristics that correlated well with expectations based on droplet-to-particle conversion.523
Zhao et al. employed spray pyrolysis to synthesize zinc oxide NPs with average sizes ranging from 20 to 30 nm from an aqueous solution of zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2O). They studied the thermal decomposition of the zinc precursor using differential thermal analysis (DTA) and thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) and found that water was removed from the zinc precursor at temperatures below 200 °C, the anhydrous precursor melted in the range of 210 °C to 250 °C, and fully decomposed into zinc oxide at approximately 400 °C. They investigated the decomposition mechanism of the anhydrous precursor and inferred that a random nucleation mechanism best matched their observations. In addition, they developed a random nucleation model for the synthesis which was able to predict the morphology of the NPs and describe the influence of the reaction temperature on the properties of the NPs.576
Lenggoro et al. employed a temperature-graded laminar flow spray pyrolysis reactor to synthesize zirconia NPs by thermal decomposition of zirconyl hydroxide chloride (ZrO(OH)Cl). They developed a model of droplet evaporation and precursor precipitation, then employed both experimental and numerical methods to investigate the effects of the operating parameters on the morphology of the synthesized NPs. They found that solid dense NPs were generated at low reactor temperature or with a gradual increase in reaction temperature along the reactor, high concentration of precursor solution, and low carrier gas flow rate. Based upon the simulation results, they found that the low number concentration of aerosol droplets and large aerosol droplets favored the synthesis of hollow NPs. Beyond rationalizing these expected trends, they were able show how solute concentration profiles within droplets that cross the equilibrium solubility limit (above it at the surface, below it in the center) lead to formation of hollow particles.558
Eslamian et al. also developed a mathematical model to predict the morphology of NPs synthesized in a spray pyrolysis reactor. Their model considered non-continuum effects on the evaporation of the aerosol droplets by combining the continuum based governing equations with an interpolation formula developed from a numerical solution of the Boltzmann equation for the transition regime. These non-continuum effects were found to be significant for evaporation of submicron aerosol droplets at ambient pressure. They considered the thermal decomposition of zirconyl hydroxide chloride (ZrO(OH)Cl) to produce zirconia NPs as a case study and found that for high reactor temperatures, near 400 °C, the NPs had a hollow morphology due to the evaporation rate exceeding the rate of diffusion of precursor within the aerosol droplets, which produced steep intradroplet concentration gradients. Moreover, they found that decreasing the pressure below 250 Torr, or decreasing the size of aerosol droplets from several microns to sub-microns favored the synthesis of NPs with a solid dense morphology due to a decrease in evaporation rate as non-continuum effects became more important. In addition, they reported that increasing the temperature of the reactor increased the size of product NPs, while reactor temperatures below 200 °C facilitated the synthesis of smaller dense NPs. They also investigated the effects of reactor temperature and pressure, and the initial concentration of the precursor solution and found that the normalized size of NPs and the wall thickness are not substantially affected by the reactor pressure, but absolute wall thickness did change with pressure. Notably, the initial concentration of the precursor solution had a weak effect on the wall thickness of NPs.577
Emil et al. performed an experimental and theoretical study to regulate the morphology of zinc oxide NPs synthesized by spray pyrolysis of zinc nitrate hexahydrate (Zn(NO3)2·6H2O). They investigated the effects of operating parameters such as the precursor concentration, carrier gas flow rate, and reaction temperature on the morphologies of the synthesized NPs. Based on the experimental and modelling results, they found that various morphologies from spherical NPs to sphere-like structures assembled from nanoplates and interwoven nanoplates were attainable by operating the spray pyrolysis system under appropriate conditions. Notably, they performed a precise crystallographic study on the zinc oxide NPs with unique planar morphology through XRD and Williamson–Hall analysis.578
Among three main steps in the ultrasonic spray pyrolysis technique, including the generation of aerosol droplets by the nebulizer, delivery of the aerosol droplets into the reaction zone, and the coupled physicochemical processes in the reaction zone, Muñoz et al. focused on the first step. They performed experimental and theoretical studies to relate the precursor properties to the distribution of aerosol droplet sizes. They were interested in hexamethyldisilazane ([(CH3)3Si]2NH) as a precursor for Si/C/N nanocomposites, and therefore studied droplet formation from water, ethanol, and water/methanol mixtures with properties similar to hexamethyldisilazane. The aerosol droplet distribution was determined by a shadowgraphy analysis, which revealed a bimodal size distribution of aerosol droplets. Moreover, they concluded that partial evaporation of the smallest aerosol droplets and the possible coalescence of some aerosol droplets during their delivery to the furnace, because of the flow deflection, resulted in evolution of the aerosol droplet size distribution downstream of the ultrasonic nebulizer. Finally, they tested and improved correlations for prediction of droplet size in from ultrasonic nebulization.579
Kastrinaki et al. investigated the effects of various operating parameters including reaction temperature, flow rate, carrier gas, post-calcination temperature, and characteristics of the precursor solution including concentration, chemistry, type of precursor, and type of solvent on the morphology, crystallinity, average size, and iron oxidation state of iron- and iron oxide-based NPs synthesized by spray pyrolysis. Iron chloride hexahydrate (FeCl3·6H2O), iron nitrate nonahydrate (Fe(NO3)3·9H2O), and a water-dispersible gel derived from a steel industry byproduct were employed as precursors. Aqueous solutions of iron chloride and iron nitrate were sprayed at four concentrations. Using iron nitrate as the precursor led to spherical NPs, while the chloride precursor produced irregularly shaped NPs. They suggested that the effect of the vapor pressure of precursors on the surface evaporation at the single-droplet level led to the observed difference in morphology. Moreover, they found that higher flow rates of the carrier gas yielded hollow NPs, while a solid dense morphology was achieved at lower flow rates that allowed slower solvent evaporation. They employed models from the literature to calculate the size of NPs and online measurement at the outlet of the reactor to find the average size of NPs experimentally. They reported that the solubility of the precursor plays a significant role in the average size of NPs; precursors with low solubility favored the synthesis of larger NPs with a hollow morphology. Furthermore, they found that addition of ethanol to the precursor generated a shell of carbon and/or iron carbide on the iron oxide core NPs. In addition, the oxidation state of the NPs could be tuned by adjusting the composition of spraying and post-calcination gas. Post-calcination under hydrogen at 400 °C followed by cooling to ambient conditions under hydrogen produced metallic iron particles. Notably, employing the dispersible gel derived from a steel company byproduct as the precursor, in a solvent mixture of ethanol and water, favored the formation of iron NPs with amorphous iron oxide and carbon passivation layers.580
Chellali et al. used APT to investigate the distribution of constituent elements in high entropy oxides (HEOs) at the atomic scale. They synthesized three classes of HEOs by spray pyrolysis, including (Co0.2Cu0.2Mg0.2Ni0.2Zn0.2)O as a transition metal based HEO, (Ce0.2La0.2Pr0.2Sm0.2Y0.2)O2−δ as a rare earth based HEO, and (Gd0.2La0.2Nd0.2Sm0.2Y0.2)(Co0.2Cr0.2Mn0.2Fe0.2Ni0.2)O3 as a perovskite HEO with rocksalt, fluorite, and orthorhombic type crystalline structures, respectively. They compacted the as-synthesized NPs under high pressure (6 GPa) to make pellets and found that all three classes of HEOs retained their phase-purity even after compaction. In conventional APT, the samples are prepared by high temperature sintering which is problematic because of temperature mediated phase transitions. The high-pressure compaction route was advantageous to solve this problem. Using this technique, they were able to demonstrate the homogeneous distribution of the cations at the atomic level in all the three classes of HEO systems.575 This paper is most notable for its application of APT to aerosol synthesized materials, which demonstrated the potential of aerosol methods to produce such complex materials with uniform elemental distribution.


2.3.3. Flame-based aerosol synthesis. 
In flame-based synthesis methods, chemical energy is converted to thermal energy in the flame. Like laser and plasma methods, this initially heats only the gas phase, and does not require heat conduction from the surroundings, which enables much faster heating and cooling rates than in CVC or spray pyrolysis. Generally, easy synthesis of simple oxides at short residence times, demonstrated scale up for oxide nanomaterials, simple and relatively inexpensive equipment, and high purity are advantages of flame synthesis, while hard agglomeration, broad particle size distribution, non-uniform temperature and velocity profiles, and difficulty in producing non-oxide materials are disadvantages of the flame-based synthesis methods.175 In general, flame-based aerosol synthesis methods are classified as Vapor-fed Aerosol Flame Synthesis (VAFS) methods and Liquid-fed Aerosol Flame Synthesis (LAFS) methods, as discussed in the following paragraphs.581
VAFS typically involves the combustion of a low-cost volatile metal precursor, such as a metal chloride or metal alkoxide, which is supplied as a vapor. VAFS can use a hydrocarbon, hydrogen, or halide flame. Decomposition of precursors into reactive fragments or atoms induces homogeneous nucleation from the gas phase and the formation of particles. Thereafter, the particles grow by surface reaction, coagulation, and coalescence into larger particles.175,581,582 Simple oxides like Al2O3,583,584 SiO2,583–585 TiO2,583–586 GeO2,584 Fe2O3,584 ZrO2,584 and V2O5,584 carbon-coated oxides like SiO2/C,587 and TiO2/C,588 and noble metal/metal oxide composites including Pt/TiO2,589 and Cu/ZnO/Al2O3590 are examples of nanomaterials that can be synthesized by VAFS methods. Advantages of VAFS methods are that no solvent is needed for the precursor, and they are relatively easy to operate and scale up. The main drawback of VAFS methods is the limited availability of volatile precursors at reasonable cost.581
The use of non-volatile precursors in LAFS methods endows them with more versatility than VAFS methods to produce NPs of a wide variety of materials. In these approaches, a liquid precursor solution, emulsion, or slurry is sprayed into a flame. If the precursor evaporates completely, the formation mechanism of nanomaterials is similar to that in VAFS methods. Otherwise, hollow, shell-like, and micron-sized particles will be produced by droplet-to-particle conversion in the flame.
Based on the combustion conditions, LAFS methods are divided into two subgroups: Flame Assisted Spray Pyrolysis (FASP) and Flame Spray Pyrolysis (FSP).581 When the solution of inorganic precursor has a low combustion enthalpy content (less than 50% of total combustion energy), or is endothermic in nature (typically an aqueous solution) an external flame, which is usually an oxy-hydrogen or oxy-hydrocarbon flame, is required because the precursor itself cannot produce a self-sustaining flame. Such a process is called Flame Assisted Spray Pyrolysis (FASP).581,582 TiO2,591 ZnO,592 BaTiO3,593 SrMnO3,594 NiMn2O3,594 La1−xCexCoO3,595 La(Co, Mn, Fe)O3,596,597 Eu3+ doped Y2O3,598–602 and Eu2+ doped BaMgAl10O17603,604 have been synthesized by this approach. Advantages of FASP include the ability to use aqueous solutions of low-cost metal nitrates and sulfates as precursors and the ability to produce NPs of complex composition (containing 3 or more metals). The tendency to produce submicron particles with inhomogeneous size and morphology, at relatively low yield, because of insufficient energy available to form metal vapor, can be considered the major disadvantage of FASP.582
When the solution of organic precursor, also in liquid form, has a high combustion enthalpy content (higher than 50% of total combustion energy) only a small pilot flame is required to initiate and sustain the spray combustion. This process is called flame spray pyrolysis (FSP).581,582 FSP is the newest flame-based synthesis method and it has a great number of advantages. Employing a self-sustaining flame, using fewer volatile precursors, achieving high flame temperature with large temperature gradients (heating and cooling rates), and the ability to scale up its production rate are some advantages of the method. Its products have applications in areas such as catalysis, optics and photonics, medicine, and fuel cells.582 MgAl2O4,605 Al2O3,606 Mn2O3,606 ZrO2,606–609 Y2O3-stabilized ZrO2,606 Ta2O5,610 Y3Al5O12,611 BaCO3,612 Fe2O3,613,614 SiO2,615 Pt/SnO2,616 Au/TiO2,617 Pt/Ba/Al2O3,618 and Pt/Ba/CexZr1−xO2619 are some of the nanomaterials that have been synthesized by this method. The limited NP compositions, mainly metal oxides, that can be produced under open flames in ambient conditions is the major disadvantage of FSP.620

Table 9 summarizes, and Fig. 80 provides representative TEM images of, materials synthesized by flame-based methods.

2.3.3.1. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Flame-based aerosol technology has a long history and was employed even in prehistoric times.621 Flame-based production of carbon black, first by the lampblack process, then by the channel black process, and later by the furnace black process has been a commercial enterprise for centuries.622 Unwanted soot formation has long been a topic of interest as well. Nevertheless, Bonne, Homann, Grewer and Wagner can be considered pioneers of the scientific understanding of this field for using a novel approach to studying formation of carbon NPs in premixed flat flames.623,624 A schematic of their flame reactor is shown in Fig. 81. They employed flat flames burning on cooled porous plate burners at pressures of 10 to 60 Torr. The flames used oxygen as the oxidant with fuels such as methane (CH4), acetylene (C2H2), ethylene (C2H4), propane (C3H8), benzene (C6H6), ethanol (C2H5OH), and a mixture of carbon disulfide and hydrogen (CS2:H2). Liquid fuels were vaporized by a flow of nitrogen, while the gases directly came from cylinders. They used a sensitive mass spectrometer to monitor stable products, free radicals, and other short-lived species to find the concentration profiles of carbon-containing species before and during the production of carbon NPs (soot). A quartz probe was directly connected to the ion source of a mass spectrometer to take samples. A stream of gas from the flame was extracted into the mass spectrometer through a 100 μm hole in the probe under critical flow conditions. High-speed pumping behind the probe expanded the entering beam rapidly. They minimized the wall influence on the NPs arriving at the mass spectrometer using a slit at the middle of the beam to allow only the center portion of the stream of NPs to enter the mass spectrometer. Using this approach, they found that the formation of polyacetylenes was the key factor in the formation of carbon NPs. The polyacetylenes reached a maximum concentration early in the reaction zone and approached a constant final value in the burned gas, which increased with increasing fuel content. A short distance behind the oxidation zone, particles with a diameter near 4 nm could be collected and imaged by electron microscopy. The agglomeration of the ultrafine particles along with the addition of high polyacetylenes and polyacetylene radicals, in the region where the concentration of the polyacetylenes decreased, led to an increase in the size and decrease in the number density of carbon NPs. Finally, radicals were converted to the final products and the amount of carbon NPs, the average size of NPs and the particle number remained constant, while acetylene and polyacetylenes remained at relatively high concentration in the final products.623,624 The study of soot formation has since grown into an entire scientific field of its own, which has many aspects in common with vapor-phase synthesis of other nanomaterials, but also many unique features. Thus, we do not review soot formation in detail here, but refer the reader to several classic and more recent reviews.
