We do include some discussions of soot modelling in later subsections, because of the direct relevance to modelling of aerosol dynamics of particle synthesis.625,626
 Fig. 81  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Bonne et al. to synthesize carbon NPs through flame-based aerosol synthesis. Adapted from ref. 624 with permission from Walter De Gruyter and Company, Copyright 1960. 
2.3.3.2. Semiconductors. 
Chung and Katz developed a rectangular counterflow diffusion flame burner for the synthesis of silica NPs from silane (SiH4). A schematic of their synthesis set-up is presented in Fig. 82. The burner consisted of two opposing vertical conduits of rectangular cross-section, from which fuel and oxidant flowed, meeting in a stagnation plane. The fuel and the oxidant were hydrogen and oxygen, both diluted in argon. The fuel flowed upward, while the oxidant flowed downward and combustion products flowed outward. Stainless-steel plates with a thickness of 40 μm divided each tube into three channels: two side channels with dimensions of 12.70 mm × 6.35 mm and a central main channel with dimensions of 12.70 mm × 63.50 mm. To force the combustion gases to exit only via the long sides, they fitted fused silica plates to the outside of the two side channels. A bed of glass beads, several fiberglass layers, a perforated plate, and three stainless-steel 40 mesh wire screens were employed to create a uniform gas flow across the burner outlets. To seal the system and maintain the flow of outward gas parallel to the surfaces of the burner, they fitted flanges on each side of the burner openings. As a result, the produced flame was stable, flat, and rectangular in shape. The concentrations and the temperatures were uniform across the horizontal plane. The flame color was light blue before the addition of silane into the fuel and it changed to a whitish light yellow after the addition of silane. The flame brightness increased with increasing silane flow rate. Upon addition of 9.3 sccm of silane, a second thin orange flame appeared on the fuel side of the H2–O2 flame zone. There was a dark space between the two flames which corresponded to an intense peak in light scattering. In addition, they found that homogeneous nucleation occurred to produce a high particle concentration upon injection of silane at a flow rate beyond a critical value of 2.3 sccm.641 Hung and Katz employed this system to produce crystalline rutile titania NPs coated with an amorphous layer of silica, which were promising for catalysis applications. For this, titanium tetrachloride and silicon tetrachloride were decomposed in hydrogen–oxygen flames. Moreover, they studied the effect of the flame temperature and silicon to titanium precursor ratio on the morphology of the nanocomposites, finding that flame temperature had the greatest effect on the morphology. Titania NPs were encapsulated by silica at low temperatures and high silicon to titanium ratios, while discrete silica particles covered the titania NPs at high temperatures and low silicon to titanium ratios (Fig. 83).651
 Fig. 82  Schematic of the apparatus developed by Chung and Katz to synthesize SiO2 NPs. Adapted from ref. 641 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1985. 
 Fig. 83  TEM images of (a) TiO2 coated with silica synthesized at low flame temperature and high Si to Ti ratio and (b) SiO2 attached to TiO2 synthesized at high flame temperature and low Si to Ti concentration ratio. Adapted from ref. 651 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1992. 
Hurd and Flower studied the structure of flame-made silica NPs by in situ static and dynamic light scattering and ex situ TEM imaging. Liquid hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2), methane, and oxygen were the silicon precursor, fuel, and oxidant, respectively. Growth and aggregation of the particles evolved with position in the flame. Based on scattering measurements, the fractal dimension of the aggregates was about 1.49, although it could not be determined precisely. Based on the dynamic light scattering results for polydispersity, they found that the growth trend did not match expectations for an aggregation-condensation process in the free-molecular regime. TEM results surprisingly showed evidence of silica clusters shrinking with increasing residence time in the flame (height above the burner).642 Zachariah et al. further studied the synthesis of silica NPs using a counterflow diffusion flame reactor with silane as the silicon precursor and an argon-diluted hydrogen–oxygen flame. They studied the effect of temperature, silane loading, equivalence, and momentum ratios on the process and found that flame temperature was the most important factor. At temperatures of around 2000 K, they observed surface growth of initial nuclei, while increasing the flame temperature led to smaller particles in higher number concentration. In addition, they observed that faster reaction at higher temperatures of around 2500 K led to the nucleation of other particle compositions rather than SiO2, which could grow by subsequent silica deposition. Moreover, spherical particles were produced at sufficiently high temperatures due to sintering. Furthermore, they observed that silane loading affected the average particle size along with the growth regime. Higher silane loadings accelerated the nucleation of particles, producing larger particles via surface growth processes. They concluded that they could control the final product morphology and the process yield by adjusting the processing parameters.643 In another study, they synthesized SiO2/TiO2 nanocomposites by decomposing three different sets of precursors in CH4:O2 flames. Combinations of titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) with silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4), silicon tetrabromide (SiBr4), or hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2) were used as precursors. The average size of SiO2/TiO2 nanocomposites was 15 nm. In addition, they produced silica NPs with an average size of 10 nm using the three different precursors and titania NPs with an average size of 13 nm. They found the size of single component silica NPs and the species distribution in the nanocomposites were independent of the silicon precursor.652 They synthesized binary SiO2/Fe2O3 NPs in addition to binary SiO2/TiO2 NPs with diameters below 100 nm and various molar ratios of titanium or iron to silicon using the same system. They observed segregation in the particles comparable to that expected based on equilibrium phase behavior of the systems at the flame temperature. The species distribution in the binary NPs was in qualitative agreement with the expected distribution from the phase diagrams at the flame temperature, demonstrating the utility of phase diagrams for predicting behaviour in this system. The phase diagrams predicted the segregation in the SiO2/TiO2 system better than the SiO2/Fe2O3 system. They suggested that the thermodynamics and differences in the ability of Ti4+ and Fe2+/Fe3+ to replace Si4+ in the silica network were the causes of the differences in the SiO2/TiO2 and SiO2/Fe2O3 systems.653
Almquist and Biswas investigated the effect of particle size on the photocatalytic activity of titania NPs. They produced anatase titania NPs with average sizes of 5 to 48 nm by thermal decomposition of titanium tetraisopropoxide in methane flames with oxygen and air. They also calcined commercially available titania NPs at temperatures in the range of 170 to 700 °C to increase their effective particle size, and tested conventional TiO2 powders (DeGussa P25, and TiO2 from Aldrich) to span an effective size range from 5 to 165 nm. They tested activity for photodegradation of phenol in water and found that particle size played a substantial role in the apparent photocatalytic activity of the titania NPs, particularly for effective particle sizes below 30 nm. In that range, an increase in the particle size led to a significant increase in apparent photocatalytic activity, probably because of the substantial particle size dependence of the efficiency of light absorption and charge-carrier dynamics in NPs with sizes in this range. Based on this dependence, they concluded that the optimum effective particle size was in the range of 25 to 40 nm. The available surface area for redox reactions played a more important role in the photocatalytic activity of titania NPs for effective particle sizes above 30 nm. Accordingly, increasing the particle size beyond 30 nm led to a decrease in the apparent photocatalytic activity.656
Sahu and Biswas employed a single-step flame aerosol technique to synthesize copper-doped titania NPs. Titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) was the host precursor and copper nitrate trihydrate (Cu(NO3)2·3H2O) was the dopant precursor in a methane-oxygen flame. Increasing the doping level led to conversion of anatase TiO2 to the rutile crystalline phase (Fig. 84a) due to substitution of Ti4+ by Cu2+ in the crystal structure of titania, as well as a decrease in the size of primary NPs and their crystallinity (Fig. 84b), and the improved dispersibility due to charging. Increasing the copper doping content to 15 wt% shifted the maximum absorption wavelength to the visible region. Furthermore, they annealed titania NPs doped with 1 and 15 wt% copper at temperatures of 400 and 600 °C in air, which increased the crystallinity of the NPs and transformed their spherical morphology to a hexagonal structure.672
 Fig. 84  (a) XRD patterns of Cu-doped TiO2 nanomaterials (A: anatase and R: rutile) and (b) TEM images of Cu-doped TiO2 nanomaterials with different doping levels after annealing at 600 °C. Adapted from ref. 672 under creative commons license. 
Fotou et al. investigated the photocatalytic activity of titania NPs synthesized by a flame-based approach. They used titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) as the source of titanium and decomposed it in CH4-air flames. To generate pure crystalline anatase titania NPs with a high specific surface area of 110 m2 g−1, they used 277 sccm of methane as the fuel and 2314 sccm of air as the oxidant which produced a flame temperature of 1440 K. They provided 1.4 mol min−1 of TiCl4 as the precursor which led to a residence time in the flame of about 75 ms. They measured photodegradation of phenol in water under UV-irradiation using the flame-synthesized titania NPs and found that they produced even better results than commercial titania. To enhance the photocatalytic activity of the titania NPs, they performed in situ doping with silica via simultaneous decomposition of silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4).657 They later expanded their investigations of the doping of titanium NPs using the same system. In this study, titania NPs were doped with alumina and tin oxide, in addition to silica, using metal chloride precursors. They found that doping with silica decreased the sintering rate of titania as well as the primary particle size, while doping with tin oxide did not have any effect on the primary particle size (Fig. 85). However, it led to conversion of the anatase crystalline phase to rutile because of the similarity of the crystal structure of tin oxide and rutile titania. Using alumina as the dopant also promoted conversion from anatase to rutile via generation of excess oxygen vacancies in the titania-alumina solid solution668 Pratsinis's group also produced V2O5–TiO2 NPs from titanium(IV) alkoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) and vanadium(V) alkoxide (VO[OCH(CH3)2]3) precursors in a CH4:O2 flame. They tested the catalytic activity of the NPs for selective catalytic reduction of NO by NH3. They could control the specific surface area of the NPs in the range of 23 to 120 m2 g−1 by adjusting the oxygen flow to the flame. In addition, they observed a homogeneous dispersion of vanadia on the surface of spherical titania. Moreover, they concluded that the submonolayer amount of vanadia loaded on the titania NPs played a substantial role in the catalytic activity, so that increasing it from 2.5 to 7 μmol m−2 improved the catalytic activity by a factor of 30 per vanadium site. They reported that 10 μmol m−2 loading of vanadia on the surface of titania led to less than 1% N2O production up to 350 °C, illustrating the high activity of the NPs. Also, there was no substantial amount of vanadia within the titania lattice. Despite containing minimal porosity at high specific surface area compared to the conventionally-synthesized catalysts, the flame-synthesized titania-vanadia NPs exhibited selective catalytic activity matching the best results obtained from catalysts produced by conventional methods. They investigated the effect of silica addition to the NPs by adding hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2) to the precursor and found that it led to a decrease in the overall activity by covering part of the titania surface.671
 Fig. 85  TEM images of (a) pristine TiO2 NPs, (b) SiO2-doped TiO2 NPs, and (c) SnO2-doped TiO2 NPs reported by Vemury and Pratsinis. Adapted from ref. 668 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 1995. 
Inturi et al. synthesized transition metal (Cr, Mn, Fe, V, Co, Ni, Cu, Mo, Ce, Y, and Zr) doped titania NPs by a single-step liquid flame aerosol synthesis method with a premixed methane-oxygen flame. The titanium precursor was titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) and the solvent was a mixture of o-xylene (C8H10) and acetonitrile (C2H3N). They used chromium(III) 2-ethylhexanoate (C24H45CrO6), vanadium(V) oxytriisopropoxide (C9H24O4V), iron(III) naphthenate (C22H14O4Fe), manganese(III) naphthenate (C22H14MnO4), nickel(III) naphthenate (C22H14NiO4), copper(II) 2-ethylhexanoate (C8H15CuO2), cobalt 2-ethylhexanoate (C8H15CoO2), molybdenum 2-ethylhexanoate (C32H60MnO8), zirconyl 2-ethylhexanoate (C32H60O8Zr), and yttrium(III) naphthenate (C33H21O6Y) as the metal precursors. They tested the photocatalytic activity of the doped NPs for degradation of acetonitrile in the gas phase under visible light irradiation at ambient pressure and temperature. They observed generation of metal–O–Ti bonds for all synthesized nanomaterials which demonstrated the strong interaction of the dopants with the host in all cases. They reported that the Ti3+ content in the Cr-doped TiO2 was much higher than in the other doped titania nanomaterials which enhanced visible light-induced photocatalytic activity due to band gap narrowing induced by Ti3+ species. Accordingly, the Cr-doped TiO2 had the best photocatalytic activity, which was around 8 to 19 times that of the other nanomaterials. Among all the doped titania NPs, only doping with Cr, Fe, and V (in this order) improved the photocatalytic activity of the titania NPs via reduction of its bandgap energy.667
Boningari et al. used a single-step flame-based approach to prepare sulfur-doped titania NPs. The key point in this synthesis was the preproduction of an unconventional precursor, titanium oxysulfate (TiOSO4). To make the precursor, titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) was added to water, and the resulting precipitate was dissolved with sulfuric acid (H2SO4) to generate a clear solution, to which they then added ethanol (C2H5OH) as a fuel. Sulfur doping of titania NPs not only caused conversion of anatase to the rutile phase, but also enhanced the absorption of the visible light and reduced the band gap energy to 2.78 eV. They found that S4+ and S6+ were the main oxidation states of the sulfur atoms doped into the titania. High S6+ content improved the photocatalytic activity under visible light so that the doped NPs synthesized with 2 M H2SO4 with a S6+/S4+ ratio of 5.61 exhibited the best visible light photocatalytic activity. Because of their excellent activity for photocatalytic destruction of acetaldehyde under visible light, sulfur-doped titania NPs can be considered promising candidates for water purification applications.670 The same group investigated the photocatalytic activity (through visible light degradation of phenol) of flame-synthesized nitrogen-doped titania NPs produced by the same strategy. They added titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) to dilute nitric acid (HNO3), with ethanol (C2H5OH) and urea (CH4N2O) to create a clear miscible precursor solution. They thermally decomposed the precursor solution in a CH4–O2 flame. The nitrogen doped into the crystal lattice of titania was mainly interstitial nitrogen (Ti–O–N) rather than substitutional nitrogen (Ti–N) which was beneficial for narrowing the TiO2 band gap from 3.15 to about 2.51 eV. This modification of the bandgap structure and suppression of photogenerated electron–hole recombination due to interstitial nitrogen enhanced photocatalytic activity under visible light irradiation.664 Furthermore, they reported that the band gap energy could be monotonically reduced by increasing the amount of urea (CH4N2O) as a secondary nitrogen source, while it was not affected by increasing the primary nitrogen source (HNO3).665
Huo et al. synthesized Ti3+ self-doped titania NPs with sizes of 20 to 40 nm via in situ surface hydrogenation in a novel single-step flame-based aerosol synthesis technique. In their system (Fig. 86), the chemical reaction section was composed of a hydrogen–oxygen diffusion nozzle to generate the flame and a stainless-steel pipe ring with a diameter of 10 mm and 16 uniformly distributed holes of 1 mm diameter placed immediately above the nozzle. This pipe ring was used for hydrogenation of titania. The precursor was titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4). Ti3+ ions were formed near the surface of titania NPs with a relative content of about 8%. The morphology of the NPs was controlled by adjusting the precursor feed rate, while modifying the concentration of Ti3+ ions in the titania NPs was possible by adjusting the hydrogen flow. The hydrogenation and subsequent formation of Ti3+ ions created a disordered surface layer by altering the lattice spacing from that of the bulk material. The Ti3+ self-doping of titania NPs improved photocatalytic properties like photocurrent density and visible-light degradation of methylene blue due to enhanced visible light absorption and suppression of charge recombination.666
 Fig. 86  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Huo et al. to synthesize Ti3+-self-doped TiO2 NPs. Adapted from ref. 666 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2014. 
Akurati et al. synthesized WO3/TiO2 nanocomposites from titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) dissolved in ethanol (C2H5OH) and ammonium metatungstate ((NH4)6H2W12O40·xH2O) dissolved in dimethyl formamide (C3H7NO) in an acetylene-oxygen flame. They found that increasing the concentration of WO3 changed the WOx species coordination from tetrahedral to octahedral. For samples with up to 3.6 mol% WO3, unsaturated wolframyl species were dispersed on the surface of titania NPs, increasing the surface acidity. For samples with higher WO3 content, crystalline WO3 domains appeared. The residence time of the particles in the flame at high temperature, which influenced the surface species type and the surface acidity, could be controlled by adjusting the oxygen-to-fuel ratio of the reactants. Activity of the flame-synthesized nanocomposites with up to 3.6 wt% WO3 for photodegradation of methylene blue was enhanced relative to commercially available TiO2 due to higher surface acidity and better charge separation. In these materials, WO3 formed an amorphous surface layer. Crystalline WO3 in nanocomposites with higher WO3 content produced poor photocatalytic performance even though adsorption of methylene blue increased.678
Fujiwara et al. investigated the photocatalytic activity of flame-made palladium cluster-decorated titania NPs for NO destruction under solar light. They dissolved the precursor mixture of titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) and palladium acetylacetonate (C10H14O4Pd) in mixtures of acetonitrile (C2H3N) and 2-ethylhexanoic acid (C8H16O2) and decomposed this precursor in a methane-oxygen flame. They observed palladium clusters below 1 nm in size with intermediate palladium oxidation state between metallic Pd and PdO and high air-stability up to at least 600 °C for 2 hours, although annealing for 2 hours in nitrogen at 400 °C led to partial reduction to metallic palladium with sizes exceeding 1 nm. They found that the flame-made titania NPs with or without 1% doping with palladium clusters performed 3 to 7 times better than commercially available titania NPs for the removal of NOx under solar illumination. Moreover, they observed that the palladium nanoclusters on the surface of titania interacted with oxygen defects which suppressed the adsorption of CO on palladium.675
Lin et al. employed flame spray pyrolysis to synthesize Pt/TiO2 and Pt/TiO2–Vo (Vo: oxygen vacancy) using in situ hydrogenation and tested their photocatalytic activity in gas-phase CO2 reduction. Their synthesis set-up is presented in Fig. 87. They used a bubbler to vaporize titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) at 90 °C with nitrogen as the bubbling gas. The evaporated precursor was mixed with O2 and delivered through the central port of a burner into a flame of premixed oxygen and methane introduced through the outer ports of the burner. To load platinum, a precursor solution of platinum acetylacetonate (C10H14O4Pt) in a mixture of xylene (C8H10) and acetonitrile (C2H3N) was atomized and carried by nitrogen into the reaction zone. For in situ hydrogenation, a hydrogen flow was added through a ring with 16 orifices introduced at a point above the flame where the temperature was ∼700 °C. Upon comparing photocatalytic activity of TiO2 and TiO2–Vo, they found that the activity of Pt/TiO2–Vo (141 μmol (gcatalyst h)−1) was 1.9 times that of Pt/TiO2, 2.4 times that of TiO2–Vo, and 13.8 times that of commercial titania. Addition of platinum led to formation of methane on both the Pt/TiO2 and Pt/TiO2–Vo with methane selectivities of 70 and 81%, respectively and a quantum yield of 1.49% for Pt/TiO2–Vo. In addition, they investigated the effect of strong electronic metal-support interaction (EMSI) in Pt/TiO2–Vo for CO2 photoreduction and reported that EMSI allowed electron transfer from TiO2–Vo to platinum NPs which increased the electron density on the surface of platinum NPs, which enhanced their photocatalytic activity toward CO2 reduction and increased their methane selectivity. Moreover, the EMSI slowed catalyst deactivation, reducing the gradual loss of Pt0 and oxygen vacancies during 8 hours of photoreduction. In contrast, rapid deactivation was observed for both Pt/TiO2 and TiO2–Vo.679
 Fig. 87  Schematic of the system used by Lin et al. to produce Pt/TiO2–Vo nanomaterials. Adapted from ref. 679 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
In a paper focused on TGA analysis, Mueller et al. compared the purity (carbon content) and –OH surface density of titania and silica NPs synthesized by a flame-based approach with those of NPs synthesized by a sol–gel method. While sol–gel prepared particles had varying levels of carbon contamination depending on preparation conditions, only water desorbed from the flame-made samples. This allowed quantification of the –OH surface coverage of flame-made samples by TGA alone.646
2.3.3.3. Ceramic oxides. 
Mädler and Pratsinis employed a flame-based pyrolysis technique to produce pure, homogeneous, and tetragonal crystalline β-Bi2O3 NPs with controlled size and crystallinity with specific surface areas of 20 to 80 m2 g−1 at production rates of 6 to 46 g h−1. Bismuth(II) nitrate heptahydrate (Bi(NO3)3·5H2O) was the solid precursor, which was dissolved in two different solvents. Using a mixture of nitric acid and ethanol as solvent produced a combination of sintered dense spherical NPs with average diameters between 50 to 100 nm and aggregates of primary particles with mean diameters of 10 nm, along with hollow particles of approximately 1 μm diameter, produced by a droplet-to-particle conversion mechanism (Fig. 88a). In contrast use of acetic acid led as solvent produced solid NPs by a gas-to-particle conversion mechanism due to the generation of an acetobismuth complex that evaporated before decomposing (Fig. 88b). They investigated the effect of acetic acid to ethanol ratio to elucidate the solvent influence on the process and found that increasing this ratio led to an increase in the crystallinity and the homogeneity of the NPs. Only nanograined particles were formed for acetic acid fractions above 0.6. In addition, they reported that using bismuth acetate instead of bismuth nitrate produced the same NPs, illustrating that the ion composition in the precursor solution is more important than the precursor itself. They increased the specific surface area of the NPs from 20 to 80 m2 g−1 by increasing the flow rate of oxygen dispersion gas and decreasing the feed rate of the precursor.725 Pratsinis's group employed the same process to generate monocrystalline zinc oxide NPs. The zinc oxide primary particles were smaller and more homogeneous than the zinc oxide primary particles synthesized by other techniques. Zinc acrylate (C6H6O4Zn) was dissolved in a mixture of methanol and acetic acid for use as a precursor. By adjusting the feed rate of the precursor solution, they controlled the mean primary particle diameter in the range of 10 to 20 nm. Increasing the feed rate of precursor solution increased the flame height and consequently enhanced coalescence and/or surface growth which increased the mean primary particle diameter. Fig. 89 shows the morphologies of the flame-synthesized ZnO nanomaterials produced at different precursor feed rates.692 Moreover, they synthesized zinc oxide nanorods doped with tin and indium by this scalable flame-based aerosol technique. In this case, the precursor solution was prepared by dissolving zinc naphthenate (C22H14O4Zn) in toluene (C7H8) and stannous 2-ethylhexanoate (C16H30O4Sn) in tetrahydrofuran ((CH2)4O) for tin-doping, or indium acetylacetonate (C15H21O6In) for indium-doping. The doping level was up to 10 at%. These two dopants selectively passivate specific zinc oxide crystal planes and were incorporated into its lattice. Increasing the doping concentration led to an increase in the specific surface area of the NPs and this increase was sharper for doping with tin than with indium. In addition, doping with tin affected the synthesis of nanorods more than doping with indium at low dopant levels. They attributed growth of nanorods to the higher valency and coordination of tin and indium as dopants compared to zinc, which disrupted growth in particular lattice directions. Moreover, they doped zinc oxide with lithium using lithium acetylacetonate (C5H7O2Li) or lithium tert-butoxide (C4H9OLi) dissolved in tetrahydrofuran. They observed that doping with lithium, which has an equivalent ionic radius to tin and indium, but a valency lower than zinc, did not affect the zinc oxide texture, supporting their hypothesis about the effect of higher valency of the dopants on the formation of nanorods. Fig. 90 presents the morphologies of the zinc oxide nanomaterials containing 4 at% of each dopant.696
 Fig. 88  TEM images of Bi2O3 particles synthesized by Mädler and Pratsinis from (a) Bi(NO3)3·5H2O in a mixture of HNO3 and ethanol. (b) Bi(NO3)3·5H2O in CH3COOH. Adapted from ref. 725 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2002. 
 Fig. 89  TEM images of ZnO particles synthesized by Tani et al. by flame spray pyrolysis with various feed rates. Reproduced from ref. 692 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2002. 
 Fig. 90  TEM images of ZnO particles with 4 at% of various dopants, as indicated. Reproduced from ref. 696 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2006. 
Pawinrat et al. synthesized Au–ZnO and Pt–ZnO nanocomposites by a single-step flame-based aerosol pyrolysis technique. For Au–ZnO nanocomposites, they used zinc naphthenate (C22H14O4Zn) and gold(III) chloride trihydrate (HAuCl4·3H2O) dissolved in ethanol (C2H5OH). They used platinum acetylacetonate (C10H16O4Pt) in a mixture of xylene (C8H10) and acetonitrile (C2H3N) with zinc napthenate for the synthesis of Pt–ZnO. They found that zinc oxide is produced first, followed by metallic gold or platinum. The mean size of the metal particles was 3 to 7 nm, which was affected by metal loading (1 or 3%), while the ZnO particles were roughly an order of magnitude larger. Among these two types of nanocomposites, Au–ZnO enhanced the photocatalytic activity of zinc oxide for degradation of methylene blue, although both of them were similar in physical properties. Indeed, the presence of discrete gold clusters shifted the Fermi level and led to accumulation of photoexcited electrons, slowing recombination of photogenerated electron–hole pairs. Gold-loaded particles had more intense visible light absorption and weaker photoluminescence emission intensity than bare zinc oxide NPs. However, the authors concluded that the Fermi-level shift upon Pt-decoration of ZnO allowed electrons to be discharged into the electrolyte, such that the photocatalytic activity did not improve. Gold loading of 3% gave the best photocatalytic performance.700
In an early study of the general applicability of VAFS, Formenti et al. synthesized various metal oxides including Al2O3, SiO2, TiO2, V2O5, Fe2O3, GeO2, and ZrO2 NPs by the thermal decomposition of the corresponding metal chlorides in a H2–O2 flame and tested their effectiveness for oxidation of hydrocarbons under UV illumination. Their experimental set-up is presented in Fig. 91. The precursors were delivered to the reaction zone from a bubbler through a central tube in pure oxygen or oxygen diluted with air or nitrogen. Three other gaseous flows were injected to the reaction zone through three concentric tubes surrounding the central tube. The middle concentric flow was hydrogen, the fuel. Nitrogen flowed through the inner concentric region, between the central oxidizer flow and concentric fuel flow, to delay mixing and prevent plugging of the burner by particles generated too close to it. The outer region delivered additional oxygen to adjust the temperature of the flame. This played a substantial role in the process especially when the amount of oxygen in the carrier gas was decreased to decrease the flow rate of precursors and increase the residence time, which was important to adjust the size and morphology of the NPs. An electrostatic precipitator was used to collect the NPs. Three conditions were important for the synthesis of NPs in their system: (a) the stability of the oxide at the flame temperature, which was in the range of 1700 to 3000 K and could be controlled by adjusting the ratio of hydrogen to oxygen, (b) sufficiently fast oxidation and/or hydrolysis reaction of the precursors at the flame temperature, and (c) high volatility of the precursors. Spherical nonporous and monodispersed NPs of various oxides with sizes in the range of 10 to 200 nm were produced. They reported that the size and morphology of the NPs depended upon the precursor concentration, the residence time and the flame temperature. In studies of hydrocarbon oxidation, they found that O2 adsorbed on some oxides at room temperature, especially titania, as O2−, a labile species that reacted with carbon monoxide, sulfur dioxide, nitrogen dioxide, paraffins, and olefins to partially or completely oxidize them under UV illumination.584
 Fig. 91  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Formenti et al. to synthesize SiO2 NPs by VAFS. Adapted from ref. 584 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 1982. 
Yoo et al. prepared aluminum-doped zinc oxide NPs by flame spray pyrolysis to improve their sensing performance for acetone detection. They added aluminum acetylacetonate (Al(C5H7O2)3) to a solution of zinc 2-ethylhexanoate (C16H30O4Zn) in xylene to achieve Al doping of 1 at% in zinc oxide. The precursor was dispersed by oxygen and delivered at 5 sccm through the nozzle into a ring-shaped premixed methane-oxygen flame. The final sensor material was obtained by forming a paste of the flame-synthesized NPs, drying, and annealing at 600 °C for 1 hour to improve their thermal and mechanical stability. The flame-made doped NPs outperformed both undoped flame-made NPs and doped NPs synthesized by a hydrothermal method. Upon incorporation into a chemoresistive sensor, the flame-synthesized 1 at% Al-doped ZnO NPs showed a large sensing response of 245 (relative change in resistance) with a response time of around 3 s and a sensitivity of 23 ppm−1 upon exposure to ∼10 ppm acetone at 450 °C, while these values were 54, around 12 s, and 4 ppm−1 for the NPs synthesized by a hydrothermal method. Moreover, these Al-doped ZnO NPs performed better compared other chemoresistive acetone sensors for the detection of ∼0.1 to 10 ppm acetone at a relative humidity of 90%. Notably, the acetone selectivity of these doped NPs can also be extended to other compounds of practical interest, such as ammonia, isoprene, and carbon monoxide. They suggested that the improvement in the sensing activity of the flame-synthesized Al-doped ZnO NPs compared to the pristine NPs and the NPs synthesized by a hydrothermal method can be attributed to the higher concentration of oxygen vacancies which resulted in greater adsorption of oxygen ions, which react with acetone molecules, on the surface of the flame-synthesized Al-doped ZnO NPs.695
Camenzind et al. employed a flame-based pyrolysis technique to produce yttria (Y2O3) doped with 5% europium in a single-step process. Yttrium nitrate hexahydrate (Y(NO3)3·6H2O) was dissolved in ammonia (NH3) and europium(III) 2-ethylhexanoate (C24H45O6Eu) was added as the dopant precursor. They controlled the size of NPs in the range of 10 to 25 nm. Short and cold flames produced pure monoclinic NPs, while long and hot flames generated mostly cubic phase NPs due to the longer residence time in the flame which facilitated the growth and transformation into this phase. This conversion from a compact to a less dense phase was accompanied by a particle size increase. In addition, they investigated the photoluminescence intensity and the radiative decay of the NPs and found that those prepared using the flame aerosol pyrolysis had lower photoluminescence intensity than commercial larger NPs, and also exhibited a faster radiative decay.705 Dosev et al. also synthesized europium doped yttria NPs using liquid-fed aerosol flame pyrolysis. The precursor was yttrium nitrate (Y(NO3)3) and europium nitrate (Eu(NO3)3) dissolved in ethanol at concentrations of 50 and 2.5 mM, respectively. They found that the size of the doped NPs significantly affected their crystallinity and photoluminescence properties. They observed two types of non-agglomerated NPs in the product: (a) smaller faceted NPs with an average size in the range of 5 to 60 nm and a fully dense morphology (Fig. 92a), which probably were a mixture of higher-density metastable mixed phases including mostly monoclinic phases and minor face-centered cubic phases. (b) Larger spherical NPs with smaller inclusions in their center in some cases with an average size in the range of 50 to 200 nm (Fig. 92b) in the stable cubic phase of bulk Y2O3. They suggested that the dependence of particle morphology on the size of NPs could be explained by an elevated internal pressure when the size of NPs was reduced.598
 Fig. 92  TEM images of Eu3+-doped Y2O3 NPs synthesized by Dosev et al. (a) smaller faceted NPs and (b) larger spherical NPs. Adapted from ref. 598 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2006. 
Inyawilert et al. employed a single-step flame-based aerosol pyrolysis process to synthesize 0 to 1.0 wt% PdOx-doped In2O3. The precursor solution was a mixture of indium(III) nitrate hydrate (In(NO3)3·xH2O) and palladium(II) acetylacetonate ((C5H8O2)2Pd) dissolved in ethanol (C2H5OH). It was thermally decomposed in a methane-oxygen flame. The synthesized NPs included cubic and spherical indium oxide NPs with mean sizes of 2–20 nm without any detectable Pd or PdOx secondary phases. Based upon further analyses, they suggested that the palladium-containing species might be embedded in and on grain boundaries of indium oxide NPs. XPS indicated that the Pd was in an oxidized state. In addition, they investigated the gas sensing applications of the synthesized nanomaterials and found that doping with PdOx enhanced the hydrogen-sensing characteristics of indium oxide nanomaterials especially at the optimum 0.5 wt% palladium content.718
2.3.3.4. Mixed metal oxides. 
Marchal et al. synthesized yttrium aluminum garnet (Y3Al5O12) NPs by a flame-based pyrolysis technique. They used various precursor solutions composed of: (a) yttrium nitrate hexahydrate (Y(NO3)3·6H2O) and aluminum nitrate nonahydrate (Al(NO3)3·9H2O) in ethanol (C2H5OH), (b) yttrium nitrate hexahydrate and aluminum nitrate nonahydrate in butanol (C4H9OH), (c) yttrium 2-ethylhexanoate (C24H45O6Y) and alumatrane (N(C2H4O)3Al) in ethanol, (d) yttrium 2-ethylhexanoate and alumatrane in tetrahydrofuran ((CH2)4O), (e) yttrium methoxyacetate (C9H15O9Y) and alumatrane in ethanol, (f) yttrium acetylacetonate (C15H21O6Y) and alumatrane in ethanol, (g) yttrium propionate (C9H15O6Y) and aluminum acetylacetonate (C15H21O6Al) in ethanol, or (h) yttrium propionate and aluminum acetylacetonate in tetrahydrofuran (THF). Oxygen was used as the atomizing gas. They produced NPs with a primary size below 100 nm at a rate of 50 g h−1. They reported that the choice of precursor was a key factor controlling the morphology, phase composition, and phase change upon annealing. Although the as-synthesized NPs had the Y3Al5O12 composition of the precursor feed, XRD analysis showed a mixture of phases that included Y4Al2O9 and hexagonal YAlO3 I. Most likely, the remaining alumina was amorphous or in defect structures. The most homogenous NPs had the modified crystalline structure of YAlO3 I and a composition of Y3Al5O12. Moreover, they annealed their NPs at 900 to 1000 °C for 7 to 10 days to produce free-flowing Y3Al5O12 NPs with no grain growth or necking.707
Saadatkhah et al. synthesized LiMn2O4 NPs in a natural gas-oxygen flame with two types of precursor solutions: a mixture of lithium carbonate (Li2CO3) and manganese acetate tetrahydrate (Mn(CH3CO2)2·4H2O) and a mixture of lithium nitrate (LiNO3) and manganese nitrate hydrate (Mn(NO3)2·xH2O). They investigated the effects of various operating parameters and found that residence time and reaction temperature had the greatest effects on the manganese oxide content, while the carbon content was affected by the precursor and fuel type, increasing to 2% using a mixture of lithium carbonate and manganese acetate tetrahydrate from 0.1% when a mixture of lithium nitrate and manganese nitrate hydrate was employed. In addition, the morphology of the NPs depended upon both the temperature profile and the precursor mixture. A relatively mild temperature gradient generated NPs with a wrinkled surface, while higher temperature and steeper temperature gradient created NPs with “blowholes” in their structure. Moreover, the precursor feed rate affected the size of agglomerated NPs; using 0.5 M nitrate precursors generated polycrystalline NPs with primary particle sizes of 5 to 8 nm in agglomerates of about 1 μm diameter, while the size of agglomerates increased to around 10 μm using 5 M precursor concentration. Fig. 93 presents SEM images of representative particles from this study, illustrating these effects. Furthermore, they used the NPs in Li-ion battery cathodes and found that carbon coating from the flame and increased manganese oxide content of the NPs reduced their lithium uptake (energy storage) capacity.629
 Fig. 93  SEM images of LiMn2O4 NPs synthesized by Saadatkhah et al. with various morphologies obtained by: (a) adjusting the temperature profile: (left) mild temperature gradient yielded 10 μm particles with a shrunken wrinkled surface and right: steeper temperature gradient produced 10 μm particle with a large “blow-hole” and (b) adjusting the precursor mixture: left: 5 M precursor concentration produced 10 μm particles with distributed “blow-holes” and right: 0.5 M precursor concentration yielded 1 μm spheres. Adapted from ref. 629 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 2019. 
Chiarello et al. employed flame-based aerosol pyrolysis with a methane-oxygen flame to produce LaCoO3 perovskite NPs. The precursor was lanthanum(III) acetate dihydrate (La(CH3COO)3·2H2O) and cobalt(II) acetate tetrahydrate (Co(CH3COO)2·4H2O) dissolved in propionic acid (C3H6O2). They investigated the effects of operating parameters on the specific surface area, crystallinity, particle size, catalytic activity, and thermal stability. Increasing the precursor concentration and the precursor feed rate at constant oxygen to precursor ratio, and decreasing the oxygen linear discharge velocity and oxygen flow rate under subsonic conditions, led to decreases in specific surface area and catalytic activity, and increases in the crystallinity, particle size, and thermal stability. Changing the oxygen flow rate under supersonic conditions did not affect those properties.721 In another study, they tested the photocatalytic activity of flame-made titania NPs for hydrogen generation by water splitting and for methanol reforming. The titanium precursor was titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) dissolved in propionic acid (C3H6O2), 2-ethylhexanoic acid (C8H16O2), xylene (C8H10), a mixture of propionic acid and methanol (CH3OH), or a mixture of xylene and pyridine (C5H5N). The organic solvents also served as fuel. They found that the hydrogen evolution rate increased with increasing content of anatase phase, surface area, and crystallinity of the photocatalyst. These factors were favored by high flame temperature. Among the fuels, flame formation with xylene led to production of NPs that were more active than commercially available titania. To enhance photocatalytic activity, they doped titania with gold by combining the titanium precursor and dimethyl gold(III) acetylacetonate (C7H13AuO2) in xylene. Doping 1% gold into TiO2 enhanced the rate of hydrogen generation by an order of magnitude in a water splitting process and by a factor of 30 in methanol reforming, compared to pristine titania.659
Sukee et al. synthesized pristine and Ag-loaded LaFeO3 NPs by flame spray pyrolysis and tested their sensing performance for acetylene detection. Lanthanum(III) nitrate hydrate (La(NO3)3·H2O) and iron(III) nitrate nonahydrate (Fe(NO3)3·9H2O) were dissolved in methanol (CH3OH). Silver nitrate (AgNO3) in acetonitrile (C2H3N) was added to produce Ag-loaded LaFeO3. The precursor was sprayed using an oxygen flow of 5 lpm through a nozzle into oxygen-methane subsidiary flamelets. The pristine LaFeO3 NPs exhibited high sensitivity and selectivity for acetylene concentrations below 500 ppm, outperforming LaFeO3 NPs synthesized by a sol–gel technique. Moreover, an optimal loading of 0.1 wt% of Ag on the LaFeO3 NPs provided the highest response (relative resistance change) of 60 for the detection of 100 ppm acetylene at 200 °C in a chemoresistive sensor. This value was approximately 12 times that of pristine LaFeO3 NPs. A higher loading of 1 wt% Ag on the LaFeO3 NPs gave low sensitivity and no selectivity towards acetylene. The optimal loading of 0.1 wt% Ag on the LaFeO3 NPs exhibited selectivity toward acetylene against interferents including hydrogen, carbon monoxide, carbon dioxide, methane, and ethylene, and a sensor response that changed relatively little with changes in humidity. They proposed, based on a detailed analysis, that the improvement in sensing performance of the Ag-loaded LaFeO3 NPs synthesized by flame spray pyrolysis could be attributed to a Fermi-level control mechanism. The results showed superior performance of the LaFeO3-based NPs for acetylene detection at low concentrations compared to other gas sensors, making them promising candidates for practical applications like dissolved gas analysis (DGA) for power transformer oil.720
Tada et al. employed flame spray pyrolysis to synthesize copper-loaded zirconia NPs with a high loading level ranging from 20 to 80 wt% and investigated their catalytic activity for hydrogenation of carbon dioxide and its conversion to methanol. Because flame spray pyrolysis is a simple and fast technique to synthesize catalysts, they highlighted its ability to produce catalysts for an industrial application. They atomized a solution of copper acetate monohydrate (Cu(CH3CO2)2·H2O) and zirconyl 2-ethylhexanoate (C32H60O8Zr) in methanol (CH3OH) and 2-ethylhexanoic acid (C8H16O2) using oxygen as the dispersant and delivered it to a CH4–O2 flame. They found that varying the copper loading changed the nanomaterial structure, with 20 wt% loading leading to formation of CuO with a mean size of 5 nm on tetragonal ZrO2 NPs with sizes of 5 to 10 nm, while 60 wt% loading produced a core–shell structure of ZrO2 aggregates coated with numerous CuO NPs below 5 nm and an even higher loading level of 80 wt% produced both Cu NPs with an average size of 10 nm and CuO NPs smaller than 5 nm on the ZrO2 aggregates. Hydrogenation at 300 °C reduced the copper oxide to copper NPs with dimensions below 20 nm in diameter and zirconia NPs in the range of 10 to 20 nm. They found that lower loading levels led to strong interaction between copper and zirconia which resulted high methanol selectivity. Increasing loading from 20 to 80 wt% increased the number of active sites for CO2 conversion. The catalyst with a loading level of 60 wt% combined strong interaction between copper and zirconia with a high number of active sites and performed better than a commercial CuO/ZrO2/Al2O3 catalyst for conversion of CO2 to methanol.710
Gültekin et al. used flame aerosol pyrolysis to produce SrAl2O4:Eu2+/Dy3+ NPs. They employed strontium nitrate (Sr(NO3)2) in distilled water and aluminum nitrate nonahydrate (Al(NO3)3·9H2O), europium nitrate pentahydrate (Eu(NO3)3·5H2O), and dysprosium nitrate hydrate (Dy(NO3)3·xH2O) in methanol as the precursor solution, which was decomposed in a methane-oxygen flame. They calcined the as-synthesized material to obtain luminescent NPs. Notably, they mixed the precursor solution with glacial acetic acid (CH3CO2H) as a capping agent and boric acid (H3BO3) to decrease the sintering temperature. In addition, they investigated the optical properties of the flame-synthesized NPs and found that the emission spectra exhibited a green band centered at 525 nm at ambient conditions upon excitation at 350 nm, due to anomalous luminescence from Eu2+ and Dy3+ in the host lattice. Hence, they successfully synthesized novel luminescent doped NPs with long afterglow and high intensity.704
2.3.3.5. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Li et al. synthesized ferromagnetic and superparamagnetic γ-Fe2O3 NPs with primary particle diameters of 6 to 53 nm using a flame-based technique. The precursor was iron(III) acetylacetonate (Fe(C5H7O2)3) dissolved in xylene (C8H10) and acetonitrile (C2H3N) and decomposed in a methane-oxygen flame. Increasing the ratio of precursor to oxygen, the atomizing gas, increased the temperature, residence time, and iron concentration in the flame, which resulted in larger crystallite size and higher cation vacancy concentration. Accordingly, they could gradually convert the morphology of the NPs from quasi-spherical to two-dimensional hexagonal/octagonal platelet shapes, and adjust the crystallinity from disordered cubic phase to fully ordered tetragonal γ-Fe2O3 as the particle size increased (Fig. 94). In addition, they reported that in some cases the particle size distribution broadened with increasing average particle size to an even greater extent than predicted for the self-preserving size distribution. Moreover, they studied the magnetic properties of the NPs and found that with increasing particle size, vacancy ordering led to an increase in saturation magnetization for NPs below 13 nm in diameter. Above this size, the value matched that of bulk γ-Fe2O3. Thereafter, there was a minimal effect of crystallite size on saturation magnetism. Overall, this work showed that flame-based pyrolysis can be effectively employed to produce magnetic NPs with control of magnetic and physicochemical properties, but that the relationships between process parameters and final properties are only partially understood.614
 Fig. 94  TEM images of γ-Fe2O3 NPs synthesized by Li et al. with morphologies from quasi-spherical to two-dimensional hexagonal/octagonal platelet shapes. Insets show corresponding SAED patterns. Adapted from ref. 614 with permission from Royal Society of Chemistry, Copyright 2007. 
Rajput et al. also employed flame spray pyrolysis to synthesize superparamagnetic γ-Fe2O3 NPs with tunable morphology. They used iron(III) acetylacetonate (Fe(C5H7O2)3) in xylene (C8H10) and acetonitrile (C2H3N) with a combustion enthalpy of −26.7 kJ per mole. The precursor solution was atomized using oxygen as the dispersant and delivered to a core flame with a methane-oxygen ratio of 1.5–3.2. Oxygen also served as the sheath gas. The synthesized NPs adopted a hexagonal platelet morphology with a relatively narrow size distribution ranging from 3.6 to 15.7 nm and a high specific surface area of 79 m2 g−1. The authors investigated the magnetic properties of the NPs and reported a blocking temperature (Tb) of approximately 170 K and saturation magnetizations of 45.5 and 50.2 emu g−1 at 300 and 2 K, respectively. Such magnetic properties allowed the authors to use the γ-Fe2O3 NPs as adsorbents to remove heavy metal ions such as copper (Cu2+) and lead (Pb2+) from water, using magnetic separation instead of conventional filtration to recover the particles.683
Strobel and Pratsinis used a scalable flame spray pyrolysis technique with a controlled atmosphere to synthesize iron oxide NPs with three different oxidation states and crystal phases, including wurtzite (FeO), maghemite (Fe2O3), and magnetite (Fe3O4) with average primary particle sizes near 50 nm. In addition to the flame spray nozzle, their set-up (Fig. 95) included a metal tube with an inner diameter of 4 cm that confined the flame and separated it from the surrounding atmosphere. A second spray nozzle, mounted at an angle of 45° to the flame and located 20 cm above the flame spray nozzle delivered a spray of de-ionized water dispersed by nitrogen to rapidly cool the flame products and prevent reignition of the hot unreacted gases upon exposure to air after exiting the reaction tube. They used two types of iron precursor: (a) iron(III) nitrate nonahydrate (Fe(NO3)3·9H2O) dissolved in ethanol (C2H5OH), tetrahydrofuran ((CH2)4O), and 2-ethylhexanoic acid (C8H16O2); and (b) iron(II) naphthenate (C22H14O4Fe) diluted with tetrahydrofuran ((CH2)4O). They could control the iron oxidation state by adjusting the ratio of the precursor solution to air and by the choice of iron precursor, because iron was in a different oxidation state in each precursor. They studied the magnetic properties of the NPs and found that while all of them exhibited superparamagnetism, a mixture of maghemite and magnetite exhibited the highest saturation magnetization.681
 Fig. 95  Schematic of the synthesis set-up employed by Strobel and Pratsinis to synthesize FeO, Fe2O3, and Fe3O4 NPs. Adapted from ref. 681 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 2010. 
Hilty et al. explored the potential of flame-made iron-containing nanomaterials for biological applications, specifically considering iron fortification of foods. Accordingly, they used a scalable flame-based approach to produce poorly water-soluble iron and iron/zinc compounds in nanoparticulate form. These small particles, with a specific surface area of around 190 m2 g−1 produced less change in the color of food compared to conventional iron fortificants. Moreover, the addition of zinc to FePO4 and magnesium to Fe/Zn oxide could promote the absorption of iron from the compounds, and in the latter case improved their color as well.727
Dosev et al. synthesized Co:Nd:Fe2O3/Eu:Gd2O3 NPs with core/shell structure, in which Co:Nd:Fe2O3 served as a magnetic core and Eu:Gd2O3 was a luminescent shell. They employed a two-step flame-based approach for this purpose. In the first step, a solution of cobalt nitrate (Co(NO3)2), neodymium nitrate (Nd(NO3)3), and iron nitrate (Fe(NO3)3) dissolved in ethanol (C2H5OH) was atomized and injected into a H2-air flame with a temperate of 2000 °C. The flame-synthesized loosely-agglomerated NPs were mixed with a solution of europium nitrate (Eu(NO3)3) and gadolinium nitrate (Gd(NO3)3) in ethanol and sonicated to break up loose agglomerates. The aerosol droplets of this precursor solution were introduced into the flame to create the final magnetic core/luminescent shell particles that were used for immunoassay applications.688
Bickmore et al. employed a novel flame-based aerosol reactor to generate NPs of magnesium aluminum double alkoxide. A schematic of their apparatus is presented in Fig. 96. Their reactor consisted of: (a) a cylindrical 7.5 cm diameter by 15 cm long quartz reaction chamber, (b) twin aerosol generators at the entrance of the reaction chamber, (c) a 5 cm diameter quartz tee at the reactor exit, (d) four concentric wire-in-cylinder electrostatic precipitators (5 cm diameter by 1 meter length) arranged with two in sequence on each branch of the tee, with a 5–10 kV DC bias applied between the wire and the wall, and (e) exhaust piping and valves to control the flows. The twin aerosol generators consisted of: (a) a precursor tube with an inner diameter of 0.4 mm, and (b) a tube perpendicular to the precursor tube with an inner diameter of 0.8 mm to deliver oxygen at high velocity as the atomizing gas. The atomized droplets introduced to the reaction zone decomposed in natural gas/oxygen flames from a separate burner. The precursors were prepared from aluminum hydroxide (Al(OH)3) and magnesium hydroxide (Mg(OH)2) or magnesium oxide (MgO) by heating in ethylene glycol with triethanolamine to reactively dissolve them, then evaporating most of the ethylene glycol, and finally diluting with ethanol. They synthesized dense, spherical, ultrafine monocrystalline NPs with average sizes, specific surface areas, and production rates in the ranges of 10 to 100 nm, 40 to 60 m2 g−1, and 50 to 100 g h−1, respectively. They suggested that rapid oxidation of the organic ligands and subsequent nucleation and growth from oxide species as the particle formation mechanism.605
 Fig. 96  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Bickmore et al. to synthesize Mg–Al double alkoxides NPs: (a) the synthesis reactor and (b) the ignition chamber. Adapted from ref. 605 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 1996. 
2.3.3.6. Nanocomposites. 
Sotiriou et al. synthesized biocompatible silica-coated Ag/Fe2O3 as hybrid plasmonic-magnetic NPs with a morphology they described as “Janus-like” (Fig. 97), using a single-step scalable flame-based aerosol process. To do so, they used iron(III) acetylacetonate (Fe(C5H7O2)3) and silver acetate (AgC2H3O2) in a mixture of acetonitrile (C2H3N) and 2-ethylhexanoic acid (C8H16O2) as a high-enthalpy precursor solution. The core NPs formed by thermal decomposition of the precursor solution in a premixed methane-oxygen flame. Subsequent swirl injection of hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2) as a silicon precursor led to in-flight silica coating of the core nanocomposites. The nanothin layer of silica around the core nanocomposites did not alter the magnetic and plasmonic properties or the morphology of the nanocomposite particles. However, it made them more suitable for biological applications, by minimizing release of toxic Ag+ ions and direct contact of Ag with live cells. It simultaneously prevented their flocculation, facilitating dispersion of the NPs in biological and aqueous buffer solutions without any surface treatment. Furthermore, they investigated the magnetic properties of the nanocomposites and found that the nearly superparamagnetic maghemite component facilitated their magnetic manipulation in aqueous suspensions. At the same time, the distinct LSPR absorbance of silver became clear with increasing particle size and silver content.687 In another investigation, they produced biocompatible silica-coated silver NPs with sizes of 5.7–20.4 nm and an inert nanothin layer of silica by the same approach. They evaluated the cytotoxicity of the coated NPs for murine macrophage cells, which are one of the first types of cells that would encounter silver NPs upon intake by mammals. They observed that the smaller nanocomposites released higher fractions of their mass after dispersion in aqueous solution, which played a substantial role in the cytotoxicity of the nanocomposites upon incubation with murine macrophages. They concluded that the toxicity of the silver nanomaterials with sizes below 10 nm was mostly mediated by the release of Ag+ ions, while these ions had less effect when the size of silver NPs was above 10 nm. Indeed, the direct interactions between the silver nanomaterials and macrophages dominated the toxicity of silver nanomaterials with sizes above 10 nm.717
 Fig. 97  TEM images of SiO2-coated Ag/Fe2O3 nanocomposites synthesized by Sotiriou et al. with different Ag content: (a) 10 and (b) 35 wt%. Adapted from ref. 687 with permission from American Ceramic Society, Copyright 2011. 
Strobel et al. designed a novel flame-based reactor (Fig. 98) based upon two nozzles with methane-oxygen flames, used it to produce Pt/Ba/Al2O3 NPs, and investigated the catalytic activity of these materials for NOx storage. One nozzle sprayed the aluminum precursor solution and the other sprayed a precursor solution containing barium and platinum. The first precursor was aluminum(III) tri-sec-butoxide (C12H27AlO3) in diethylene glycol monobutyl ether (C8H18O3) and acetic anhydride (C4H6O3) and the second was barium(II) 2-ethylhexanoate (C16H30BaO4) and platinum(II) acetylacetonate (Pt(O2C5H7)2) in ethanol (C2H5OH). Using two separate nozzles allowed synthesis of individual alumina and monoclinic barium carbonate with good NOx storage activity, while using one nozzle resulted in alumina NPs with amorphous barium species and very limited NOx storage capacity. In addition, they reported that late mixing of the two flame products achieved by increasing the distance between the nozzles increased the crystalline barium carbonate content. The initially-produced monoclinic barium carbonate was converted to the orthorhombic phase at ambient conditions. All of the nanocrystalline barium carbonate samples showed much lower thermal stability than the corresponding bulk material.724
 Fig. 98  Photograph and schematic of two-nozzle flame-based process employed by Strobel et al. to synthesize Pt/Ba/Al2O3 nanocomposites: blue flame color arises from Al and the yellow-greenish flame is from Ba and Pt. Adapted from ref. 724 with permission from American Ceramic Society, Copyright 2006. 
Gänzler et al. employed two-nozzle flame spray pyrolysis to synthesize Pt/CeO2–Al2O3 catalysts in which the concentration of reduced platinum and ceria were 1 and 5 wt%, respectively. For this, they sprayed a solution of aluminum acetylacetonate (C15H21AlO6) in xylene (C8H10) from one nozzle and a solution of platinum (II) acetylacetonate (C10H14O4Pt) and cerium(III) ethylhexanoate (C24H45CeO6) in xylene from another nozzle, with oxygen as the dispersant for both nozzles, into a methane-oxygen flame. They also synthesized CeO2–Al2O3, as a reference catalyst, without adding platinum. To remove organic residues, they calcined the catalysts in air for 5 hours at 500 °C. They tested the nanocomposites as diesel oxidation catalysts, investigating the effect of platinum NPs on the diesel oxidation as well as fast oxygen storage and release by ceria. They found that the size of platinum NPs determined the available sites to activate the reductant (H2, C3H6, or CO) at low temperatures for reduction of ceria. The size of platinum NPs also played a substantial role in determining the platinum-ceria interfacial area; increasing the size of platinum NPs decreased the concentration of interfacial sites and consequently, decreased the reduction rate of ceria.638
2.3.3.7. Scale-up. 
Wegner and Pratsinis investigated scale-up of vapor-fed flame aerosol synthesis, focusing on the well-studied titania and silica systems. Their reactor system (Fig. 99a) consisted of a precursor delivery system, a particle collection unit, and a co-flow diffusion flame reactor with three concentric tubes to pass argon as the carrier gas containing the precursor, oxygen as the oxidant, and methane as the fuel. They employed three different co-flow burners with outer diameters of 5.6, 10, and 27 mm (Fig. 99b). The flow rates of argon and methane, and the feed rate of the precursor were held constant at 0.3 lpm, 0.5 lpm, and 6.5 g h−1 for all three burners, respectively, while the flow rate of oxygen could be adjusted in the range of 1 to 50 lpm to control the size of NPs produced. Increasing the flow rate of oxygen led to a transition from laminar to turbulent flames. The velocity difference between the fuel and oxygen jets at the burner outlet played a substantial role in governing reactant mixing and quenching of these flames. They analysed the primary NP size based on this velocity difference and found that results for all three reactors with different co-flow burners collapsed onto a single curve for each material. NPs with the same primary particle diameter could be synthesized at the same velocity difference between the fuel and oxygen jets with over-ventilated single diffusion flames which was advantageous to control the reactor process. In addition, they scaled by a factor of 0.25, 0.5, 3, and 5 the standard conditions for the synthesis of silica NPs. They observed different operating lines in the plots of the primary particle diameter based on the velocity difference after the scale-up, but in each case the operating line followed an exponential function with two constants for the smallest and largest mean primary particle diameters (Fig. 99c). These constants were different for different operating lines. They reported a correlation between these two constants with the production rate based on a mass balance, which could be advantageous in developing scale-up procedures for diffusion flame-based synthesis reactors.644
 Fig. 99  (a) Schematic of the reactor employed by Wegner and Pratsinis for the scale-up of flame-based aerosol synthesis. (b) Geometric dimensions (mm) of the three co-flow burners. (c) Burner operating lines for the standard equipment without scale-up (5 g h−1 silica production rate), with scale-up by a factor of three (15 g h−1 silica production rate), and with scale-up by factor of 5 (25 g h−1 silica production rate). Adapted from ref. 644 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2003. 
In another study, Pratsinis’ group scaled-up a process for the synthesis of silica to production rates as high as 1.1 kg h−1. The primary particle size in this case was in the range of 10 to 75 nm. They used a commercially available stainless-steel gas assisted atomizer to spray the precursor. A baghouse filter unit was employed to collect the NPs and was cleaned periodically by air-pressure shocks. The precursor was hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2) used pure or diluted in ethanol to investigate the effect of precursor concentration on the average primary particle size in their scaled-up system. They found that the precursor concentration did not affect the size, but that the size could be controlled by adjusting the precursor feed rates and the oxidant flow rates, as in the well-established flame reactor at smaller (laboratory) scale. Furthermore, they could influence the particle size to a limited extent by the choice of the dispersion gas (air or oxygen) or by applying oxygen as a sheath gas (Fig. 100).645
 Fig. 100  TEM images of SiO2 NPs synthesized by Mueller et al. using: (a) 12.5 lpm of air as the dispersion gas, (b) 12.5 lpm of O2 as the dispersion gas, and (c) an additional 25 lpm of O2 as sheath gas. Adapted from ref. 645 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2003. 
2.3.3.8. Reduced metal-containing nanomaterials. 
Scharmach et al. developed a high-temperature reducing jet (HTRJ) reactor to synthesize metal NPs by a flame-based approach. A 3D schematic of the HTRJ reactor is presented in Fig. 101. They combined hydrogen reduction with thermal decomposition to synthesize metal NPs, rather than the more common metal oxides. In their set-up, about 2 lpm of oxygen was introduced into the combustion zone through a central tube, while about 10 lpm of a H2:N2 mixture was introduced through a surrounding concentric annular region to form an inverted diffusion flame. They used a converging-diverging nozzle to accelerate the hot combustion products and injected a precursor solution into the throat or the diverging portion of the nozzle. By adjusting the length and diameter of the region between the nozzle and the quench zone as the particle nucleation and growth section, they could control the residence time in the region in the range from milliseconds to hundreds of milliseconds. They designed the process for a residence time of ∼50 ms with a total gas flow rate in the range of 10 to 20 lpm. An excess flow of about 100 lpm nitrogen at the outlet of the reactor was used to quench the process, enabling the synthesis of loosely-agglomerated NPs. To test their system, they first produced carbon-coated copper NPs as a prototype for non-oxide materials that could not be easily synthesized in conventional flame-based aerosol processes. Copper formate (C2H2CuO4) was selected as the precursor because of its ability to directly produce carbon-coated NPs as well as its high water solubility, low cost, and decomposition to the favored product and environmentally safe byproducts. They synthesized non-agglomerated carbon-coated copper NPs with an average size below 50 nm, demonstrating the potential of the HTRJ reactor to generate non-oxide NPs of metals that can be reduced by hydrogen in the presence of water vapor.689 Later, Konda et al. added an inlet to the quench section of this HTRJ reactor to spray octylamine to cap palladium-copper alloy NPs in-flight. With this in situ functionalization, they successfully combined synthesis and functionalization of Pd–Cu NPs in a single-step process to produce NPs that form stable dispersions in common solvents.713 Recently, Mohammadi et al. used this system to synthesize functionalized bimetallic nickel-silver nanomaterials with an average primary particle size of 4 to 5 nm702 and crumpled reduced graphene oxide (RGO) decorated with non-oxide transition metal NPs in the form of binary and ternary alloys such as Co–Ni, Co–Pd, Co–Ni–Pd, and Co–Ni–Fe with controlled average metal particle sizes below 4 nm.631
 Fig. 101  Schematic of the HTRJ reactor developed by Swihart's group to synthesize non-oxide nanomaterials: (1) (center region) O2 inlet and (outer annular region) H2/N2 inlet, (2) precursor solution inlet, (3) quench gas inlet, and (4) outflow to collection filter. Adapted from ref. 702 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2019. 
2.3.3.9. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Ulrich performed a valuable study on the mechanism of NP growth in flame-based synthesis of silica NPs using a Brownian collision-coalescence model. He used silicon tetrachloride (SiCl4) and a mixture of hydrogen and oxygen to produce a flame that generated silica clusters with average particle diameters of 13.6 and 6.8 nm and specific surface areas of 200 and 400 m2 g−1, respectively. The translational velocities and collision frequencies of the silicon NPs were modeled as those of large gas molecules due to their very small size (behavior described as the free-molecular regime in aerosol dynamics). Because of a relatively short chemical reaction and nucleation period compared to the total synthesis period, the product size distribution was only affected by Brownian collisions and was independent of the early history of the system. Accordingly, the mean logarithm of particle size was directly proportional to the mean logarithm of the growth time. To cool the NPs to temperatures where they do not sinter, a secondary air flow was injected into the commercial flame jets which made the growth time and the average particle size dependent on the initial flame temperature. He concluded that the mechanism could be expanded to treat the synthesis of carbon and other oxides synthesized by flame-based approaches if the size of primary particles was relatively small compared to the mean-free path of gas molecules, the reaction times were relatively short compared to the quench times, and the supersaturation ratios were sufficiently high.639 In another study, Ulrich and Riehl employed CH4–O2 flames for the thermal decomposition of SiCl4 to synthesize silica NPs. They measured the growth of aerosol clusters by a laser light scattering approach and found the synthesized particles were composed of flocs or aggregates containing numerous smaller primary particles.640
Landgrebe and Pratsinis derived a self-preserving map in the process parameter space of dimensionless chemical reaction time and dimensionless process residence time for aerosol synthesis of NPs with a finite rate of precursor reaction or decomposition. Though we discuss this landmark paper here, in the context of flame-based synthesis, because of its generality, it is equally applicable to CVC or other processes with finite rates of monomer generation followed by aggregation and sintering. In general, if allowed to aggregate and sinter for sufficient time, any aerosol will approach a self-preserving size distribution that is approximately log-normal and that changes in mean size and number concentration, but not in shape, with further coagulation and sintering.728,729 Landgrebe and Pratsinis developed a dimensionless discrete-sectional model for free-molecular aerosol coagulation and validated it by examining the section spacing and numerically conserved aerosol characteristics such as number concentration, volume concentration, and volume squared concentration. The self-preserving map described conditions, particularly the minimum dimensionless time, required to synthesize NPs with the asymptotic self-preserving size distribution. The required time to reach a self-preserving size distribution was four times the characteristic time for the coagulation of monomers when reaction (monomer generation) was instantaneous (dimensionless time for reaction <0.25), while it reached around 15 times the characteristic dimensionless time for chemical reaction for slower reactions. Moreover, they compared the results from their discrete-sectional model to moment models for coagulation that assumed a monodispersed, self-preserving, or lognormal aerosol size distribution function. They found that the results from the discrete-section model were in good agreement with the all three moment models in terms of integral aerosol characteristics for the case of instantaneous reaction, while the moment models could not predict the geometric mean diameter, the total number concentration, and geometric standard deviation reliably in the non-self-preserving region of the self-preserving map for conditions with slower precursor reaction and monomer generation.730
Akhtar et al. developed a Monte Carlo simulation to model the morphology changes due to gas-phase coagulation and sintering during high-temperature aerosol synthesis of NPs based upon a modified cluster–cluster aggregation model and taking a finite interparticle binding energy into account. This allowed particles to migrate along the surface of the growing aggregates to simulate sintering. They observed that more compact aggregates formed due to the enhanced surface mobility of monomers at sufficiently high temperatures. The fractal dimension and the sintering rate decreased at longer times (more Monte Carlo steps) as the particles became larger. Moreover, they found that the surface area of aggregates was a more useful and reliable descriptor of aggregate structure than the fractal dimension, due to its greater sensitivity to changes in the simulation conditions such as the reaction temperature and the sintering rate. The main challenge in Monte Carlo simulations is the consideration of two different time scales for the simulation (those of aggregation and sintering) and linking these time scales to real time, e.g. residence time in a flame. To solve this challenge, they defined an effective sintering rate through the time evolution of the surface area of aggregates to relate the two time scales in the model. This paper was a notable advance in understanding of the detailed evolution of aggregate morphology during aerosol synthesis.731
Tsantilis et al. developed an effective and efficient moving sectional model, i.e. a model in which the size bins describing the aerosol size distribution evolve over time, that considered simultaneous gas phase and surface reactions, coagulation, and sintering. They validated it by comparison with standard sectional solutions and detailed literature models. They considered the synthesis of titania NPs in a premixed CH4–O2 flame, using titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) as the precursor, as a case study. They compared their predictions with experimental results for Sauter (surface area) mean diameter, geometric standard deviation, and the full primary particle size distribution at various locations in the flame to investigate and distinguish between three different reaction pathways for production of titania NPs, including pure gas phase thermal decomposition, gas phase hydrolysis, and gas phase and surface decomposition of the precursor to describe the measured primary particle size distributions. Agreement between the modelling and experimental results was excellent in the monodispersed regime for the primary particle size distribution. Under those conditions, surface reaction was the dominant pathway for the growth of NPs.732
Rosner and Pyykönen developed and applied a bivariate quadrature method of moments simulation using 9 moments to model synthesis of alumina particles in a trimethyl aluminum-seeded CH4–O2 laminar counterflow diffusion flame reactor for which they had previously collected an extensive data set. They predicted the evolution of alumina NP aggregates using known transport laws for coagulation, sintering, and thermophoresis, capturing most of the observed behavior in their extensive data set. Although they were able to fit an effective activation energy for particle coalescence, they did not necessarily view it as physically meaningful, and discussed several potential improvements in the various rate laws that will be needed. They nonetheless suggested that the incorporation of this simulation technique into full probability density function (PDF)-methods for turbulent reacting flows could enable the description of realistic turbulent synthesis reactors using improved rate laws, while recognizing both the advantages and limitations associated with quadrature-based moment methods that do not assume or provide explicit distribution functions for size or other properties.733
Mädler et al. combined measurements and modelling of droplet evaporation and flame temperature as well as particle properties in a systematic study of the flame-spray-pyrolysis synthesis of silica using an external-mixing gas-assisted atomizer with six premixed methane-oxygen flamelets as supporting flames. They investigated the effect of oxidant and precursor solvent/fuel composition with different enthalpy contents on the specific surface area of the flame-made NPs. For this, they considered the production of silica NPs from precursor solutions of hexamethyldisiloxane (O[Si(CH3)3]2) in methanol, ethanol, or iso-octane. Oxygen and air were each used as both dispersion gas and as oxidant. They employed a spray combustion model to compute the droplet lifetime and describe the effect of oxidant/dispersion gas type on the structure of the flame and the characteristics of the synthesized NPs. They observed that increasing the flow rate of oxidant at low oxidant flow rates increased the specific surface area of NPs because it resulted in shorter residence times in the shortened spray flame, and thus, less time for growth of NPs. The specific surface area reached a maximum and then decreased at higher flow rates of the oxidant as the flame height further decreased. At the highest oxidant flows, both solvent (fuel) and precursor oxidized faster in a smaller volume, reaching higher temperatures that induced a higher sintering rate. They observed this maximum for both methanol and ethanol. For iso-octane, with higher enthalpy content driving faster evaporation and combustion, increasing the flow rate of oxidant produced a monotonic decrease in specific surface area. Further increase in the flow rate of oxidant did not allow sufficient heat transfer and time for ignition from the assisting flamelets, and the main flame was extinguished. Iso-octane with the lowest volatility exhibited the longest ignition delay among the three fuels. Methanol with the lowest enthalpy content and accordingly, the slowest evaporation rate, gave a maximum specific surface area at higher flow rates, confirming the lesser importance of the decrease in the residence time compared to the increase in combustion intensity at high flow rates. In situ flame spray temperature measurements revealed a longer residence time for growth at higher temperature, achieved using oxygen as the oxidant compared to air, which accordingly resulted in lower specific surface area. The results of their study provided valuable insights into the flame spray synthesis of NPs, particularly for liquid precursors that are not easily evaporated.734
Some of the most sophisticated experimental and simulation studies of aerosol particle formation have been developed for soot formation. These are most often driven by a desire to prevent unwanted soot formation, but are also applicable to intentional synthesis of nanomaterials, particularly carbon black. In an exemplary study, Zhao et al. measured the soot particle size distribution function (PSDF) in a laminar premixed ethylene flame using a scanning mobility particle sizer, which detected particles down to a mobility diameter of 3 nm, in combination with a carefully-designed sampling and dilution system that quenched chemical reactions and minimized coagulation and losses in the sampling system. They studied the evolution of the PSDF from the very early stages of soot nucleation to particle mass growth. They observed that the PSDF followed a power-law dependence on particle diameter at the very early stages of soot formation, then became bimodal at higher positions above the burner surface, and maintained a bimodal distribution in the post-flame region. The small-particle part of the PSDF followed a power-law function, while the large-particle part of the PSDF was approximately log-normal. They employed a detailed numerical simulation to analyze the experimental results, in which the flame structure was described with detailed reaction kinetics and species transport. Nucleation and growth of the soot particles were also described through a kinetic model that included both surface growth and oxidation. In addition, they used a stochastic method to simulate the evolution of the PSDF.
