The numerical simulation was in a good agreement with experimental data, also predicting the bimodal distribution of the PSDF. Further analysis of the aerosol dynamics equations revealed that the bimodal behavior is intrinsic to an aerosol process involving cluster–cluster coagulation with nucleation of particles dominated by monomer dimerization.735
Zucca et al. integrated the Direct Quadrature Method of Moments (DQMOM) approach for solution of the population balance equations describing evolution of soot size distribution with a commercial finite-volume-based CFD code to simulate soot formation in a turbulent non-premixed ethylene-air flame. They used simplified kinetic expressions for nucleation, molecular growth, and oxidation of the soot particles, and a Brownian aggregation kernel. Furthermore, they simulated the fractal dimension evolution with a monovariate population balance as the simplest means to track the evolution of the morphology of the aggregated NPs. While the volume fraction of soot is well predicted, independent of assumptions about fractal dimension, because it does not depend on aggregation, the overall aggregate size was quite sensitive to fractal dimension. Thus, they showed that a suitable treatment of the particle fractal dimension was necessary to reasonably predict the morphology and overall size of the soot aggregates.736
Neto et al. employed a similar strategy to simulate the flame-spray-pyrolysis synthesis of NPs with polydispersed size distributions and solved the bivariate population balance model through DQMOM, which was selected based on its relatively straightforward adaptation from monovariate to bivariate cases. Their CFD simulations applied an Eulerean–Langrangian–Eulerian framework to simulate the reacting turbulent multiphase flow of the burning spray. The evaporating droplets, which are initially relatively large, were treated in a Lagrangian framework, while all other variables, including those associated with the NPs formed, were treated in an Eulerean framework. They considered the synthesis of zirconia NPs from a precursor solution of zirconium n-propoxide (C12H32O4Zr) in ethanol and propanol as a case study. They reported an accuracy of approximately 90% for the first two moments of the size distribution and good agreement between modelling and experimental results for the prediction of primary particle number per agglomerate.737
Singh et al. performed numerical simulations and sensitivity analysis to predict soot particle size distributions (PSDs) in laminar premixed ethylene flames and compared their predictions with experimental results for six flame configurations. They investigated the sensitivity of the soot PSDs to the defining soot model parameters such as soot inception, particle and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon (PAH) collision efficiency and enhancement, and soot surface activity. They observed that increasing size of incepting soot particles played a significant role in the lower end of the PSDs, especially the position of the trough between modes in bimodal soot PSDs. Accordingly, understanding the chemistry of higher PAHs and a more detailed inception model in the case of interaction of PAHs to give different sizes of incepting soot particles are necessary for the precise prediction of soot PSDs. They also developed a new model to predict surface activity decay, in which only the soot particle history and the local flame temperature affected the soot particle activity. This was the first attempt to employ local flame variables to determine the surface aging, which was useful to predict the higher end of the soot PSDs.738 This group also performed a similar investigation on a laminar premixed ethylene burner-stabilised stagnation flame and studied the soot morphology in the post-flame region. Based on their sensitivity analysis, they found that the minimum particle inception size, the inception species concentration, and the coagulation rate have the greatest effect on the PSDs. Changes in the particle inception size and the coagulation rate produced an overall shift in the coagulation peak position. Moreover, they observed that a systematic shift in the both position of the trough between the bimodal PSD modes and the coagulation peak at larger diameters were both driven by particle inception size changes. Furthermore, they reported that a good agreement between the modelling and experimental data could be obtained only through changes in the concentration of inception species with each burner-stagnation plate separation.739
In another report, Kraft's group investigated the evolution of particle size distribution function (PSDF) in premixed stretch-stabilized flat ethylene-oxygen-argon flames to study the formation of soot in a pseudo-one-dimensional flow configuration that extended the experimental flame condition space by stretch-stabilization. They considered three sets of atmospheric-pressure ethylene-oxygen-argon flames with maximum temperatures of approximately 1980, 2000, and 2160 K to measure PSDFs and found strong dependence of PSDFs on the flame temperature. In agreement with prior studies, soot nucleation and growth stopped or reversed at sufficiently high temperatures. They observed a bimodal size distribution for PSDFs for the lower-temperature flames with peak temperatures of approximately 1980 K, while they were unimodal for the higher flame temperatures. Although the population balance soot modelling results were in reasonable agreement with the experimental data for the flames with maximum temperatures around 1980 and 2000 K, it predicted the size and number density of soot to be much higher than the experimental results in the flame with a maximum temperature of around 2160 K. Based on sensitivity analyses, they proposed that the high temperature reversibility in soot nucleation, coagulation, and surface growth is the main reason for this overprediction. Accordingly, taking the thermodynamic reversibility of these processes into account is of great importance for the precise prediction of soot formation at temperatures above 2000 K.740
Botero et al. presented a theoretical and experimental investigation of the evolution of primary soot particles along the centerline of a co-flow laminar diffusion flame. They analyzed the size and nano-structure of soot particles from the ethylene flame, which were taken at various heights above the burner. They observed that the initial soot NPs, after nucleation, coagulate and coalescence to generate primary particles with a size of 5 to 15 nm. Coagulation and surface growth led to an increase in the size of the primary soot NPs along the centerline of the flame. Then, an amorphous layer of hydrocarbons condensed on the surface of these NPs which facilitated aggregation of primary particles into fractal-like structures. Higher temperatures in the flame led to fast carbonization of the amorphous layer to form a graphitic shell around the primary NPs. This graphitization reduced the size of NPs by compacting the primary particles. Thereafter, rapid oxidization near the flame tip reduced the size of NPs. Notably, based upon the experimental results, the primary particle size distribution was unimodal and relatively sharp at all the heights above the burner. It broadened and shifted toward larger size during particle growth, then shrunk and narrowed in the oxidation region of the flame. Moreover, they employed a detailed population balance model to study the mechanisms affecting the primary particle size distribution. They showed that although the average size of primary particles was relatively insensitive to model parameters such as sintering pre-factor, smoothing factor, and critical diameter for “liquid-like” behavior, the primary particle size distributions were sensitive to these factors. Accordingly, good agreement between the modelling and experimental results for the average size of primary particles is not a guarantee that the simulation results predict the primary particle size distributions precisely.741 This group also investigated the evolution of the soot particle size distribution in an n-heptane/toluene co-flow diffusion flame along its centerline. For this purpose, they reported the flame temperature and soot particle size distribution along the centerline of the diffusion flame. They added 0–15 mol% toluene to n-heptane and found that with increasing concentration of toluene, soot inception occurred at lower height above the burner (lower in the flame) which caused a larger soot growth zone and thus, an increase in the size of soot primary and agglomerated NPs. However, it had a negligible effect on the maximum number density of soot NPs. This study provides a new dataset for vapor-fed flames in a configuration (the Yale burner) that is well established for gas-fed, e.g. ethylene, flames.742
Mehta et al. developed a multiscale tool for modelling TiO2 synthesis from TiCl4 in a realistic turbulent flame aerosol reactor. They studied the effect of various chemical mechanisms, including one-step, detailed, and flamelet models, on the nucleation of NPs. They first employed a plug-flow reactor (PFR) and a partially stirred tank reactor (PaSR) to simulate the flow field under relatively ideal conditions. The simultaneous development of detailed chemical mechanisms to define gas-phase combustion and the evolution of particles, along with advanced tools to simulate the turbulent flow field and its interactions with the chemical processes, are necessary to develop such a predictive simulation. Toward this goal, they proposed a combination one-step/detailed model and a flamelet model to describe detailed chemistry for the nucleation of particles and combustion. The PFR simulation results showed that most nuclei were generated upstream of the flame surface for the one-step nucleation mechanism and then passed through the high-temperature zone in the flame, while the bulk of the nuclei were generated downstream of the flame surface for the, presumably more realistic, detailed nucleation mechanism. Thus, the one-step nucleation mechanism should predict very different high-temperature sintering behavior, in comparison with the detailed nucleation mechanism. The PaSR modelling revealed that, although there was a clear difference between the one-step and detailed nucleation mechanisms, the vigorous back mixing inside the turbulent flow led to formation of quite uniform particles. For the one-step nucleation mechanism, increasing the flame temperature increased the nucleation rate and the nucleation took place on the lean side of the flame, while it was predicted on both the lean and rich sides using the detailed mechanism, which predicted nucleation at temperatures ranging from 1500 K to 1800 K. In addition, they developed a large-eddy simulation tool to investigate the dependence of the synthesis of NPs in turbulent flames on the precursor injection configuration and saw that the formation of NPs happened very close to the surface of the flame in the case of the one-step nucleation mechanism, which was in agreement with results from PFR and PaSR simulations.743
Shekar et al. developed a chemical kinetic model for simulating the flame-based aerosol synthesis of silica NPs from tetraethoxysilane (TEOS) (SiC8H20O4). The first considered reactions involving species with high concentrations at equilibrium and identified silicic acid (Si(OH)4) as the main decomposition product. Then, they performed flux and sensitivity analyses to determine the key reaction pathways and observed that the generation of silicates from the TEOS decomposition under their process conditions was not important. Based upon level of importance (LOI) analysis for each species, they reduced the kinetic model to only the important species and coupled the reduced kinetic model to a detailed population balance model for particle formation by an operator splitting technique. They incorporated new particle inception and surface growth steps into a stochastic particle model in which the interaction of silicic acid monomers led to the formation and growth of particles. They considered the coagulation and sintering of particles in the model. They employed low discrepancy sequences to fit the model to the experimental data for collision and primary particle diameters and extract material-dependent sintering parameters consistent with experimental results. With this approach, they were able to predict the particle size distributions and create computer-generated TEM-style images in a good agreement with experimental data. Notably, they presented the temporal particle size evolution and the gas-phase reactor composition at various temperatures as well.744
Manuputty et al. modeled the nucleation, surface growth, and coagulation of spherical TiO2 particles in a stagnation-flame-synthesis system, assuming a simple one-step decomposition reaction of titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) and one-dimensional stagnation flow. They fully coupled the model to the flow and gas phase chemistry and solved the PBE for particle size using the method of moments with interpolative closure (MoMIC), assuming spherical particles due to the high flame temperature. They accounted for a free-jet region in the flow by matching the simulated OH* peak location with the experimentally observed position. Although they obtained good agreement between the modelling and experimental data on the mobility particle size with changes in the loading level of the precursor, they underpredicted the geometric standard deviation at high precursor loadings. Moreover, they reported high sensitivity of the particle size to the sampling location close to the stationary stagnation surface based upon the modelling results, which increased with increasing stagnation temperature and loading level of the precursor. Coagulation was the dominant process near the stagnation surface. Furthermore, they evaluated the effect of surface growth by comparison to results using an alternative reaction model and revealed the significant role of surface growth in the very early stage of particle formation. Omiting it produced extreme discrepancies in particle size and geometric standard deviation. Reducing the length of the combustion zone led to greater significance of surface growth than coagulation overall and produced a narrower size distribution.745 In a more recent study, they developed a general two-step simulation methodology to describe the complex morphology of aggregated NPs in a stagnation flame. They applied the methodology to the same case study as the previous work. First, a fully-coupled detailed gas-phase chemistry model, a one-dimensional flow model, and a spherical particle model treated by the MoMIC were employed to produce profiles of gas-phase conditions. Then, a detailed stochastic population balance model was applied to these gas-phase conditions to simulate the evolution of aggregating, sintering particles. To consider the thermophoretic transport effects due to sharp temperature gradients close to the stagnation flame, they applied a thermophoretic correction in the second step through a simulation volume scaling term which improved consistency between the second particle-evolution simulation and the first fully-coupled simulation.746 They employed the same strategy to simulate the formation of soot in a laminar premixed burner-stabilised benchmark ethylene stagnation flame which produced excellent agreement between the modelling and experimental results for burner-stagnation plate separations of at least 7 mm and for particles with mobility diameter above 6 nm.747 In addition, they performed a comprehensive parametric sensitivity analysis to find the effects of various processes on the computed primary soot particle size distributions in a laminar premixed burner-stabilized ethylene stagnation flame and observed that the production rate of pyrene, coagulation efficiency, and surface growth rate had the greatest effects on the computed primary soot particle size distributions.748 Moreover, they performed a parametric sensitivity study on the synthesis of titania NPs in a stagnation flame using this two-step simulation methodology, but this time they introduced new sintering parameters to consider the sintering behavior of NPs below 10 nm instead of the thermophoretic correction. Based upon this parametric sensitivity analysis, they found that the sintering parameters have the most significant effect on the morphology of the flame-synthesized titania NPs.749
In another related study, this group investigated, both experimentally and theoretically, the synthesis of a metastable “high-pressure” phase known as TiO2-II or α-PbO2-type TiO2 in a single-step flame-based aerosol synthesis process using a laminar premixed stagnation flame. They could adjust the fuel/oxygen equivalence ratio to produce other nanocrystalline phases of titania, such as anatase, rutile, and TiO2-B, and found that fuel-rich conditions led to generation of a rutile/TiO2-II mixture, while anatase/rutile and anatase/TiO2-B mixtures were synthesized under stoichiometric and fuel-lean conditions, respectively. The phase-controlled synthesis of heterojunctions of titania phases may be of great significance for photocatalytic activity, due to enhanced electron–hole separation. They proposed that the synthesis of TiO2-II in an atmospheric pressure flame is kinetically possible by the oxidation and solid-state transformation of a sub-oxide intermediate (TiO2−x), though it is not the thermodynamically stable phase. Based on this hypothesis, the metastable TiO2-II phase was considered responsible for the nucleation of rutile and was not directly generated from a molten/amorphous state.750
Rai et al. investigated the growth stages of ramified aggregates, representative of those formed in sooting flames and flame-based aerosol synthesis of metal oxides, through analysis of previously-reported in situ small angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) as a function of height above the burner. They considered the synthesis of silica aggregates in a premixed hexamethyldisiloxane (HMDSO) (O[Si(CH3)3]2)/methane/oxygen/nitrogen flame by Kammler et al. as a case study.751 They analyzed ultra-small angle X-ray scattering (USAXS) using a recent Unified Fit approach to identify intrinsic details of aggregate size and structure. With this approach, they were able to quantify the minimum paths, branch length, branch fractions, average number of branches, total mass of aggregates, coordination number of primary particles, Sauter mean diameter, and polydispersity of primary particles, providing new insights into the aggregate growth path. They summarized the growth process of aggregated NPs in three steps: (i) initial nucleation and growth, (ii) coalescence as the flame cools, and (iii) agglomeration of aggregates at higher heights above the burner to form the larger aggregated NPs, which can be considered universal steps applicable to production of most NP aggregates in flames.752
Li et al. studied droplet micro-explosions during single droplet combustion of precursor solutions in flame spray pyrolysis with optical techniques, including interferometric particle imaging (IPI) to find the droplet size changes and standard rainbow refractometry (SRR) to observe the rainbow pattern that changes with refractive index and refractive index gradient within droplets. They considered a solution of tin(II) 2-ethylhexanoate (C16H30O4Sn) in xylene (C8H10) as a case study. To analyze the composition and temperature changes due to heat and mass transfer within the burning droplets, based on the experimentally-observed droplet size changes and rainbow patterns, they developed a multicomponent diffusion-limited transport model. Accordingly, they suggested the following mechanism for the droplet evolution and micro-explosions: (i) Droplet ignition resulted an initial increase in the rainbow scattering maximum angle, due to a sharp increase in the surface temperature and then, a decrease associated with the subsequent temperature equilibration between core and surface. (ii) Accumulation of the metal–organic precursor with a low vapor pressure at the surface of the droplets upon evaporation of the more volatile solvent limited solvent transport to the flame. This led to flame contraction and droplet surface reheating which then drove thermal decomposition of the precursor. This caused the formation of a viscous shell as a diffusive barrier for the solvent inside the droplet. Hence, a second increase in the rainbow maxima angle was observed as a result of the temperature gradient between the core and surface of the droplets. (iii) The subsequent temperature balance between the core and surface again decreased the rainbow angle maximum. The increasing temperature inside the droplet initiated the nucleation of vapor bubbles of the high volatility component, which caused the droplet micro-explosion. They observed a substantial difference in rainbow pattern evolution between precursor-laden droplets that undergo micro-explosions and single component droplets. Moreover, they found that the metal–organic precursor concentration significantly affected the required time for the droplet micro-explosions and the rainbow pattern evolution of the burning droplets. Indeed, increasing the concentration of metal–organic precursor reduced the time required for the droplet micro explosions by increasing the rate of accumulation of the precursor at the surface of the droplets and its subsequent decomposition and shell formation.753
In a study that focused on the dynamics of aerosol aggregation, Matsoukas and Friedlander synthesized spherical magnesium oxide and zinc oxide NPs with average primary particle sizes of 5 and 10 nm, respectively. The precursors were magnesium acetate trihydrate (Mg(CH3CO2)2·3H2O) and zinc nitrate hexahydrate (Zn(NO3)2·6H2O) which were decomposed in a methane-air flame. They investigated the dynamics of the agglomerate size distribution and found good agreement between the distributions of the mobility equivalent diameter of the synthesized agglomerates and the coagulation theory for fractal-like agglomerates in the free molecule regime, which leads to a self-preserving size distribution for the agglomerates. Consistent with this theory, the average agglomerate size was inversely correlated with the size of the primary particles, given sufficient time for agglomeration.633
Biswas' group also studied the collisional growth rate of titania NPs synthesized in a flame-based aerosol reactor with an average size of 5 to 8 nm. Their system comprised: (a) a flame-based aerosol reactor to synthesize initial titania NPs by thermal decomposition of titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) in an O2:CH4 flame, (b) a nitrogen dilution probe placed horizontally, (c) a charged particle remover, (d) a first tube furnace at a fixed temperature of 823 °C to produce charge-free titania NPs with spherical morphology, (e) a second tube furnace for the collisional growth at temperatures of 400, 600, and 800 °C in laminar flow, (f) a nano-differential mobility analyzer (Nano-DMA) for particle classification after each furnace, (g) a condensation particle counter after each Nano-DMA to measure the particle number concentration and size distribution, and (h) a gas analyzer to measure the CO2 concentration in the combustion products. They numerically solved the aerosol general dynamic equation to model evolution of the particle size distribution. They compared the changes in particle size distribution at the outlet of the second tube furnace with predictions of the model and found that the collisional growth rate of the flame-synthesized titania NPs was greater than the rate predicted by the kinetic theory of gases. Accordingly, they calculated a collisional growth correction factor. They recognized that lower temperature led to an increase in the collisional growth factor because of increased importance of inter-particle attractive forces between the NPs. The collisional growth factor decreased with increasing temperature from 400 °C up to 800 °C, although it remained above 1, showing that even at high temperatures the attractive forces are non-negligible.660
Gan et al. applied a deep learning method to model the chaotic movement of the trapped NPs in the recirculation zone of a buoyancy-opposed flame aerosol reactor. Such buoyancy-opposed flames, or inverted flames, can produce macroscopic aggregates via the long residence time of these recirculating particles in and near the flame zone, leading to “gelation” of aggregates. They employed a high-speed camera to capture the Lagrangian trajectories of particles in the flame. Subsequently, they reconstructed the trajectories in a 3-dimensional cylindrical space and used them to train the two most widely adopted deep generative models: the generative adversarial net (GAN) and the variational autoencoder (VAE). Then, they investigated the performance of these two models and determined that the GAN model performed much better than the VAE. Results from the GAN model met both generalization and accuracy criteria, while a distinct trade-off between these criteria was unavoidable for VAE. Obtaining non-repeating multimodal outputs with statistical accuracy was possible in the GAN model. However, the VAE model could only select a single answer equivalent to the average of a wide variety of outputs because it is trained based on minimization of the mean squared error between input and output. Moreover, the GAN model was able to produce non-repeating trajectories of unlimited length, using only finite trajectories for training. Accordingly, the GAN model can be used to generate an unlimited number of trajectories with a fixed length, which is advantageous. Notably, these trajectory simulation methods are based upon statistics and therefore their performances is limited by the quality of the training datasets. Such statistically-based methods thus cannot fully replace deterministic modelling in which the physics responsible for the system's dynamic evolution are captured, particularly for extrapolating outside the parameter space of the training set.754


2.3.4. Laser pyrolysis. 
In most laser pyrolysis methods, a 10.6 μm CO2 laser beam is used as the source of energy to decompose a precursor. Mixing with an unheated gas that does not absorb at the laser wavelength leads to rapid quenching to form clusters. After the initial precursor decomposition, fast particle growth can relieve remaining supersaturation. Surface reaction as well as coagulation and coalescence are then responsible for further growth. The synthesis of hard agglomerates can be avoided by the rapid transition between high-temperature (rapid coalescence) and low-temperature (negligible coalescence) conditions.755–757 Energy of the laser at the wavelength of 10.6 μm should be absorbed in the reaction zone. Three classes of materials have this property: (a) precursors, such as silane (SiH4), that directly lead to the synthesis of the nanomaterials; (b) photosensitizers, like sulfur hexafluoride (SF6), which only absorb and transfer energy to reactants by collisions, without participating in the synthesis reaction; and (c) species that absorb, decompose and react to drive decomposition of other precursors, like ethylene (C2H4) employed to synthesize vanadium oxide.757 The most important advantages of this method include producing nanomaterials with a narrow size distribution and high specific surface area that cannot be made by conventional thermal gas phase methods. Other advantages are no reactor corrosion, no product contamination, and no need for a high temperature furnace, since the hot zone is confined to the region in which gaseous reactants and the laser beam interact; the lack of contact with reactor walls is helpful in generating high purity products. Furthermore, rates of heating and cooling in the method are very high because only the gas, which has a small heat capacity, is heated.755–758 There are also important challenges for widespread use of laser pyrolysis like agglomeration, difficulty in scale up, laser safety issues, and use of relatively expensive laser energy, but the greatest challenge in this method is difficulty in the identification and adoption of efficient and safe precursors, because the precursors for some common materials that are synthesized by laser pyrolysis are dangerous, and alternative precursors are less effective.759Table 10 summarizes and Fig. 102 provides representative TEM images of some nanomaterials that have been synthesized using laser pyrolysis.

2.3.4.1. Silicon-based nanomaterials. 
Cannon, Danforth, Flint, Haggerty, and Mara were the pioneers of the laser pyrolysis synthesis technique.760,761 They first used it to synthesize, silicon, silicon carbide, and silicon nitride. They used silane as the silicon precursor. Because silane can absorb the energy of the laser at 10.6 μm, there was no need to use any additional photosensitizer. In addition, they added ethylene (C2H4) and methane (CH4) to produce silicon carbide or ammonia (NH3) to produce silicon nitride. Both ethylene and ammonia could absorb the energy of the laser, while this was not possible for methane. In their set-up (Fig. 103), the gaseous precursors were introduced through a stainless-steel nozzle with an inner diameter of 1.5 mm. They used an argon stream at 400 sccm as a sheath gas surrounding the precursor inlet, which served not only to confine the gaseous precursors in a small zone, but also to quench the hot products to stop particle growth and limit the particle size. Moreover, a stream of argon at 600 sccm was directed at the KCl windows through which the laser entered the reactor, to cool them and keep them clean. A mechanical pump and throttling valve were used to control the pressure in the system in the range of 1.17 to 14.50 psia. The energy source was a 150 W laser with unfocused intensities from 270 to 1020 W cm−2, from which focused intensities could reach 105 W cm−2. They synthesized highly-pure, monodispersed, spherical, and loosely agglomerated NPs with sizes in the range of 10 to 100 nm and an average crystallite size of about 15 nm (Fig. 102a). Their production rates were about 1 g h−1, and a maximum of about 10 g of NPs was synthesized during a run of the reactor. Increasing the laser power led to production of crystalline silicon NPs rather than amorphous ones. Notably, the fully dense silicon NPs did not possess any surface or internal porosity and were extremely pure with oxygen content below 0.1 wt%. The silicon carbide and silicon nitride NPs were in the ranges of 18–26 and 10–25 nm, respectively.760,761
 Fig. 103  Schematic of the reactor developed by Cannon et al. to synthesize Si-containing NPs by laser pyrolysis. Adapted from ref. 760 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 1982. 
Decomposition of metal carbonyls such as iron carbonyl (Fe(CO)5), chromium carbonyl (Cr(CO)6), molybdenum carbonyl (Mo(CO)6), and tungsten carbonyl (W(CO)6) using this technique following the pioneering research of Cannon and coworkers demonstrated the versatility of the laser pyrolysis approach for synthesis of various nanomaterials.830
Ehbrecht and Huisken employed time-of-flight mass spectrometric analysis for in-flight characterization of silicon nanomaterials synthesized by pulsed laser pyrolysis of silane (SiH4) with a pulse energy of 50 mJ. They observed that the silicon nanomaterials consisted of Sin+ clusters in which n ranged from a few tens to several thousand. When n was between 22 and 100, the intermediate-size Sin clusters fragmented to yield Si6+-Si11+, while single Si+ and Si2+ ions were evaporated from the nanometric silicon clusters when the ionizing laser fluence was sufficiently high. They also observed multiply charged nanoclusters. Moreover, they found that the size of silicon clusters ejected from the pulsed source played a key role in their velocity which enabled use of a mechanical chopper in the path of the cluster beam to select different portions of the products of each pulse, which allowed them to use quasi-size-selected clusters for subsequent experiments.768 Hofmeister et al. employed the same synthesis system and studied the lattice contraction in silicon NPs. They observed that spherical monocrystalline silicon NPs possessed almost no planar lattice defects. The synthesized silicon NPs were covered by thin amorphous oxide shells that resulted in lattice contraction. Lattice contraction decreased with decreasing particle size and oxide thickness. Accordingly, the lattice contractions could be transformed to lattice dilatation of NPs with very small size. HRTEM images and the SAED pattern of these silicon NPs are presented in Fig. 104.769 Ledoux et al. used the same synthesis process and studied the size-dependent photoluminescence characteristics of silicon nanocrystals. They observed that the photoluminescence of the nanocrystalline silicon was in close agreement with a quantum confinement model. They concluded that the intrinsic photoluminescence quantum yield of the nanocrystalline silicon could reach almost 100%. A simple model described the silicon nanocrystal photoluminescence shift with air aging of the samples due to the decreasing size of the nanocrystalline core via self-limiting surface oxidation. The oxide layer covering the core silicon nanocrystals affected the lattice parameter which in turn effected the width of the photoluminescence emission spectrum, along with its peak position. This study was also helpful in explaining an astrophysical phenomenon of extended red emission, which was one of the authors’ goals.770,772,773
 Fig. 104  Si NPs synthesized by Hofmeister et al. (a) HRTEM image of a monocrystalline silicon NP coated by an amorphous oxide layer. (b) SAED pattern. Adapted from ref. 769 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1999. 
Li et al. synthesized silicon NPs below 5 nm in diameter in macroscopic quantities (20–200 mg h−1) with intense visible photoluminescence after etching. They used silane as the main precursor with SF6 as a photosensitizer for more effective decomposition of silane. Hydrogen was used as the sheath gas to confine the reaction zone and was also added to the precursor stream to decrease the silane concentration, while a larger flow of He was used to purge the outer regions of the reactor and keep its windows free of particles. The laser power was 60 W and the pressure of the reaction chamber was typically 8 psia. Reducing the operating pressure below atmospheric pressure decreased the average particle size. They reported control of particle size, crystallinity, and production rate by changing the flow rates of hydrogen and helium to the reactor and by addition of SF6. They studied the effects of hydrogen flow rate in the central precursor flow and helium flow rate on the synthesis process and found that increasing either or both of these parameters decreased the average size of particles. Furthermore, they dispersed the silicon NPs in methanol then etched them with hydrofluoric acid (HF) to reduce the size of particles and passivate the silicon surface. Etching with HF produced particles with intense visible photoluminescence, while the as-synthesized NPs exhibited almost no photoluminescence.774
Cauchetier et al. synthesized Si/C/N nanocomposites by laser pyrolysis using ultrasonic spray delivery of aerosol droplets of hexamethyldisilazane ([(CH3)3Si]2NH) as a low-cost and effective precursor that could absorb the laser at 10.6 μm. They employed a commercially-available ultrasonic spray generator (Pyrosol model from RBI, France) as illustrated in Fig. 105. They used a mixture of argon and ammonia or pure argon as the carrier gas. For the thermal decomposition, they used a 600 W industrial CO2 laser which produced a beam with a diameter of 13 mm. They collected the NPs on a cylindrical stainless-steel filter that was preheated and placed in a glass chamber. The nanocomposites were amorphous with average NP diameters of 30 to 60 nm. In addition, they investigated the effect of various operating parameters on the synthesis process and found that the total gas flow rate played the main role in increasing the NP production rate.780
 Fig. 105  Schematic of the apparatus employed by Cauchetier et al. to synthesize Si/C/N nanocomposites. Adapted from ref. 780 with permission from John Wiley and Sons, Copyright 1994. 
Herlin-Boime's group also advanced the synthesis of silicon carbide NPs by laser pyrolysis. They used a mixture of silane (SiH4) and ethylene (C2H4) as the precursors. Laser power was 600 W and the pressure was 14.31 psia. The production rate was 3.8 g h−1 and the average size of the synthesized particles was 16.6 nm. Notably, they increased the silane to ethylene ratio in the precursor solution above the stoichiometric ratio to incorporate a slight excess of silicon in the final product to facilitate dispersion of the NPs in water by the generation of surface Si–OH groups. In addition, they broke up agglomerates through magnetic stirring and wet ball-milling of the aqueous NP dispersion followed by slip-casting of slurries to obtain green bodies. They performed densification by spark plasma sintering at a pressure of 70 MPa which produced final densities of up to 96% of the theoretical full density.784 This group also synthesized uniformly carbon-coated silicon nanomaterials from the same precursors using a novel two-stage laser pyrolysis approach. Fig. 106 presents a schematic of their reactor. Silane (SiH4) was introduced to the first reaction zone to synthesize crystalline silicon NPs. They could control the size of silicon nanocrystals by adjusting the flow rate of helium as the diluting gas and focusing the laser. The silicon nanocrystals were delivered to the second reaction zone where the decomposition of ethylene (C2H4), injected after the first reaction zone, produced a carbon shell around the silicon nanocrystals. Notably, they adjusted the laser power between the two reaction zones in a way that ensured crystallinity of the silicon produced in the first zone while the remaining laser power was adequate to drive decomposition of the ethylene in the second zone. They saw no evidence of formation of silicon carbide at the interface of the core and shell. In addition, the synthesis in a single-step continuous process instead of a multi-step batch synthesis reduced the presence of silicon oxide in the final products, which in turn made the nanomaterials promising for lithium-ion battery anode applications. The carbon-coated silicon nanocomposites provided a high and stable capacity because of the protective effect of the carbon shell, which prevented direct contact of the silicon core with the electrolyte.782 In another study, they controlled the experimental conditions to synthesize the amorphous Si@C nanocomposites using the same strategy. Because the silicon nanocrystals used as an anode material were amorphized during the first charge/discharge cycle, which negatively impacted stability, the initially amorphous carbon-coated silicon nanocomposites provided more stable capacity. Notably, they created the carbon shell with a thickness of 1 nm, which was sufficient to prevent oxidation of the amorphous silicon core which is more easily oxidized than crystalline silicon. In doing so, they improved the properties of the nanocomposites for lithium-ion battery applications.783
 Fig. 106  Schematic of the novel two-step laser pyrolysis reactor employed by Sourice et al. to synthesize Si@C nanocomposites. Reproduced from ref. 782 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2015. 
Hyperdoping, which means doping to concentrations above the nominal equilibrium solubility of the dopant, extends the range of electronic properties that can be obtained from a semiconductor. Rohani et al. employed the laser pyrolysis technique, which is capable of producing strongly non-equilibrium conditions where doping is governed by kinetics instead of thermodynamics, to synthesize spherical boron-hyperdoped silicon NPs with an average size around 25 nm. The precursor mixture was composed of silane (SiH4) and diborane (B2H6). The hyperdoping with boron did not alter the diamond cubic structure of silicon nanocrystals. The hyperdoped NPs exhibited tunable LSPR and were stable in air for long periods (minimum one year). Moreover, the synthesized NPs retained their structure without B segregation upon annealing at up to 600 °C.778
2.3.4.2. Boron-based nanomaterials. 
Boulanger et al. synthesized graphitic boron nitride NPs using laser pyrolysis. The precursor mixture included boron trichloride (BCl3) and ammonia (NH3) which was thermally decomposed by an industrial high-power CO2 laser. They investigated the morphology of the NPs with and without heat treatment at temperatures from 1000 to 1650 °C. The as-synthesized NPs were roughly spherical with concentric graphitic shells and sizes from 20 to above 100 nm. The smaller as-synthesized particles with average sizes of 20 to 50 nm were mainly nested polyhedra that consisted of about 40 concentric shells near the center, and were considered as filled-to-the-center. The larger particles with average sizes above 100 nm consisted of about 10 graphitic layers with large empty spaces in the structures and were considered hollow particles. After thermal treatment under a nitrogen atmosphere at 1100 °C, the same morphology for all particles, with a larger diameter above 200 nm, was observed. Further treatment under the same atmosphere at temperatures of 1400–1650 °C produced nanoplatelets with 10 to 50 graphitic layers and typical lateral dimensions of 50 nm, which were highly stable and remained unchanged for several months.763
Rohani et al. used laser pyrolysis to create boron NPs for use in hydrogen generation by water-splitting (ideally, 2 B + 3 H2O → B2O3 + 3 H2). Among water-reactive metals, boron has the highest theoretical specific capacity for hydrogen generation by reaction with water. To achieve complete reaction of boron, they needed highly-active boron NPs with high purity, small size, and high specific surface area, which can be produced by laser pyrolysis. They used diborane (B2H6) as the precursor and SF6 as the photosensitizer to generate boron NPs. The NPs were oxide free, amorphous, highly pure, air stable, and formed stable dispersion in water and alcohols. Because boron hydrolysis is not a spontaneous reaction, they needed an activator to launch the reaction. Among various possibilities tested, NaH was found to produce the greatest amount of hydrogen. The hydrogen generated by boron hydrolysis was used in a fuel cell, where its performance was very close to that of pure hydrogen from a compressed gas cylinder. Although in this case the total H2 generation did not reach the theoretical stoichiometric limit, it exceeded the theoretical limit for competing materials including silicon and aluminum, and thus shows great promise. These demonstrations also show that no reaction byproducts were generated that would affect the performance of fuel cells.762
2.3.4.3. Carbon-based nanomaterials. 
Herlin et al. employed laser pyrolysis to generate carbon-based nanomaterials from hydrocarbon precursors such as ethylene (C2H4) or butyne (C4H6). They found that by changing the reaction temperature they could synthesize nanomaterials with various characteristics. Amorphous carbon-based materials were produced at temperatures below ∼1200 K. The IR spectra of these materials had a broad continuum absorbance with stronger peaks associated with CC and CH aromatic groups. However, synthesis at higher temperatures resulted concentric microtexture nanomaterials which exhibited only a continuum with negligible features in the IR spectra. They suggested that the hydrogen content played a central role in appearance of these peaks in the more amorphous nanomaterials. They went on to conclude that IR laser pyrolysis synthesis of carbon-based nanomaterials followed a similar mechanism as the generation of carbon dusts in the envelopes of evolved carbon-rich stars.765 This group also investigated the synthesis of highly-aromatic carbon NPs by laser pyrolysis of hydrocarbons as a function of laser power and found that increasing the temperature of the reaction zone by increasing the laser power led to evolution of more turbostratic concentric carbon NPs from poorly organized ones.766
2.3.4.4. Magnetic nanomaterials. 
Bi et al. employed laser pyrolysis to generate iron-containing NPs including α-Fe, Fe3C, and Fe7C3. A schematic of their reactor system is shown in Fig. 107. The iron precursor was iron carbonyl (Fe(CO)5) delivered from a bubbler by a flow of ethylene. Because the ethylene can absorb the laser at 10.6 μm, it served as both photosensitizer and carrier gas and, in the case of carbide particles, as the carbon source as well. They controlled the operating parameters to synthesize NPs with different chemical compositions. Increasing the laser power from 30 to 50 W and the chamber pressure from 1.93 to 5.80 psia while decreasing the nozzle diameter from 1.7 to 0.8 mm resulted in Fe3C rather than α-Fe. Further increasing the laser power from 50 to 54 W, the chamber pressure from 5.80 psi to 9.67 psi, and the flow rate of ethylene from 9 to 25 sccm while decreasing the width of the beam from 1 mm to 0.2 mm produced Fe7C3 instead of Fe3C. The NPs were collected in a Pyrex trap with a stack of ferrite magnets that drew the ferromagnetic particles away from a Teflon membrane filter. Notably, they performed in situ passivation by flowing 4 to 10% oxygen in helium through the collection trap, which avoided spontaneous combustion of the NPs (α-Fe in particular) upon exposure to air. In addition, they placed a thermocouple inside the collection trap to monitor the process and prevent runaway oxidation. The average size of the NPs was in the range of 5 to 30 nm. The production rate for Fe3C7 NPs was about 3 g h−1, illustrating the versatility of the technique for relatively high-rate synthesis of NPs. Flowing excess ethylene led to carbon coating of the Fe3C and Fe7C3 NPs. Moreover, they investigated the magnetic properties of the NPs and found that they were ferromagnetic, rather than superparamagnetic, despite their small size.798
 Fig. 107  Schematic of the system employed by Bi et al. to synthesize Fe-containing NPs. Adapted from ref. 798 with permission from Springer Nature, Copyright 1993. 
Veintemillas-Verdaguer et al. synthesized crystalline γ-Fe2O3 NPs with a high degree of purity and a low degree of aggregation with an average particle size of ∼5 nm. The precursor was 30% iron pentacarbonyl (Fe(CO)5) in isopropanol (C3H8O). They nebulized the precursor solution and delivered aerosol droplets to the reaction zone where a 100 W continuous wave CO2 laser beam with a diameter of 4 mm induced decomposition and particle formation. The production rate was in the range of 33 to 96 mg h−1. The product yield, defined as the amount of the NPs synthesized relative to the iron pentacarbonyl delivered, ranged from 12 to 84%. Moreover, they investigated the magnetic properties of the NPs and observed superparamagnetism at ambient temperature with a saturation magnetization of 30.5 emu g−1. The size of the NPs played a substantial role in their magnetic characteristics, especially for particles below 10 nm in diameter. For such NPs, enhanced coercivity could arise from extra anisotropy in addition to magentocrystalline anisotropy due to the high surface to volume ratio.810 This group used the colloidal suspensions of iron oxide NPs as contrast agents in MRI after coating them with dextran (C8H32O16) in an aqueous sodium citrate (Na3C6H5O7) solution. The magnetic properties and 1H NMR relaxation times of the suspensions demonstrated their potential as contrast agents for MRI. They found that the greatest saturation magnetization was observed for the largest particles with the largest crystallite size, although all cases exhibited superparamagnetic behavior at room temperature.811 In another study, this group used metallic iron NPs synthesized by laser pyrolysis in addition to the iron oxide NPs as contrast agents for MRI applications after formulating them as biocompatible ferrofluids. For laser pyrolysis synthesis of iron oxide NPs, they flowed air together with argon to clean the ZnSe windows, while they performed slight oxidation by flowing nitrogen with 1 ppm of oxygen in the case of metallic iron NPs, which led to the synthesis of stable metal core particles with high crystallinity, covered by a thin oxide layer. Accordingly, they produced a stable ferrofluid of the sub-10 nm metal particles in aggregates of about 50 nm. The metallic iron NPs provided greater saturation magnetization and susceptibility than the iron oxide-based suspensions. In addition to increasing crystal size, using metallic iron NPs instead of iron oxide was a key factor in increasing the relaxivity value, the key parameter for MRI contrast.799 In addition, they compared the iron NPs covered by a thin layer of iron oxide with commercially available iron oxide in MRI applications in small animals and found that the laser pyrolysis synthesized samples produced a “negative” contrast enhancement of 60% in the liver compared to the commercially available iron oxide contrast agent, demonstrating their advantages as a contrast agent for liver and spleen MRI.800 They also investigated the mechanism of dextran adsorption on the surface of Fe2O3 NPs synthesized by laser pyrolysis and compared the strength of bonding in the case of laser pyrolysis synthesis with coprecipitation synthesis. They found that the favored mechanism is the collective hydrogen bonding between the iron oxide surface and dextran hydroxyl groups. They observed a strong bond between the iron oxide NPs and dextran in the case of heating dry samples of the suspensions of laser-pyrolysis-synthesized samples, while such a bond was not observed in the samples produced by a solution-phase coprecipitation approach. This difference led to better dispersion of the laser pyrolysis derived samples. Accordingly, they suggested that the coating of dextran on the surface of iron NPs is more effective for samples synthesized at higher temperatures and shorter times, which resulted in permanent dextran linkage to the particles.812 Furthermore, this group performed acid treatment with nitric acid (HNO3) on iron oxide NPs with sizes below 5 nm synthesized by laser pyrolysis to increase the saturation magnetization by 40% and enhance the initial susceptibility. Additionally, the optimized acid treatment led to a decrease in the aggregate size, increase in surface charge density, and improved colloidal stability.815
A laser pyrolysis synthesis of nickel NPs was presented by Swihart and coworkers. They used nickel carbonyl (Ni(CO)4) as the main precursor. Although nickel carbonyl has a high vapor pressure and its decomposition will lead to the synthesis of pure nickel NPs, it is very toxic which limits its safe use as a gaseous precursor. To circumvent this issue, they used in situ generation of nickel carbonyl by flowing CO through hydrogen-regenerated nickel powder in a tube furnace. Fig. 108 presents a schematic of their experimental set-up. A helium gas stream was used as a sheath gas to confine the reaction zone. Because the nickel carbonyl cannot absorb the laser at 10.6 μm, a photosensitizer is necessary. Very high specific surface area NPs with a mean diameter of less than 10 nm are prone to oxidization upon exposure to air. Hence, larger particles were targeted to study the effects of parameters on the size and morphology. However, because smaller NPs with the size less than 10 nm have the most interesting magnetic behavior, they also synthesized smaller particles. The NPs were loosely agglomerated and dispersible in nonpolar solvents, but did not form long-term stable colloidal dispersions. They studied the effect of CO flow rate, inert gas, photosensitizer, and precursor concentration on the synthesis process. Additionally, they studied the magnetic properties of nickel NPs. They reported the flow rate of carbon monoxide changes the nickel carbonyl concentration, delivery rate, and flow patterns in the reactor. The choice of inert gas affects the reaction time and appearance of a visible flame in the reaction zone. They used both SF6 and C2H4 as photosensitizers and found that although a high flow rate of SF6 led to high reaction temperature, it caused fluoride and sulfur contamination in the product. Use of C2H4 as the photosensitizer led to a change of morphology and crystal structure and eliminated sulfur and fluorine contamination. Finally, they found that laser pyrolysis synthesized nickel NPs with a size below 8 nm exhibit superparamagnetism.816
 Fig. 108  Schematic of the reactor system used by He et al. to synthesize Ni NPs. Reproduced from ref. 816 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2005. 
2.3.4.5. Titanium-based nanomaterials. 
Simon et al. synthesized nitrogen-doped titanium monoxide in a single-step laser pyrolysis process. They used liquid titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) as the precursor, which was atomized by a Pyrosol system and delivered to the reaction zone by nitrogen carrier gas, which had a much lower thermal diffusivity and led to better confinement of the reactants compared to helium. They supplied a low flow of ammonia (NH3) just before the outlet of the injecting nozzle to serve as both photosensitizer and nitrogen source. They increased the residence time in the reaction zone by reducing the total flow rates of gaseous streams, to increase decomposition of the reactants and generate reducing species from ammonia decomposition to incorporate nitrogen atoms as a dopant in the NPs. The laser power was 1 kW and a production rate of 1.44 g h−1 was obtained from 6.35 g h−1 of the titanium precursor. The product particles had the rock-salt crystal structure and an average size of 7 to 9 nm. Doping with nitrogen led to enhanced photocatalytic activity by reducing the optical band gap into the visible range of the solar spectrum. Notably, the as-synthesized nanopowder contained carbon, which was obvious from its black color, but it could be removed by mild heat treatment in air. Such heat treatment also partially or fully converted the rock-salt structure to the anatase phase by oxidation to a stoichiometry closer to TiO2.789
Herlin-Boime's group synthesized TiO2/MWCNT nanocomposites by in situ and ex situ processes based on laser pyrolysis. For the in situ synthesis, they mixed titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4) with MWCNT dispersed in toluene (C7H8) to make a precursor solution for a single-step laser pyrolysis synthesis by “on-flight” growth of titania NPs on the surface of MWCNT. In separate experiments they used titanium tetraisopropoxide as the precursor to synthesize titania NPs by laser pyrolysis and mixed the product powder with MWCNT to prepare ex situ formed TiO2/MWCNT nanocomposites. As evident in Fig. 109, in the case of in situ synthesis, the coating of titania on the MWCNT was highly dense so that there was almost no bare MWCNT surface, while ex situ mixing produced only partial coating of titania on the surface of MWCNT. The in situ synthesis by laser pyrolysis improved the electronic interactions between the two components of the nanocomposites. Moreover, the MWCNT dispersion in the titania NPs was significantly enhanced in the in situ synthesis. The titania, in situ TiO2/MWCNT, and ex situ TiO2/MWCNT had average diameters of 12, 13, and 12 nm and surface areas of 122, 166, and 120 m2 g−1, respectively. They employed the laser pyrolysis synthesized nanomaterials for solar cell applications and found that using in situ synthesized TiO2/MWCNT nanocomposites as the active layer in porous photoelectrodes led to a 20% improvement in the efficiency of solid-state dye-sensitized solar cells because of a higher short-circuit current density compared to the result using ex situ synthesized ones. Reduced charge recombination and the intimate electronic interaction between the NPs and nanotubes enhanced the charge extraction to the photoelectrode.792
 Fig. 109  TEM images of TiO2/MWCNT nanocomposites synthesized by Wang et al. through laser pyrolysis by: (a) in situ process and (b) ex situ processes. Adapted from ref. 792 with permission from American Chemical Society, Copyright 2015. 
Herlin-Boime's group synthesized pure titania, nitrogen-doped titania, gold-doped titania, and gold-modified nitrogen-doped titania NPs following the laser pyrolysis synthesis procedures they developed for the other titania-containing NPs and investigated their photocatalytic activity. They used titanium tetraisopropoxide (Ti[OCH(CH3)2]4), hydrogen tetrachloroaurate trihydrate (HAuCl4·3H2O), and ammonia (NH3) as the precursors. They monitored the destruction of formic acid (HCOOH) under UV and visible light irradiation to test the photocatalytic activity of the various laser pyrolysis synthesized NPs and compared them with one another and with commercially available titania. They reported that all the laser pyrolysis synthesized samples showed enhanced photocatalytic activity compared to commercially available titania under UV irradiation. In addition, the laser pyrolysis synthesized samples with even very low content of gold performed better than pure titania in the degradation of formic acid under UV light. Finally, they observed that the gold-modified nitrogen-doped titania samples exhibited superior photocatalytic activity under visible light compared to the nitrogen-doped titania samples.790
Scarisoreanu et al. employed a single-step laser pyrolysis technique to produce TiO2/SnO2 nanocomposites. They used two bubblers to separately deliver titanium tetrachloride (TiCl4) and tin tetrachloride (SnCl4) as precursors and carried them into the reaction zone in a mixture of argon and air to facilitate their oxidization. Because none of these streams could absorb the energy of the CO2 laser at 10.6 μm, they introduced ethylene (C2H4) as a photosensitizer. They produced nanocomposites with various tin concentrations from 1.1 to 4.8 at%. The nanocomposites contained both anatase and rutile titania, but anatase was the major component. The average crystallite sizes were in the range of 8 to 22 nm. They investigated the photocatalytic activity of the nanocomposites for degradation of methyl orange under UV irradiation and found that the nanocomposites with tin atomic concentrations in the range of 1.8 to 4.8% exhibited greater photoactivity compared to commercially available titania powder, and all the nanocomposites possessed a lower band gap energy.797
2.3.4.6. Zinc-based nanomaterials. 
In recent work, we have developed a low-cost ultrasonic atomizer that enabled us to spray any stable precursor solution into the reaction zone for the synthesis of nanomaterials with various compositions. A schematic of the ultrasonic atomizer is presented in Fig. 110a. We synthesized an array of zinc containing NPs including zinc oxide, pristine zinc sulfide, nitrogen-doped zinc sulfide, and zinc fluoride. To synthesize zinc oxide, zinc acetate dihydrate (Zn(CH3COO)2·2H2O) was dissolved in deionized water as the precursor solution. Because this precursor cannot absorb the energy of the laser, we introduced SF6 as a photosensitizer. The uniform zinc oxide NPs had the wurtzite crystalline structure and sizes below 10 nm. Increasing the flow rate of SF6 and the laser power led to production of zinc fluoride NPs using the same precursor solution. Further tuning of the operating parameters led to formation of zinc fluoride NPs with two different morphologies: spherical and rod-like. To synthesize NPs with rod-like shape, we increased the laser power and the SF6 flow rate compared to the conditions used to produce spherical NPs. In addition, the sheath gas used for the former case was hydrogen instead of nitrogen, which led to more rapid quenching because of its higher thermal conductivity. To synthesize pristine zinc sulfide, we added thioacetamide (CH3CSNH2) to the aqueous solution of zinc acetate dihydrate and sprayed it into the reaction zone, which led to production of zinc sulfide NPs with sphalerite crystalline structure and sizes below 10 nm, with a relatively narrow size distribution compared to the other vapor-phase synthesized nanomaterials. Adding thiourea (NH2CSNH2) instead of thioacetamide to the zinc acetate dihydrate aqueous solution resulted the formation of nitrogen-doped zinc sulfide NPs which had a narrower band gap compared to the pristine zinc sulfide, potentially extending their photocatalytic activity under visible light irradiation. By adjusting the operating parameters, we were able to synthesize nitrogen-doped zinc sulfide with different combinations of crystalline structures: sphalerite- and wurtzite-rich. Because the synthesis of the wurtzite crystalline structure requires higher temperature in the reaction zone compared to the synthesis of sphalerite, we increased the flow rate of SF6 and the laser power for this purpose. The relatively low vapor pressure of thiourea led to the formation of microspheres by a droplet-to-particle conversion mechanism rather than by gas-to-particle conversion as observed for the other materials. The morphologies of the various synthesized zinc-containing NPs are presented in Fig. 110b–g. All in all, the newly-developed low-cost ultrasonic atomizer should extend the utility of the laser pyrolysis by expanding the array of precursors that can be used with low-power, widely available CO2 laser sources.818
 Fig. 110  (a) Schematic of the ultrasonic atomizer employed by Malekzadeh et al. to synthesize Zn-containing nanomaterials by laser pyrolysis: (1) metal mesh disc with piezoelectric disk, (2) electronic driver circuit for piezoelectric element, (3) precursor solution injection port, (4) return line to pump, (5) precursor supply line from pump, (6) carrier gas inlet, and (7) aerosol droplets of precursor solution outlet to the reaction zone. TEM images of (b) pristine ZnS, (c) sphalerite-rich N-doped ZnS, (d) wurtzite-rich N-doped ZnS, (e) ZnO, (f) rod-like ZnF2, and (g) spherical ZnF2. Insets present the corresponding SAED patterns. Adapted from ref. 818 with permission from Elsevier, Copyright 2020. 
2.3.4.7. Instrumentation and modelling. 
Kruis et al. developed a simple model to describe the evolution of particle size, morphology, and number concentration during simultaneous sintering and coagulation in an aerosol flow reactor that neglected the polydispersity of primary and aggregated NPs. They considered the effect of irregular/fractal structure on the collision kernel from the free molecular to the continuum regime. This simple model was in good agreement with a computationally-demanding two-dimensional sectional model for the aggregates with respect to both the specific surface area and the primary particle volume. They applied the model to the synthesis of silicon from silane (SiH4) in a laser pyrolysis reactor. They found that grain boundary diffusion was the main coalescence mechanism for the silicon NPs, based upon their theoretical evaluation of different sintering mechanisms. Moreover, the model showed that growth of primary particles through sintering was possible at temperatures well below the melting point. The results from the model were in reasonable agreement with experimental data for the primary particle sizes, suggesting that the sintering process was accurately represented. Notably, they accounted for the production of smaller and more aggregated silicon NPs produced by the laser pyrolysis based on the assumption that the outer boundaries of the laser-heated region were cooled and diluted by the surrounding unheated sheath gas.831
Bomatí-Miguel et al. employed a simple coagulation model to simulate the synthesis of NPs by laser pyrolysis. The model had only the coagulation time as a tunable parameter. They considered the laser pyrolysis synthesis of iron NPs as a case study, for which they collected substantial experimental data. They applied the model to find operating conditions that maximized productivity as function of particle size. They found that the pressure and the carrier gas flow were the main factors determining productivity for larger NPs with mean diameter above 20 nm, while the vapor pressure of the precursor (temperature of delivery system) played the main role in determining productivity for smaller NPs with diameters below 20 nm.832
Mwakikunga derived two equations to describe their experimental observations for the flame temperature trends based upon laser power and laser wavelength in laser pyrolysis synthesis using spray-based precursor delivery. They considered the thermal decomposition of tungsten ethoxide (C12H30O6W) and vanadium ethoxide (C6H15O4V) aerosols through CO2 laser pyrolysis as case studies. The first equation, to find the flame temperature trend as a function of laser power, was extended from the original Haggerty–Cannon energy balance equation.833 The Haggerty–Cannon equation equates the energy absorbed from the laser beam to the total energies used to raise the aerosol temperature and initiate the chemical reactions plus energy losses through radiation, convection, and conduction. Based upon this equation, they concluded that the conversion of the precursors into product nanostructures was driven by a small fraction of the overall laser power, which was in a good agreement with experimental data. Moreover, they attempted to precisely identify the formation enthalpies and heat capacities through both modelling and experiment results, obtaining surprisingly low values of the heat capacities of the droplets, with ethanol as the solvent. This paper raises interesting questions about assumptions of thermal equilibrium during the laser pyrolysis process that merit further consideration.834
Dang and Swihart developed a fully-coupled two-dimensional model to investigate the laser pyrolysis synthesis of silicon NPs from silane. The model included laser heating, fluid dynamics, gas phase and surface phase chemical reactions, and aerosol dynamics (NP transport and evolution through convection, diffusion, nucleation, surface growth, thermophoresis, and coagulation processes). They simulated the aerosol dynamics using a moment method, assuming a lognormal particle size distribution, and using a sectional model and compared the results from these two methods. They found that although only the sectional model was able to predict the local bimodal size distribution occurring at some points within the reactor, the moment method was able to predict the final product characteristics as well as the sectional method at much lower computational cost. They also performed a parametric study to find the influence of various operating parameters such as laser intensity, gas phase composition, inlet gas velocity, and the concentration of precursor on the final product characteristics. They observed that increasing the temperature of the reaction zone resulted in an increase in the size of the NPs as well as silane conversion. Also, increasing the inlet gas velocity led to a decrease in the size of NPs and silane conversion due to the shorter residence time in the hot zone of the reactor. Moreover, increasing the concentration of hydrogen reduced both gas phase and surface reaction rates. Accordingly, it reduced the rate of particles formation and thus had a similar effect to an increase in the gas inlet velocity. Furthermore, they investigated the effects of the interconnected mechanisms on the synthesis of NPs. They reported that variation in the reaction conditions for different pathways through the three-dimensional reactor domain played the main role in determining size polydispersity and creating non-lognormal overall size distributions.835 In another study, they considered three sub-models to develop the simulation method: (i) They modeled the chemically reacting flow inside the reactor spatially with full geometric detail, without the consideration of aerosol dynamics, and with highly-simplified chemistry. (ii) Then, they employed an axisymmetric two-dimensional CFD model to describe the reaction zone, in which the results from the first step were used to find the boundary conditions. (iii) Finally, the synthesis of silicon NPs was investigated through a two-dimensional aerosol dynamics model by the consideration of more complete silane decomposition chemistry, along with the velocities and temperatures obtained from the second step. They employed a bivariate model to simulate the particle size evolution and morphology, and a moment model, based upon a lognormal particle volume distribution. Moreover, CFD simulation to incorporate the fluid dynamics data into the aerosol model made it appropriate for applications with complex reactor geometries. The model predicted that spherical NPs were generated at high temperature at the center of the reaction zone, followed by aggregation downstream of the reaction zone to generate non-spherical larger aggregates. In addition, they reported that thermophoresis plays a crucial role in the laser pyrolysis synthesis process because the small volume heated at the intersection of the laser beam and photosensitizer inlet stream resulted in steep temperature gradients.836 While most of these dynamics are similar to those observed in flame synthesis, the laser pyrolysis process combines some aspects of both a premixed flame, with energy deposition across the radius of the hot zone with aspects of a diffusion flame, with steep radial temperature gradients, and thus has some unique features.

3. Conclusions
In this review, we classified vapor-phase nanosynthesis methods into three categories based on the chemical similarity between the initial precursors and final products: (a) physically-based vapor-phase nanosynthesis methods in which the chemical structure of the initial precursors and final products are similar, including inert gas condensation, spark discharge generation, and pulsed laser ablation. These methods use direct heating, electrical discharges, intense laser light, or other methods to vaporize the precursor and then nucleate NPs of the same material or a simple compound of it. Generally, synthesis of highly-pure nanomaterials is the main benefit of these approaches and the limitations on the batch size or production rate per unit time are the major shortcomings of these approaches; (b) plasma processing nanosynthesis methods which can use precursors that are chemically similar to the final product, or molecular precursors that are quite different from the final product. Within this class, we distinguish between cases in which the precursors are injected into or downstream of a high-temperature plasma with thermal equilibrium between the electrons and ionized gas, and those using non-thermal plasmas in which the electrons have much higher temperatures than the ionized gas. In general, rapid heating and cooling rate in plasma is the main advantage of these approaches, while the need for complex equipment is the major barrier to broader use of these methods; (c) chemically-based vapor-phase nanosynthesis methods in which the initial precursors possess a different chemical structure compared to the final products include chemical vapor condensation, spray pyrolysis, flame-based aerosol synthesis, and laser pyrolysis. In each of these methods, a vapor or liquid droplets containing molecular (usually metalorganic or metal salt) precursors are decomposed following energy input, from furnace walls, a flame, or a laser. High production rate is a key advantage of many of these methods, while complex mechanisms and difficulty in achieving a high degree of purity can be limitations in these processes. In addition to the brief introduction of each method and the summarization of interesting nanomaterials produced using them, we described in detail some of the highest-impact studies that have been published about these methods, highlighting the corresponding applications of the nanomaterials produced.
Overall, although solution-phase methods of nanomaterial synthesis, including hydrothermal, solvothermal, sol–gel, and related processes play a dominant role in the scientific literature on nanomaterial synthesis, vapor-phase routes can provide a very fruitful alternative that can dramatically reduce the use of solvents and generation of waste, and that can be conducted in a steady-state continuous operation mode that is typical of industrial chemical processing. The present review aims to demonstrate the wide range of possibilities and of materials that can be prepared by these vapor-phase routes. While it is extensive, it is by no means comprehensive, and many more worthwhile studies have been published. For each synthesis route, and particularly for the more complex ones including plasma processes, flame processes, and laser-driven processes, there is much room remaining for both advances in fundamental understanding that will enable predictive modelling and scale-up, and for creative application to new materials and, especially, material combinations produced in a single-step continuous process.
Despite the decades of development described in this review, many opportunities for further advances in vapor-phase synthesis remain. Rapid advances in computer science and simulation methods, along with increasing computational power are improving our ability predict the formation of NPs in vapor-phase techniques using advanced modelling techniques. These advances move the field ever closer to the elusive ability to fully model industrial-scale processes that couple turbulent reactive flow and steep temperature and concentration gradients with NP nucleation, growth, aggregation, and coalescence, linking processes over many orders of magnitude in length and time scales. At the same time, new experimental developments are expanding the ability of researchers and practitioners to adopt these vapor-phase synthesis approaches. For example, we recently combined the ultrasonic atomization technique with laser pyrolysis to demonstrate a low-cost, green, and single-step route to synthesize fluorine-containing compounds with interesting properties.818 This is only a representative example of how the combination of techniques, in this case spray pyrolysis (using aerosol delivery of one precursor), CVC (using vapor-phase delivery of another precursor), and laser pyrolysis (using laser heating) can provide routes to synthesize new materials with interesting properties for existing and emerging applications. Similarly, advances in preparation of multi-component nanomaterials, particularly in the realm of catalysis, provide abundant opportunities for developing both materials and processes.31,837 Multi-stage aerosol processes, like the two-stage laser pyrolysis method of Herlin-Boime and co-workers (Fig. 106)782 and the two-stage non-thermal plasma reactor of Mangolini and co-workers354 provide new opportunities to further tailor multicomponent materials in vapor-phase synthesis. Hard- and soft-templating strategies and ligand-capping strategies developed for solution-phase synthesis methods can be brought into the gas phase, particularly in spray pyrolysis and related methods that involve droplet-to-particle conversion. Numerous other combinations of synthesis techniques can similarly open up opportunities to produce new and interesting nanomaterials, and to economically scale up the production of these materials to achieve economical and societal impact.
List of definitions
Agglomeration: agglomeration is the process in which NPs collide and adhere to one another relatively weakly, forming “soft” aggregates that can be simply broken through mechanical forces.838,839
Aggregation: aggregation is the process in which NPs collide and adhere to one another strongly, via interfacial reaction or partial coalescence, to form larger and relatively dense aggregates.838 It depends on both particle transport mechanisms and interfacial chemical reactions.840
Annealing: annealing is a heat treatment process usually applied to reduce crystal defects and/or increase ductility and malleability.841 Annealing is usually done an inert atmosphere.
Calcination: calcination is a heat treatment often intended to increase the crystallinity of NPs and remove impurities on their surface.842 It is generally done in an oxidizing atmosphere.
Coalescence: coalescence is a process in which merging and mixing of particles leads to homogeneous NPs.843 Liquid droplets always coalesce upon collision, but solid NPs may also coalesce at temperatures below their melting point. Partial coalescence leads to the formation of hard aggregates.27
Coagulation: coagulation can be taken as synonymous with agglomeration as defined above.844 Coagulation leads to the formation of loose agglomerates.27
Droplet-to-particle conversion: droplet-to-particle conversion is the dominant mechanism for the synthesis of NPs when aerosol droplets of precursor solutions react to form product particles without evaporating.818 It leads to relatively large, spherical particles, which may be hollow or porous.
Fragmentation: fragmentation is the process of breaking of aggregates into smaller clusters.845
Gas-to-particle conversion: gas-to-particle conversion is the dominant mechanism for forming NPs from gaseous precursors or when aerosol droplets of precursor solutions first evaporate and then react in the gas phase.818
Nucleation: nucleation is a self-limiting process that relieves supersaturation (thermodynamic driving force for phase change) by spontaneous formation of new particles.846,847
Primary particles: the small quasi-spherical particles that initially form by gas-to-particle conversion or that make up aggregates.
Sintering: sintering, in the context of material processing, is the compacting of the particles to form a solid mass through heat and/or pressure without melting.848 However, in the context of NP synthesis, it is often used as a synonym for coalescence.
Surface growth: surface growth is an irreversible process in which particles grow by condensation or reaction of vapor-phase species on their surface.849 It tends to yield equal growth rates for particles of all sizes, and thus narrows the relative width of the particle size distribution.
Self-assembly: self-assembly is the process of spontaneous organization of nanoscale building blocks into ordered structures.850
Thermophoresis: thermophoresis is a phenomenon in which a macroscopic temperature gradient drives transport of aerosol particles from a higher to lower temperature region.851

Table Content:

Mohammad Malekzadeh
	Mohammad Malekzadeh is a PhD Candidate under the supervision of Professor Mark T. Swihart in the Chemical and Biological Engineering Department, University at Buffalo (SUNY) since 2016. He earned his Master's degree from Sharif University of Technology, Iran and his Bachelor's degree from Persian Gulf University, Iran. His research mainly focuses on the design and synthesis of nanomaterials using vapor-phase (aerosol) techniques.

Mark T. Swihart
	Mark Swihart is UB Distinguished Professor and Chair of Chemical and Biological Engineeirng and Empire Innovation Professor in the RENEW Institute at the University at Buffalo (SUNY). His research focuses on development and application of both aerosol (vapor-phase) and colloidal (solution-phase) methods of nanomaterial synthesis, and on diverse applications of nanomaterials in health, energy, and the environment.
